O, children, since I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, by its completion its end is achieved, and through its explanations and declarations, it seems to me that I have fulfilled it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the practical application of the art, that is, surgery.
For the art of surgery is now, in our region and time, almost entirely lost, so that the science of it is greatly endangered, and its knowledge is obscured; and of it only some brief descriptions remain in the books of the ancients, which, through the passage of time, have given rise to errors and uncertainties, until its understanding is clouded and its strength and art have diminished.
And it seems to me that I can revitalize it [the art of surgery] by means of this treatise, in accordance with the method of exposition, declaration, and summary; and that it may be understood through the descriptions of cautery instruments and other surgical tools, accompanied by an explanation and preparation of them, a necessary process.
However, the reason why a skilled craftsman with a skilled hand is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and its practitioner needs to be versed in the science of anatomy, which describes the parts of the body so that he knows the structure and form of the limbs, their connection and separation; and has knowledge of the bones, nerves and muscles, their numbers, and their distribution; and of the pulsating and stationary veins, and the places from which they arise.
And therefore Hippocrates says that a name is much, but the work is little, especially in the art of handcraft.
But we have already discussed this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is ignorant of what we have said about anatomy will inevitably fall into errors that will cause people to die, as I have seen many such instances, for some are trained in this science, but abandon it without knowledge and experience.
For I saw an unskilled and foolish physician open a scrofulous abscess on a woman's neck, and cut some arteries in the neck, causing a large amount of blood to flow until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician attempt to remove a stone from a man who had grown old, and the stone was large; he went to him and removed the stone along with a piece of the bladder, so that the man died on the third day.
And I already warned against doing that, and saw that due to the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I knew beforehand that he would die.
And I saw another physician, who received payment from a duke in our land for surgery, and went to a dark-skinned man whose leg was fractured; the physician, due to his ignorance, reduced the fracture, pressing against the wound with bandages and splints with excessive force, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he treated it according to his own desires, and bound it together, and left it for several days, and commanded that we loosen the ligature; and it remained like that until the thigh and foot became infected with an abscess, and led to ruin.
And I was called to him, and I proceeded to loosen the ligature, and achieved tranquility and alleviation of his pain.
However, corruption had already taken hold in the limb, so I could not stop it, and the corruption continued in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who opened a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated in a few days, until the evil of it was greatly aggravated by the patient's condition.
Because cancerous tumors are pure melancholy humor, therefore they should not be treated with a complete cauterization, unless the limb is deemed to be completely diseased.
Therefore, children, it is necessary for you that the surgical operation be divided into two parts: one being the operation associated with health, and the other, an operation that is perilous in the most extreme cases.
And I have already described this throughout this book, where there is operation that involves error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to guard against this and abandon it, so that the fools may not find a way to speak ill and disgrace you.
Therefore, take your tools with diligence and with God's protection, and treat your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best method, through God's grace, seeking health and a praiseworthy outcome, and abandon terrible illnesses with difficult cures, and remove from your practice anything that causes you fear, so that your dedication to your faith and your name may not fail you: for greater glory remains for you, and higher in the world and behind your holiness.
Gualia has already said in some of his teachings: Do not treat a serious illness, lest you be called bad doctors.
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter, ordered from head to foot, describes the forms of instruments and the implements of cauteries, and what is necessary for the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of incision and perforation, and of cupping, and of plasters, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, is entirely categorised and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and on the treatment of twisting and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and concerning the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary for me to describe the benefits of this cautery and its harms, and in what constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the discourse on the nature of the benefits of cautery and its harms is a long and verbose discourse, subtle in its science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and it suffices for me to briefly mention some of their discourses, fearing length.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all constitutions universally, whether with or without matter, except for two constitutions, which are hot constitutions without matter.
In the hot and dry constitution with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial in that case, and others say the opposite, that cautery is not suitable in an illness characterized by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is undesirable to treat a hot and dry illness with something that is also hot and dry.
And those who say the opposite claim that cautery provides benefit in every hot and dry illness that afflicts humans, for when you compare the body and its humours to the constitution of fire, you find the human body to be cold.
And I say, concerning this matter, that experience has shown this to me several times.
However, this should not be accepted without careful consideration by someone who is already experienced and skilled in the practice of cautery, who has understood the diversity of human constitutions, the various types of illnesses, their causes, the symptoms, and the appropriate timeframes for treatment.
But in other constitutions, you have no need for such caution, and especially in cold and moist illnesses, for all physicians have agreed on this, and are not in disagreement about the use of cautery in those cases.
And understand, children, that the secret of healing through cautery with fire, and its goodness, lies in the difference between cautery with fire and cautery with medicinal remedies.  This is because fire is simple; its action affects only the member being cauterized, and does not harm other members connected to it except with minor damage.
However, the action of medicinal cauterization extends to the affected member and the surrounding areas, and it can easily introduce difficult-to-cure illnesses and pain to the member itself.
But fire, due to its purity and beneficial nature, does not do this; it simply removes excess.  We already know this from experience and a long study of the art and the truth of its causes; therefore I excuse myself from further lengthy discourse.  And if it were not that this topic, concerning the secret of fire's application to the body, and its ability to expel illnesses through its specific action, is already covered in detail in my book,  I would demonstrate its operation with a philosophical and conclusive argument, to enlighten you better.
And understand, children, that they disagree on the best time for cautery, and consider springtime the most suitable.
And I say that cautery is appropriate at all times, because the harm caused by unfavorable timing is overshadowed by the benefit of what is drawn out by the cautery, especially if the cautery is necessary for sharp, sudden pains, which cannot tolerate delay, as the fear of greater harm from a prolonged illness outweighs any small harm from an immediate cautery.
And this is not something to consider, my children, it is only the opinion of the common people and foolish physicians, that is, that a disease cured by cautery does not always require a return to the original state, and that condition is considered to be sufficient.
And the reason is not as they think, for cautery is not a replacement for medicine, but rather an alteration of the body's constitution, drying out the moisture that causes the pain.
However, cautery is superior to medicine in terms of its power and efficacy and the greatness of its effect.
And it is possible for the illness to return at some point in time, depending on the patient's constitution, the location of the illness, and the patient's resilience, and the build-up of excess fluids within them, and what has been consumed in food and similar matters; but if the illness for which cautery is administered is a localized illness with limited excess fluids and moisture, like toothache and similar conditions, it is possible that the pain will not return there, but this happens in a milder form.
And it is a common saying that cautery is the last resort in medicine, and it is a truthful statement, not in the way they understand it, for they believe there is no cure that works as well as cautery.
And the truth is the opposite; the idea that cautery is the last resort in medicine is only true when we've tried other treatments for some illnesses, and those treatments did not work, and only then, at the end of the line, is cautery administered, and proves useful. Therefore, from this, it can be seen that cautery is the last resort in medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and many foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except because of the nobility and superior quality of gold's substance.
And they said that the place of cautery doesn't burn, which is absolutely not true, for I have already tested this and found that it only happens in some bodies and not others, and cautery with gold is good and better than with iron, as they said; however, when you do cautery with gold fire, it isn't clear to you, because it ignites according to the amount you want due to the gold's resilience, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you apply heat to it, the gold melts by the fire, and some of the gold falls into the fire, so that the craftsman is burned by working with it.
Therefore, cautery with iron is done more easily and more readily for the correct performance of the operation.
And I have organized this chapter on cautery according to sections, and I have ordered them from the head down to the feet, so that the seeker may find what he wants in it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization remedies moisture and coldness affecting the brain, the cause of which are twofold:  a cause of stiffness and a multitude of rheumatic flows, called "corissa" [likely a form of rhinitis], flowing from the head to the eyes and ears, a multitude of sleep disorders, toothaches, and throat ailments, and universally any illness brought on by coldness, such as paralysis, stroke, and similar ailments.
The method of this cauterization is this: first, you should order the patient to be purged with laxatives, cleansing the head, for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and usual constitution.
And then you instruct him to shave his head, and then make him sit between your hands on a smooth bench and have him place his hands on his feet.
And then place the tip of your palm, near the bridge, on the root of his nose between his eyes. However, when the tip of your middle finger has reached that spot, mark that spot with a probe, and then apply the olive cautery, of which this is the form:
And then place that [cautery] on the spot marked with the probe, so that it descends, with which your hand is gradually tightened, and you turn it.
And then gently raise your hand and look at the area, and if you see any significant amount of bone exposed, roughly the size of a grain of millet, then raise your hand, and if not, return your hand with that iron, with another one of those, if it is chilled, until the bone is visible as I have told you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the area and leave it for three days.
Then place a piece of cloth soaked in butter on it and leave it there until the scab from the burning is destroyed. And then treat the area with tetrafarmacon ointment until it is healed.
And if the wound remains open, it is better to move the arm, because this is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the bone should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until a part of the bone's surface is burnt.
And then scrape away what has adhered to the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that with the cautery, they should reach the back, to strongly press on the bone until it breaks, and this is the method of a small (minor) cauterization. And they believe that vapors from the head escape from that location.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then treated until it is healed.
However, I have not seen these two types of cauterization in every case, but only in some men according to the way of fear; and in my view, it is better, when possible, with the treatment, because the head is weakened by a disruption of continuity in it, just as we have attested in other limbs, and even more so when the patient's head is of weak constitution.
And the first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion; and using that method and procedure will be beneficial.
Second Chapter.
Of the cauterization of the head.
When there is chronic and long-lasting pain in the whole head of a prolonged illness, and the patient has used leeches, cupping, head purges, plasters, poultices, herbs, and similar treatments, and especially if they have already been cauterized with a cautery we have discussed and it hasn't been of any help, then consider that if the patient's head is of strong constitution, and it is not weak and has a great deal of cold, then cauterize with another cautery beneath the first one, a little below. And then cauterize that area on the top of their head with the cautery until the scab is broken. Cut the scab open and expose the bone to the extent we have described, and cauterize that area with a cautery on the back of the head in the place called the *vas*—and that is the spot where a cupping glass is placed. And ease your hand in doing this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience very great pain different from the pain of all other cauteries of the head.
And I would say this cautery is in its proper place, for the cautery used to cauterize the temples and the back of the head should be a more delicate cautery than the one used for the middle of the head; and this is the manner of it.
Third Chapter.
Of the cauterization of not-ancient (or, perhaps, non-chronic) migraine.
When pain is sudden or spreading within the head, the head should be treated with purifying medications, and the other cure, which I have described in the discussions of illnesses, should also be administered.  If this proves ineffective, then cauterization in that location can be done in two ways: either with a burning medicinal cautery, or with an iron cautery.
But a cautery with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and drain it and cauterize the ends of both of its heads. And then, you should make an incision at the location of the pain in the temple with a sharp lancet until a small area beneath the skin has been created and is hidden.
Then apply a bandage to compress it, and leave it for three hours.  Then remove it and the garlic, and allow the wound to heal for two or three days, and then apply a compress of wool soaked in broth until the area is healed.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines, which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But the cauterization with iron, according to this method: fire by cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called a "claus clavilhs," because its head is shaped like a nail, in which there is a slight curvature, and in its middle is a small elevation.
And then place it on the painful area, and hold your hand — so that the surrounding tissue is not burned — and turn the small iron slowly but with speed.
And if the amount of tissue that is burned by the cautery's heat is about half of it, then lift your hand so that the artery underneath is not burned, lest blood flow results.
Then soak some cotton in salt water and place it over the area, and leave it for three days; then place cotton moistened with butter, and then treat with ointment until healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the edge of a scalpel, like a cautery, and be careful not to cut an artery in this particular, not-ancient, method.
Fourth Chapter.
On the cauterization of ancient migraine.
When you treat migraine with what we have said about the previous treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment does not work, and sometimes because the disease does not respond to what we have said about the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire, then you must heat the knife-cautery until it is white-hot, and then apply it to the painful area along a line that is half the length of your finger, or even less; and press your hand once, and hold it until you cut through the flesh and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be mindful of the continuity of the jawbone, which is strained by chewing, and that the ligament and the nerve that control it are not injured and do not cause spasm.
And if it is about the care and caution of the flow of blood from the severed artery, for fear arises in its severance, especially for one who does not know what they are doing and is not skilled or experienced; and lets the operation be unfinished at the beginning.
And I will come back to the treatment of accidental blood flow from the artery according to its method, in its place within this book.
And if you see that this treatment does not cure this ailment and the patient's condition seems bearable, cauterize the middle of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it heals.
And if you wish, administer the cautery we described in the chapter on artery constriction with a cautery having two blades, and this is a better and more useful cauterization.
Chapter 5.
Of the cauterization of earaches.
When pain in the ear comes from cold and is treated with laxatives and the other treatment I mentioned in the division, and the pain does not go away, then cauterize the ear with a cautery, and this is the one called a "puncta," and this is its form:
And then puncture near the ignition, and let it move around the entire ear in a circular fashion, or in the circuit of each affected spot if the pain is in multiple places. Move the cautery along the ear's base slowly, after marking the location with a probe.
And if the cautery makes ten points on the entire ear, cauterize those areas, and then treat the spots until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the cauterization of tumors.
Tumors which are cured by cauterization are only of the types that arise from phlegm, as we have stated in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type that comes from drying out or spasms of the nerve.
When you treat that type of tumor with leeches, purges, and remedies, and your treatment does not prove effective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries: the first at the root of the ear, the second a small one below their temple, and the third at the juncture of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the diseased area, for softening occurs only on the side that appears healthy.
And the method of cauterization is to cauterize with the cautery on the right side of the ear's extremity, then slightly higher on the temple, and another on the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the fingertip, and hold your cautery until the amount of the affected area is about half consumed/burnt.
And this is the method of cauterization, and it is a type of surgery, the method of which is described above. However, it is more subtle than what you've seen; and one needs to cut away any superfluous fat/thickness slightly.
And then treat the area with what was previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the cauterization of old apoplexy.
When apoplexy is longstanding, and you've treated it with what we've discussed, and the patient shows no fever, then cauterize the area with four cauteries: one on each temple (corner of the head); one in the middle of the head, as described previously; and one on the nape of the neck (occiput). And the method of cauterization is done as previously stated.
And it has already been cauterized on the opening of the stomach; and further back; and subsequently be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
On cauterization for forgetfulness caused by a flame.
Large jars and pills should be given to the patient at the beginning, to purify the brain.
And then shave the patient's entire head and apply a plaster of cedar described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply it several times, for this is a method of cauterization. And do this according to the same order I have already mentioned.
And so, if this treatment is successful, well and good; but if not, then make three cauteries on the back of their head, descending from the highest part of the head to the lowest part of the neck. And place the thickness of a finger between each cautery, and then treat the cautery with what has been previously described.
But if you wish to add more, and the patient tolerates it, then make a cautery in the middle area; and if you want a greater addition, then cauterize the area above the two horns.
Then treat it until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped, according to the form previously given.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the entire body.
You should proceed with the cleansing of the head with herbs and what we have mentioned; then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with cauteries on all the horns of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if in the illness of the softening of the body, the patient is tolerant and the illness is severe, then cauterize it with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and continually with a cautery until it is softened from the backbone, and remove your hand. And then treat it until it is healed, and this should be an olive oil cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
Therefore, it is necessary to treat the brain at the outset with herbs and other treatments that we have described in the division, when the illness is severe and can tolerate the depletion of medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate the medicines, then administer remedies and masticatories that treat the brain, for several days beforehand, along with a good diet.
And then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, according to the previous method, and with another cautery on the back of their head, and cauterize on each side of the head. And if the illness is severe and can tolerate it, then cauterize it with the cauteries mentioned, having simultaneously applied paralysis and softening of the body to the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
Let the cautery be olive-shaped, as previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a smaller cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
On the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted moisture and thick phlegm, then cauterize that with the cauteries we have mentioned in the previous discussion on paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is an excess leaning towards melancholy, and the patient's body is moist, then give him to drink that which cleanses his brain according to what has been previously described in the division.
And then shave the patient's head, and then apply a properly made torquetum (a type of bandage or device) around the head, and then place it in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated upright, and held securely in all parts of the body.
And then apply a liniment; and it is better to use old sheep's wool. And warm that over the fire with a measured, moderate heat, which the said patient can tolerate when placed on him. And then place that in the middle of his/her head, within the bandage/torquetum, and leave it until it is cooled.
And do this with the patient once a week, along with the rest of their treatment until they are healed; or, if you wish, cauterize that area making small punctures according to his/her tolerance, holding the hand with a cautery specially designed for this; this is a type of cautery that moistens the brain evenly.
And then place that cloth soaked in wool or henbane.
Chapter 12.
On the cauterization of descending water in the ear.
When you have observed the initial symptoms of water in the ear, as described in the previous section, then begin by giving the patient drinks that will help clear their head, and keep them from excess moisture.
And make them sweat in a bath for a few days.
And then command that the patient shave their head, and cauterize the affected area in the middle of the head; and then cauterize with two cauteries on both temples if the onset of water is in both ears, or on one temple if the water is in only one ear.
Cauterize all the veins and arteries beneath the scalp; and let the cauteries be located as far apart as the temples.
And beware of blood flow during the cauterization; and if you see blood flow, stop it with any medicine possible.
And I will come with the knowledge that is in the extraction of arteries and the excision of them and the prevention of blood flow.
And sometimes the neck is cauterized below the two bones with two cauteries placed directly in contact.
Chapter 13.
Of cauterization of old sores.
When sores on the face are chronic and through the veins and arteries that are in the visible area of the head, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegm, then cauterize it with the cautery we have described in the chapter on applying hot water to sores, and with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries on the neck below the temples, and with two cauteries on the neck below the two bones. And if you need further treatment, then cauterize it along the entire length of the sore's edge, on the end of the eyebrow, with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal foul odor.
If your treatment using the methods described in the division is unsuccessful, then prepare and give the patient porridge to drink for three nights; afterwards, shave the patient's head, and cauterize it with a middle-sized cautery and an olive-oil cautery. And then cauterize it with a clove-shaped cautery, using two cauteries on both eyebrows below the hair, a small one for each.
And be careful of the sparks, lest you set it alight.
And this is the shape of the clove-shaped cautery.
Chapter 15.
Of cauterization for softening the eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened by illness or moisture, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery using this lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize a small area above the two eyebrows with two cauteries on all sides, and they should be extended away from the temples. And the length of each cautery should be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press too hard with your hand and the cautery, but according to the quantity needed for one-third of the eyelid to be adhered.
Chapter 16.
Concerning cauterization of the eyelid when the hairs are turned inwards and the eye is inflamed.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inwards, causing inflammation, cauterization in those cases is done in two ways: either with a burning iron or with a cauterizing medicine.
If with a cauterizing iron, tell the patient beforehand to remove their eyelashes, just as they would clean them, until they are elongated and spread out; and if those hairs are inflamed near their roots, then you should bind the eyes with a bandage so that they are not injured until the hairs have regrown.
Then, once they have grown back and spread out, place the patient's head on your lap, and carefully apply a cauterizing agent to the eyelid with a small tool, shaped like a leaf, starting near the hairs.
Then place cotton soaked in egg white or a pthilli (likely a type of medicinal preparation) over the eye.
And then heat the cautery iron, which has this specific shape.
And then cauterize in small, repeated passes, following the shape you marked, until the surface of the inflamed area, which resembles a myrtle leaf, has been completely burned.
A sign of successful treatment is that you see the eyelid contracted and the hairs raised towards the puncture wound on the eye.
And then elevate your hand and leave it like that for three days, and then place cotton with a bandage over it until the scab is gone. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if any cause returns to the place of origin and the eyelid is softened, then do the cauterization again, exactly as you did at first.
But if the hairs are under the eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural position and is smoothed, and the hairs do not irritate the eye.
But the cautery with a burning medicine, which you order the patient to leave the hairs until they are stretched and smoothed, and then a form of myrtle leaf is made of cloth.
And then take a known herb and a living animal's fat, or something like that, and mix those things well, and do this quickly so it doesn't get cold.  Then spread this mixture over the cloth shaped like a myrtle leaf, and place it over the one or both eyes.  Place a cloth soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head should be on a pillow.
And place the fingertip on the medicine and press it a little bit, and do not remove it until the patient feels the medicine's burning sensation, for the burning sensation should be like that of fire.
And then, as long as the patient feels the burning sensation, leave the medicine in place and hold it with your finger.  Once the burning sensation stops, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.  Then, consider whether the eyelid has been lifted to the correct position by the cauterization, either burning or incision.  If so, it is correct.  If not, apply more medicine to the area where it wasn't previously applied and repeat until the eyelid is smoothed out and the eye is healed.
Then apply a bandage to the area until the burning sensation is gone, and then treat it with a specific ointment or another remedy until it is healed.
You must, after your procedure, carefully watch that no medicine gets into the eye.  If, after a few days, the eyelid is swollen and some of the medicine is in the eye, then re-apply your medicine to that swollen part of the eyelid, as you did in the beginning.  And continue to do so until it is healed.
And this is the method of treating a wound with myrtle leaves.
Understand that human wounds vary in size and severity, and therefore your treatment is adjusted accordingly.
And the path to recovery for the one studying this art will not be hidden.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of fistulas in the tear duct of a wound.
When you treat a fistula using the methods discussed in the divisions of illnesses, and your treatment doesn't improve it, then you should cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place their head on your lap, and have a servant hold their head between your hands, so that it's not jostled or moved by their head movements.
And then apply a piece of lint soaked in egg white or a poultice of plantain to the wound.
And then apply the cautery, which is shaped like this:
which should be concave, shaped like a reed or a hawk's feather, at the end where the cautery is made.
And if desired, let it penetrate into the other end. And if you want, let it be shaped like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
And then press on the fistula if it is open, and drain and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and remove the pus from it.
And then place the heated cautery on it, and hold your hand a little above the cautery near the opening of the nose, so that neither your hand nor the patient is disturbed. Then place the cautery on the edge of the opening and burn it away.
And so, if your cautery penetrates the bone at the beginning, good.  But if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice if necessary.  Leave it like this for three days, and then place a bandage over it with a poultice and surround it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if more than ten days have passed and it is not healed, then apply a sharp corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and smooth as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another way of cauterizing a fistula, which some earlier practitioners said:  Open the site of the fistula and then place in that fissure a thin, fine lead of which the form is thus:  Pour in that fissure about one inch of molten lead; hold your hand over it with a good grip, so that the patient is not moved lest the molten lead should reach the opening.  It is necessary to place over the patient's opening a bandage soaked in egg white or water, because the lead will adhere to the site of the fistula and heal it with astonishing speed.
And if the fistula is healed by what we have described about cauterization and treatment, it is well.  But if not, then it is necessary to administer a nasal incision and reduce the fistula to its proper place within the nasal cavity.
Chapter 18.

Chapter 21.

And repeat this several times until the heat's effect reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip wool or cloth in the aforementioned hoof, and place it over the aching tooth. Then place heated iron on top of that until the iron reaches the cavity of the tooth.
However, cauterization with fire is done by using a heated iron or bronze instrument, and it should be of a certain thickness so the heat doesn't reach the patient's mouth.
And then heat the cautery, shape it as needed, and place it on the tooth, holding it until it cools; repeat this a few times until the pain is gone, either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, by pressure of this cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good broth and hold it for an hour, then spit it out; and this is the method of cauterization.

Of the cauterization of the spleen.

And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery using three incisions.
And then you treat the cautery, as long as it has stopped emitting pus after many days, for this is more important than the other preceding things in the cure.

And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for 40 days until the area and the wound are sealed.
And you must regulate the patient's diet according to the duration of their treatment, which will support their abdominal recovery and prevent the intestines from pushing out during the healing process.
And when approximately 40 days have passed, then administer a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another 40 days.
And abstain from strenuous work, overeating, and excessive drinking, and loud voices, for if they follow this treatment regimen, they will be completely healed.
And I will now describe the treatment for ruptures in other parts of the abdomen, and if it begins and you want to prevent it from recurring, cauterize the rupture with a round cautery, commensurate with its size, and the area should be dried as much as the third part of the whole is. And then, treat it as previously described, and it is well-suited.
And some earlier practitioners would say that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, following this shape, since it is marked with a probe:
and a line is drawn across the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it;  and place your hand with a wedge-shaped cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
Of the cauterization of twisting (or torsion).
When pain persists in some limb due to a blow or impact, and that pain persists for a long time and you don't find a cure with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be for each limb according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the twist and the pain in it.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cautery, good; and if not, then repeat the cautery on it; for it is customary for those pains to be punctuated in the limb closest to the affected area, so it is necessary to continue cauterizing until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Of the cauterization of leprosy.
Some lepers are greatly helped by cautery, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And therefore, when you wish to cauterize that person, consider if the leprosy is recent and can be cured by what we have said in the division and not otherwise; and if you fear that the corruption encompasses the entire body and constitution of the patient, then cauterize the affected area on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and a second lower down, between the forehead and the hairline; and two more on the temples; and one at the back of the neck or nape of the neck.
And finally, press the cautery slightly into the bone to create a small indentation, so that the expulsion of thick vapors is relieved; and cauterize on the spleen as necessary.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the patient and is evident, then you should cauterize with the cauteries previously mentioned for the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and two cauteries on both cheeks, and two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and one large one on the vertebrae near the kidneys, and one on the kidneys themselves, and two on the ribs, one on each rib, and two on the elbows, and one on each elbow, and two on the thighs, and two on the muscles, and two on the tendons of each foot; cauterize the affected areas on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of the feet, and on the hollows of the hands.
And be careful of the nerves that are near the heels, so that you do not burn them.
And sometimes a cautery is applied to the hip bone, another to the mouth of the stomach, and another to the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more beneficial and helpful it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as they do with lancets, because the patient's body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small, according to the affected limbs and joints, as was previously described in the types of instruments.
And then treat the cauterized area with flour of barley cooked with honey, and with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On cauterization for numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep, and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries, according to the size of the limb.
And let your cautery enter the skin a little bit. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes numbness affecting the hands and feet, and the vertebrae of the spine, and the nerves which innervate that limb are cauterized, and the numbness is removed.
But do not presume on this unless you are knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the pathways of the nerves which move the entire body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of albaras (which is morphea).
When albaras is old and no medicinal ointments help, then cauterize it with a cautery, pressing it slightly according to the amount of the disease's severity until the whiteness and the discoloration are removed, and its color is changed.
And then treat it with flour of barley and lentils mixed with rosemary oil and with leaves of arnica, and with a part of pigeon's blood or swallow's blood, all mixed together.  Then apply a linen cloth and place it over the area until healed.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, in the way it is curled.
And the learned ones say it should be cauterized with the back cautery in the middle; but I do not see it this way, for I fear it might become ulcerated, and I have seen this a number of times before.
And it is more appropriate that it be cauterized in a circle around it, as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 11.
Of the cauterization of malignancies.
When some malignancy is delayed in maturation, and this is due to the excess of its formation, or the patient's age, as they are old and decrepit, with little blood, or due to the length of time, and you want to hasten the maturation of the malignancy; then cauterize in a circle around it with many small pointed cauteries; and then leave it, for this will hasten its maturation.
But if you want its perforation with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, whose form is this:  and place it in the middle of the malignancy until it penetrates the core.
And if the cautery is in that place which is lower, so that the arm's condition is relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Of the cauterization of a corrosive (or eating away) condition.
A corrosive is a wandering corruption in the limb, which corrodes it as if fire consumed dry wood.
Therefore, if this corrosive is in a place suitable for cauterization with fire, then use cauterizing irons, small ones in some cases, and larger ones as appropriate for the area where the corrosive is located.
And then cauterize it from every side until you eradicate all the corrosion and no trace of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply dressings of crushed herbs mixed with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scabs are gone and all the corruption is eradicated.
And then treat it with a flesh-generating ointment.
But if, after three days, healthy flesh has grown where there was no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on the remaining corrupted areas.
And sometimes a corrosive medicine is used, for it serves as a substitute for cauterization. But however, cauterization with fire is a more immediate remedy.
And I have already discussed the treatment with sharp medicine in the division, therefore take it when you need it.
Chapter 53.
Of cauterization of ulcers and other similar afflictions.
Many times in the parts below the afflicted foot, an ailment called an ulcer develops, a deep, gaping wound, which weakens the foot.  Cauterization in these cases is done in two ways:  cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire involves igniting a concave cautery, resembling a small burning spoon, made of iron, fitting the size and shape of the ulcer's entire area, with fine edges.
Then place this ignited cautery on the ulcer, and gently rotate your hand with the cautery around the ulcer until the cautery reaches the depth of the ulcer. And leave it for three days until it begins to heal. Then apply a poultice made of crushed wild mallows and salt, and leave the poultice on it for one night, for this will eradicate the ulcer from its roots.
Then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if this does not work, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, then you must administer cauterization with water.  Take a cannula made of bronze, iron, or even a piece of vulture bone, and place it over the ulcer.  Then, pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand. Rotate the cannula, pressing firmly around the ulcer's edges, so that the hot water reaches the root of the ulcer. Allow the patient to endure the hot water for an hour, then remove the cannula; for the ulcer, with its roots, will be eradicated.
And thus do with these, and then with the others, until all those affected by the ailment on the body are treated.
And then treat those wounds where the roots of the ulcers have been eradicated with ointments that generate new flesh.
Chapter 44.
Of cauterization for tremor.
When someone has tremor due to cold in the nerves, or due to a cold fever, or some similar cause, then it is necessary for you to cauterize that person with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one cautery between each vertebra; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach, using an olive-oil cautery.
And the tremor subsides in them, hastening the recovery from the cold illness.
Chapter 45.
Concerning cauterization for white spots (or blemishes) appearing on the body.
When white spots or pustules appear on the body, and these are caused by thick, corrupted matter; then, as soon as they appear, it is necessary to cauterize the opening (or affected area) with a thin cautery made of myrtle wood, the tip of which is heated; or with the roots of a long-stemmed herb; or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes, cauterization is performed at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner; and they are not allowed to develop, and the excess is broken up by these means, and healed.
However, this must be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
Chapter LVI.
On the cauterization of accidental blood flow through an arrow wound.
Many times, an arrow wound is incised toward the ulcer, which extends outward, or towards the incision of an abscess or cauterization of the limb, and similar cases, and it is difficult to stop that.
Therefore, if this happens to someone, then you should press with your hand on the opening of the arrow wound, and place your index finger over it, pressing it repeatedly until the flow of blood beneath your finger is stopped and it does not flow out.
Then place the small and large olive-shaped cauteries, several of them, in the fire, and blow on them until they are very hot.
Then, take one of those [cauteries], small or large, depending on the location and the wound where the artery is broken, quickly placing it over that vein after you have stopped the flow with your finger, and hold the cautery until the blood is cauterized and constricted.
And if the blood is expelled from the artery's opening when you remove your finger and remove the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from among those prepared in the fire, and thus do not stop doing this one after another until the blood is constricted.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and that another harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to constrict the blood, especially if the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction if it is not already extracted, for when it is extracted, the extremities of the patient are most often burned, and the blood is constricted, or it is bound with a thread or bandage with a strong constriction, or that medicines whose virtue is to constrict the blood are placed upon it; and they are pressed with a pillow with a proper constriction.
However, whoever administers proper constriction with bandages and with constriction with cloths or by applying burning substances and similar things, does not succeed quickly.
But if this befalls someone and the physician is not present, nor medicine, then act with careful application of the finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and aim for a great constriction there until the blood is stopped from the main part of the wound, and the finger does not leave the artery; and above the artery, it should be sprinkled with very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and consider whether cautery or medicine is needed.
Of incisions and perforations, and cupping, and phlebotomy, and wounds, and their like.
We have already discussed in the first chapter all illnesses in which cautery with fire is appropriate, along with burning medicines, and the occasions and causes of it, and the necessary instruments with the forms of cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the supremacy of the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the seeker of this will be relieved of that which they have asked for.
And before I begin this, children, you must understand that in this chapter, it is similar to the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of this, great caution is required, for in the operation described in this chapter, evacuation is often achieved, which involves blood loss—the lifeblood being at risk—by the opening of a vein, or incisions on an abscess, or the opening of a wound, or the cleansing of a plague, or the extraction of an arrow, or the removal of a stone, and similar procedures. And all of those are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in their hands lies death.
And I warn you, that this should not befall you, because sometimes the ways of men, with their many diseases, can affect you. And some of them are angered by their illness, and death is easy for them,  because of the severity of their illness, and the length of their suffering, and with the illness comes fear, which signifies death.
And of those is the one who gives you their possessions and says, "You are hoping for health," and their illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not consent to those who come to you with this affliction; let your caution be greater than your desires and your haste; and do not presume any cause about it except with sure knowledge that verifies this for you, and which leads to a praiseworthy outcome.
Use in the treatment of all your patients prognostication and indication of the path to health, for in this lies your help in acquiring fame and memory, praise and glory.
Oh, children, may God inspire your understanding and not withhold guidance and protection from you, for that is in his power.
And I have organized this chapter, distinguishing it by sections, just as the chapter on cauteries did, regarding the treatment of the head to the feet, so that what you seek and need regarding this matter may be made easier for you.
First Chapter.
Of the treatment of water that is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often occurs in children after birth, and when the wet-nurse presses the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it arises due to hidden causes that are unknown.
However, I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those I saw, death followed, and therefore I have considered it a hopeless case in them.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and their head was always swollen until they could no longer support themselves due to the size of their head, and that the moisture continued to increase until they died.
And this moisture is either accumulated between the skull and the bone, or accumulated beneath the bone over the spine.
When the operation involves moisture between the skull and the bone, and the abscess is small, then you should cut open the middle of the head with a cut in the width, and the length of the cut should be about two fingers until the moisture flows out.  This is the procedure for draining the fluid (or, perhaps, the "sputum" / "pus").
And if the moisture is more extensive and the abscess larger, then there are two cuts made in this manner:
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, a sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head opened on all sides, and the water is squeezed out when you press on it with your hand from all around; this should not be hidden from you. You should cut into the middle of the head with three cuts in this manner:
And then remove all the moisture through the incisions.  Then bind the incisions with cloths and lint.  Then apply wine and oil to the area for the next five days.  Then loosen the bandages and treat the wound with poultices and salves, and bandage the head evenly.  The patient should eat dry foods with little moisture until the affected area is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, where you consider the size of the abscess and the collection of water, for sometimes it is more in the back of the head, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left side.  Therefore, make the incision where the abscess and collection of water appear most prominent, and do it in a way that seems best to you.
And be careful not to cut an artery, lest blood flow out and the patient die from that loss of blood with the evacuation of moisture.
Second Chapter.
When there is persistent inflammation or swelling in the eyes or feet, and the usual medical treatments have not been effective, then the final treatment in such cases is the incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise them if the patient is leaning back, first rub the area with a rough cloth to make the arteries prominent. Then, hold the patient's neck with a cloth and carefully find where the vein pulsates.  These locations, near the ears, often conceal themselves, and are not easily found in all people.
And then mark the artery with a probe, and then cut each one with a sharp surgical instrument, making an incision that reaches the bone, and that is appropriate for the breadth of the head.
And if you wish, insert the instrument under the artery and sever it at the points above.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers joined together, for when any vein is incised, blood flows out in a pulsating manner, leaping out with frequent spurts.
But if the artery is not apparent to the touch, then you must measure the distance of three fingers from the ear, and then mark it with a probe, and proceed to the bone.
And the amount of blood that needs to be drawn is about a wine-cup (i.e., approximately 1/4 pint), though it may vary more or less depending on the patient's constitution and the fullness of their artery.
And then consider the wound, and if there remains on the bone any cause of inflammation, then incise that so that a painful abscess does not develop.
And then staunch the wound with lint and bandages of cloth, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
When someone experiences a chronic headache or sudden severe pain, due to acrid humidity and the quality within the temples, or if it is a persistent and intense headache that resembles them, and medicine's curative methods don't help,  we have found success in these illnesses through the extraction of arteries in the temples, or cauterization of those, as we have demonstrated.
And the method of the operation and extraction of those is that the patient shaves the hair which is on the temples; and identify the visible artery in the temple because it is shown to you by its pulsation, and is hidden from except a few men due to the intensity of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the patient's neck should be tightened with a cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or apply hot water to the area until the artery becomes clearly visible to you. And then you take the instrument of which this is the shape: And then you carefully scrape the skin with that instrument until you reach the artery; and then you apply heat there and extract it, moving it in various directions, until you pull it free from the skin and expose the tissues below it, from all sides.
But if the artery is delicate, then wrap it with the end of the instrument, and then cut off a portion of it according to the extent to which its ends are stretched and contracted, and there will be no bleeding, because if it is not cut, the blood will not flow completely.
And then evacuate the blood by one to three ounces.
And if the artery is large, then you should tie it in two places with strong double-layered linen or cords, and do not cut it before the wound is healed, lest bleeding ensue.
And then cut away the excess tissue that lies between the two ligaments; and do this either at that time or later.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery, down to the bone, with a knife cautery until the ends are cut, and it will be in the place of the same work.
And it is a better course of action if the patient does not have a fever or is not of a hot temperament; for cautery is of those things that aid in the finalizing of humours, and therefore it is better for a cure.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the area should be packed with old lint, and then gently powdered, and then treated with drying medicines that promote flesh regeneration and with poultices until healed.
And if, while working, a further flow of blood from the artery occurs, then you should attempt to stop the bleeding, either with a cautery or by packing the area with lint, and pressing firmly with your hand until the bleeding is controlled. And if none of those things are available to you, then place your finger over the area until the bleeding is stopped, and apply water of intense coldness to the area until the bleeding subsides, and it is constricted as needed.
And the easiest way to extract an artery is to cauterize the vein with this cautery, having two knives, since you have marked the two places with a probe, and place each, very hot, until they reach the bone and divide the vein in two places, so that what is between the ends of it is extended, because it is not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And the sharpness of the two knives is essential. However, if the sharpness is less than two knives, it is better, because if it is sharp like two knives close to each other, it will not cut through the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since there is some thickness in those places, the heat of the fire is contained there, and the flesh is cut easily.
And this method is better than any other method and more easy.
And the space between the two knives should be the width of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the treatment of the body from acute and acrid humors of the oils.
When the humors are acrid, and the veins on the top of the head are affected, and you find no medicinal cause for the cure, and sometimes the patient's face reddens and feels a crawling sensation on their forehead as if ants were crawling, and the oils in the affected area are weakened, moist, and even the skin is reddened, and the eyelids are inflamed by the acridity of the tears, the ancient physicians were sometimes unsuccessful in their treatment for this condition, and called the cure "unsuccessful".
And you order the patient to shave the hairs on their forehead, and then make three equally spaced incisions on the forehead along its length. And if the length of the incision is two fingers wide: one incision should be placed equidistant from the length of the nose, in the middle of the forehead; the second should be placed at a length slightly beyond the movement of the eyelids near the tear ducts; and the third should be on the other side.
And beware of cutting the arteries which are on either side, and keep your hand from continuing the incision on both sides of the jawbones. And if the space of each incision is the quantity of three fingers joined together.
And if you have a prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which you dry the blood.
And then insert the spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the shape of the incision: which is from the temple area to the second incision. Scrape with that the entire membrane that is between the two incisions with the spatula that is over the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then withdraw that spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument called a knife, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that against the flesh adjacent to the membrane, and the blunt edge against the bone, and push that in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision continue along the membrane, and then do this in the middle fissure until the other fissure.

Chapter 5.
Of the treatment of sores/wounds by cauterization and the ulcers descending from the parts inside the head.
When in someone there are sharp and continuous cauterizations, and the patient's ulcers are small and extensive, and already the care of them is weakened, and the eyelids ulcerated and the skin around them is damaged, and there is sharp pain and consequent tension in the forehead area, then know by these symptoms that the matter and cauterizations come only from many veins of the parts within the head.
And the best treatment in these cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then make an incision in the middle of the forehead, a small one by its width (and begin with an incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be until the bone is exposed; and keep your hand away from the temples' edges, which are often inflamed due to chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or dry cloth, and then fill the two edges of the incision with old cloth or linen. And then cover with powders mixed in wine and oil, so that a painful abscess doesn't form.
And when you see that the abscess has decreased, then you should rub the bone until the flesh starts to grow, and then treat it with a drying regimen to promote flesh regeneration, just as if you take two parts wheat flour and four parts colophony, mix them, and make a poultice from them, which should be applied to these wounds to encourage flesh growth.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the treatment of what afflicts the ears.
Every affliction in the ears belongs to one of four kinds: either it's a mineral, or something mineral-like, such as iron or glass; or it's from a plant, like a thorn or bean; or it's a flowing substance, like water and sour wine and their likenesses; or an animal.
And therefore, when a stone or a similar unyielding substance lodges in the ear, and you place the ear in the sun, and if the stone moves, then apply to it some kind of olive oil or resin, and then administer extraction, and make the person sneeze with a condiment, and plug the nostrils during the sneezing episode. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear outward, because often it is expelled by these methods.
And if it isn't expelled, then administer extraction with the instrument called a *gesti epicicarios* (small, pointed, surgical instrument), of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the instrument, good; and if not, then administer extraction with a thin, small spoon (or similar instrument).
And therefore, if it is not expelled by that, then make a copper cannula and insert the end of the cannula into the ear hole and seal around the cannula with softened wax and oil so that only the cannula is open. And then extract with your hand using strong pulling, for often this, as we have recounted, works.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a lighted torch or a suitable device with which birds' nests are captured, and place a small amount of that at the end of the wound, and then wrap a decent quantity of cloth around it. And then gently insert that into the ear hole, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And therefore, if it is not expelled using all the methods we have described, then you should consider cutting it before it becomes a painful abscess or spasm.
And the method of cutting it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and remove the blood according to its potency, and then make a cut between your hands and turn the ear outward, and make a small incision at the root of the ear towards its fat, in the location firmly held by you. And let the incision be moon-shaped until it reaches the stone, and once the stone is found, remove it if possible with appropriate instruments; and then close the incision quickly and treat it until it heals.

And do not do this when you think that it is already softened by the vapor of the ear, and place it in small pieces, and carefully remove them with forceps or with a subtle maneuver, or with an incision as we have described, for this will ease the removal.
However, if water has entered the ear, the patient should initially use sneezing remedies, and fill their ear with old cotton, and lie on the side of the affected ear, and if this forcefully expels the water, good. If not, then take smooth, small stones of the length of a finger, and heat them slightly in the fire, and the patient should insert one of these stones into the ear canal, and step on the foot on that side and strike with another stone against the stone inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water has been expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water has been expelled. And once the water is expelled in this way, you should take paper or linen, and place one end in the ear, and extend the other end with fire until the greater part of it is burned.

However, in the extraction of a creature entering the ear, if it is a small creature, such as a tick or the like, treat it in the same way as we have said in the previous section; but if it is a large creature, which appears in the ear canal, then administer the extraction with instruments and hooks, and it is easier to deal with than all the other ailments that occur within the ear.
However, in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said previously in the section on distillations and your treatment doesn't work, then you should expose the ear to the sun's rays.
