And whatever is poured into the ear should first be warmed slightly by fire. And be careful that the substance you are distilling into the ear is not too cold, for ear remedies do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the treatment of ear blockages.
Sometimes, children born with an undeveloped part of their mothers' uteruses and sometimes even grown men, experience ear blockage due to a wound, or an abnormal growth of flesh within it; this blockage sometimes occurs at the entrance of the ear canal, preventing its opening, and sometimes in the external areas around it.
And that which occurs at the entrance, preventing the opening, is in the most difficult circumstances to heal.
And in that which allows the opening, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and observe it. And therefore, if blockage appears, open it with a very thin instrument, of which this is the shape:
If its tip has a small width, with one side sharp and the other not; and the instrument has two thin ends so that it does not damage the ear.
And therefore, if the blockage is due to flesh grown in the ear or its canal, then remove it with fine forceps and cut it away forcefully until all the flesh is removed.
And if the blockage is in the entrance to the ear, then take a thin, fine reed and warm it slightly with fire, then place it in the ear canal.
And therefore, if you have detected a blockage causing difficulty for the patient to hear, relieve the pressure gently, and take care not to damage the nerves of the ear.
Then place a cloth over the ear, sized according to the width of the hole you created, wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the wound's healing, or take some and dissolve it in water and sprinkle the pulverized residue onto it.  If the ear has become inflamed, then you must remove the poultice and replace it with another poultice wrapped in a plaster made from rose oil until the inflammation subsides; and then treat it until it is healed.
However, if bleeding occurs, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use other treatments to stop the bleeding, which is described in the treatise on distillations.
Chapter 8.
Of the treatment of a stye that forms on the eyelids.
Styes that form on the eyelids of the eyes sometimes are moist, sometimes are dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with tweezers or forceps, and carefully cut them away from their roots, and then apply a compress to the area.
But if pain with blood flow occurs, then you should cauterize them with a fine, lens-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is close to the incisions, and sometimes they return when any cause of their roots remains; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often a cause resembling a swelling in its strength and hardness develops in the eyelids of the eyes, and therefore swelling is called.
And it is a buildup of thick moisture in the eyelid above and below.
Therefore, the treatment for them is that you must then consider if the swelling is originating within the eyelid itself and spreads outwards; and the cause of it is mild.
Therefore, make a small incision along its length, then gently scrape it away from all sides until it recedes, and then suspend it with forceps and cut it off.
And if the swelling is large, then you should close it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you, for the ointment will restore and heal it.
And if the size is receding towards the inner part of the eyelid from the surface inward, then raise the eyelid, suspend the swelling with forceps, rather than making an incision, and press it from all sides.  And if the eyelid is stretched by an incision, this causes no harm.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with something that promotes healing until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a cyst on the eyelid above the eye.
A cyst is a swelling that forms within the tissues of the eyelid above the eye, and it most frequently affects children, making their eyes sore and causing difficulty when they wake up in the morning. They cannot look at the sunlight because of this, as their tears are constantly running down their faces.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, sleeping on their faces; and their eyelids below their eyebrows are damp, and there is a swelling, and a visible tendency towards a boil/abscess appearing in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel a swelling/boil beneath it.
And a method of treatment is that the patient places their head on your lap, and you place a cloth over them and heat it, and make a circle from it according to the size required to enclose the swelling on all sides, and then place that over it and press with your fingers so that the swelling is squeezed into the middle of the circle, and then puncture the middle of that swelling with a lancet, with a slit no larger than the slit made in a bloodletting procedure.
But in the process, you must break open the cyst completely until you reach the swelling, because in most cases the swelling proceeds through a rupture, like a white fat-like substance.
Then extract it with the cloths that you have wrapped between your fingers on the outside edges and rub it between your right and left hand until it has disappeared.
And be careful not to force the opening, so that with the lancet you do not reach the eye and damage it.
But if it does not appear to be swelling at the beginning of the incision, but you push slightly into the opening with ease until the swelling proceeds; then extract it in the way we have mentioned; and then apply cloths dipped in warm water and wine, and place them over the area, and compress them with powders.
And some rub salt and place it in the cavity of the incision, so that any remaining moisture is absorbed.
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if you find a hot abscess in the area, then treat it with cooling poultices until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Methods of treating the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes there are growths beneath the natural skin and they become hardened, they irritate the eye and cause various illnesses, such as constant weeping and softening of the eyelids and tissues, which ultimately can damage the eye, an ailment called "tactinir" of the eye.  It is treated in four ways: either by cauterization with fire; or with a sharp medicine as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization; or a cauterization is done through incision and stitching; or with probes, as I will explain.
And therefore, you must place the patient's head on your lap; then gently close the eyelid with your left hand.  If it closes easily, good; if not, insert a needle into the part where the growth is, below the eyelid.  Pierce the needle through the growth, close to the edge of the eyelid, near the hairs.  Pull the growth upward, away from the eyelid, and then gently close the eyelid with your fingers.
Then, in the area beneath the eyelid, beneath the hardened growths, make an incision from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.  Remove the growth, and place small, powdered cotton or cloth beneath the eyelid, then press the eyelid down with a probe, forming a shape similar to a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape must be adjusted according to how much the eyelid is raised, because this varies from one person to another. Some need a significant incision of the eyelid, corresponding to the amount of the eyelid's swelling.
And then, use the probe along the two lines you have marked, starting from the larger tear duct and going towards the smaller one. And if the incision is near the natural hairs, make it as wide as the king's finger.
Then, place the forceps on one of the two corners of the wound, and scrape the entire area clean.  Then, using the suture, join the two edges of the wound with fine wool or similar material, and clean the blood.  If you desire, apply whatever excess material you have to the eyebrows with some astringent.  If not, do not bother.
Then, leave the stitches of wool in place for three or four days, and then treat the wound.
And if you wish, leave the wound without stitches, and treat it with things that dry and constrict, for the eyelid is raised toward the sealing of the wound and its adherence; but stitches are better.  Thus, the ancient practitioners of surgery say this.
But nonetheless, there should be care for the patient, and it is a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
There is another method in surgery, and it is that you make an incision on the eyelid shaped like a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three forceps arranged and gathered according to this shape:
But if this is not possible, holding the forceps, and this is not effective, then take a needle with thread and place it in the middle of the shape, and insert the other thread next to the larger tear duct, and the second thread next to the smaller tear duct, and adjust the threads equally between those two points. Then, using those threads, elevate the eyelid, raising the entire eyelid along the shape we described.
And then close the two edges of the wound with stitches, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if an abscess occurs near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with a poultice and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes the eyelashes of the eyelid become turned inward, in which case you should use what we have described regarding incisions; however, suturing in each case is more artificial.
To treat with canes is according to this method: and it is that you turn the eyelid and cut the figure inside as I have described; and then, artificially, make two thin canes or sticks, whose length should be equal to the length of the eyelid and whose width should be less than the width of the space. And you have already incised at the extremities of each part, where you have the threads.
Then carefully attach each [thread] to what is superfluous of the eyelid of the eye, and tightly bind the canes to each part, and leave it for several days, because the flesh will die due to the binding and become rotten and will soften until it falls off by itself.
However, if it has been delayed, then break it with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Therefore, when it is solidified, the eyelid is elevated and the skin does not hurt.
And this is the method for two canes, which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of the length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Of lifting painful hairs in the boil with a needle when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural hairs, hard and harsh, causing pain in the boils, then the operation for them is that you take a fine needle and place it on a fine linen thread or silk, and then you should adjust the ends of it very subtly. And the length of the threads should be about the length of a palm.
And then you should attach another fine thread underneath it quickly, and use the ends of that if you wish.
And then place the patient's head on your lap, and keep it close to the light so your work is clear, for it is a delicate and precise operation.
Then, carefully insert the needle into the root of the attached hair, which needs careful handling, and quickly penetrate the eyelid until you remove it above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair upwards and put it in the tweezers, and there should be one, two, or three hairs, but no more.
Then, using your hand, pull it out until the hair is removed from the eyelid with the tweezers, leaving the natural hair behind.
However, if the tweezers are pulled and the hair is not removed, you will pull the tweezers downwards with the forceps you have prepared until the tweezers are pulled out of the wound completely.
And then, view the eye, because then you will throw away the tweezers and the forceps, and each person will close and cover the eye, and leave it covered for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is too short, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and lift it using that, or let it grow until it's a bit longer, and then you lift it.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of an injury that occurs on the eyelid above.
When an injury occurs to the eye, and the eyelid is contracted upwards, either to the right or left side, due to poor healing from a wound, incision, or cauterization, and is called a harelip, and this injury or is natural or accidental, and is caused by the poor healing of a wound, incision, or cauterization, or by similar poorly treated wounds.
The method of the operation in this case is that you should break open that scar tissue and separate the edges, and place a piece of soft cloth between them, and tie them together until they are healed.
And it is not appropriate to use treatments that are drying or constricting; for if you do this, it will return the injury to a worse state than before the wound was inflicted.
Use remedies that are softening, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice made of water in which marshmallow root and seeds are boiled, or a diachylon ointment (which has been liquefied with some oil).  Place the cloth in the poultice or ointment and apply this treatment.
And the goal of your treatment is that you should restore the area to the original condition before it became scarred.
And this operation, which we have described, is nothing but a correction of the injury, restoring the area to its former shape.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment for injuries to the lower eyelid.
This injury to the lower eyelid, properly called an injury, can be either natural or accidental in origin.
But accidental injuries are caused by blows, cuts, cauterization, or similar things.
And the way to treat it is to take a needle with double thread, and pierce the flesh with it, passing it from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then, gently pull the flesh outward with the needle and stitch it with the needle, and cut away the surrounding tissue with a wide spatula. And then if the eyelid returns to its proper shape, that is good; but if not, take a thin strip of material and place it in the gap, and use it to reposition the eyelid, closing the two openings on the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two slits, of the incisions you have made, meet, and form an angle where they come together, so the figure is formed by their meeting.
And this figure is the Greek letter: lambda.
And now, remove the flesh to the extent that the sharp edge of the lower eyelid is exposed.
And now, bring the separated parts together and stitch them with wool thread; and then treat them with the softening remedies and poultices we have mentioned, until they heal.
And if it rises again through a fissure, suture, or cautery, then you should make a flat incision on the eyelid skin, following the previous procedure. And now, separate with a poultice between the two lips, as we have said.
In summary, concerning the treatment of a raised (or prolapsed) eyelid, the operation must be performed on the affected person, according to the type of prolapse, and those prolapses take various forms.
And skilled artisans always prepare instruments in every possible way to restore the affected area to its natural, or nearly natural, form.  The patient should be properly positioned for the operation and the instruments should be used with the utmost care.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
When this adhesion occurs in such a way that the eyelid is firmly stuck to the white part of the eye, restricting its natural movement and function, you must carefully insert the tip of a tool beneath the eyelid and lift it or gently separate it with a hook.
Then carefully cut the adhesion with a thin blade, avoiding overly sharp incisions, using a blunt edge to minimize discomfort for the patient.  Leave the blade so that it doesn't injure the eye with its sharpness.
And make your incision such that it easily separates the adhesion until the eyelid returns to its natural form, and all the adhesion is removed.
Then you apply salt water to the eye, using it to wash the eye, or use a solution of honey dissolved in water.
Then gently separate the eyelid from the eye, and place wool soaked in egg white over the eye.  After three days, use healing remedies until it is completely healed.

Chapter 17.
Concerning the incision of a carbuncle and that which is born of inflamed flesh in the wound.
Sometimes in the wounds of some, red flesh grows, and it overgrows until it covers the pupil, or is close to or tightens on the eyelids, and strongly presses the eyelids on the outer edges, and resembles the redness of a balustrade.

Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of hawks.

Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
When various flesh growths arise in the nose, some of which are caused by something resembling a scorpion's tail or a fish's polyp; and this illness is called polyp because it resembles that depicted fish. And some of it is cancerous, ulcerated, of a dark color; and some of it is lion-like flesh, not dark in color.

Chapter 25.
Of ulcers on the nostrils' extremities.
Often an ulcer appears on the extremities of the nose, and it grows and worsens in some days until its sight is extremely painful.
And therefore, you should incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and remove all of it. And then place a cautery or a burning medicine on the area.
However, if its incision is delayed until it has grown, then consider if it is hardened, hard, of dark color, and with little sensation.  Then do not touch it with iron, for it is a deep-seated abscess; for often the one who incises this abscess brings great harm upon the person it was on.
And if the abscess is tender and not dark-colored, and sometimes incision is possible throughout, then drain the patient and incise it without fear, and treat the area with something that dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when the continuity is dissolved by plague or similar ailments.
Know that when you encounter a break in continuity in one of these cartilages, you achieve little in that procedure except in certain individuals.
Therefore, when you encounter any of those causes, consider whether it's a fresh plague wound with its own blood, and if so, you should bring the two lips of the wound together with sutures, and then treat it until it is healed.
And if the edges of the break in continuity are already separated, and each is made sound, then you must scrape each part of their visible skin until blood comes out. And then bring the two edges together with sutures, and tightly bind them.  Powder the area with dragon's blood and crushed herbs, and place a piece of diaphanous plaster, or another of the consolidating plasters, on top of the powder.  Leave this dressing for two or three days, and then remove it and replace the plaster.  Leave it until the pus is gone, then continue treatment with ointment until healed.
But the method of suturing is that you rejoin the break in continuity either with needles, as we have described in suturing the belly, or join the break in continuity with adhesive, as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the removal of those things that come between the two edges of the inside parts.
Often, men develop hard abscesses resembling kernels of grain within the interior parts of their lips. Some of these are small, and some are larger.
Therefore, you must turn back the lips and open up each of those hardnesses from every side, and then, after the excision, fill the area with crushed sage until the blood is stopped, then wash the area with sour wine and treat the area with something astringent until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of the flesh adhering to the gums.
Often, fleshy growths form on the gums, which the ancients called "albulus".  In that case, you should suspend it with forceps or hold it with scissors, and cut it away from its base, allowing the pus or blood to drain.
And then apply crushed sage or other astringent or drying powders to the area.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for the cautery will prevent it from returning.

Chapter 30.
Of the removal of teeth.
You must treat the tooth's pain with all care and take your time in every removal of it, for there is no cause that outweighs its extraction when it is removed, for it is a subtle substance, until its expulsion during removal.
However, when the patient insists on having that tooth removed, you must be firmly certain which tooth is diseased, for many times the pain deceives the patient and seems to be in a healthy tooth, and you remove that one, and the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is removed, for we have already seen this through the barbers' operations.
Therefore, how should you be certain which is the diseased tooth, and that you know it well?  Then you must scarify the area around the tooth with a substance which has some effect, until the tooth is separated from the gum on all sides. Then carefully remove it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, little by little, until it is extracted.
And then, if necessary, use strong forceps for a good grip or powerful extraction, and place the patient's head between your knees so you hold it firmly, and then pull the tooth straight, in such a way that you don't break it, or give the patient undue pain.
And therefore, if it is not easily extracted, then take one of those instruments and place it under the tooth from all sides with ease, and exert the extraction just as was done at first.
However, if the tooth is fractured or decayed, then you must cover that cavity with cloth and compress it with a thin, pointed instrument so that it is not fractured during your attempt to remove it with forceps.
And you must carefully open up the area around the gum with incisions on all sides, and be very careful in your strength so you don't break it and leave a piece behind, and return to the patient for the bad luck it could cause, which might be worse than the initial pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their rashness and boldness regarding the removal of teeth, without observing what we have recommended; for very often they cause great illnesses in people, illnesses more difficult to treat than the original problem, and the roots remain, some or all of them, or they pull out the tooth with part of the jawbone, as we have frequently witnessed.
And after the extraction, wash the mouth with hot wine or sour wine with salt.
And if bleeding ensues, as often happens, then apply a small amount of lint and sprinkle it over the wound; and if that doesn't stop the bleeding, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and fine pliers used to remove the tooth initially should be long-ended, with a short, thick handle, so they don't bend when you use them.
And they should be like that, with a thick handle, so that when you grip them, they don't bend; the ends should be short.
And they should be made of Indian iron, well-made with straight ends, and on the ends of those there should be teeth of which one fits into the other and grip tightly by constriction.  Sometimes the ends of those are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork, or a type of crane, and are very strong in their constriction.

Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth prominent above the others.

Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver filings.
When accidents to the front teeth result in a concussion and displacement due to blows or impacts, and the patient cannot hold onto what is missing, and you have treated this with astringent medicines, and your treatment has not been successful, then, because they do not hold, the missing parts are best filled with gold filings, not silver, for silver turns green and can corrode in a few days, but gold never changes.
And the filings should be of such a fineness and thickness according to the amount needed to fit between the teeth; and the method for filling is to take the filings and place twice their amount between two healthy teeth, and then to shape them with two twists of the filings between the broken teeth, whether one or several, until the material reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the binding process back to the point where you began, and press your hand with gentleness and skill until it is no longer broken.
And make sure your binding with which you hold the filings adheres firmly to the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
And then cut off any excess filings with forceps or scissors, and shape them in each place, and position them, concealing them between the healthy and broken tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightly bound as it remains.
But if it is loose or broken, bind it or them with more filings in such a manner at all times.
And this is the method for teeth, and the technique for the joining of two healthy teeth and two broken ones, in this way when they are joined:
And when it is done that a tooth or two returns to the alignment of each in their places, and are rejoined as we have described, and remain.

Chapter 34.
Of the inflammation of the ligament that affects the tongue and hinders speech.
This ligament, which sometimes lies beneath the tongue, is either natural, present from birth, or accidental, resulting from a wound that has healed.
And the treatment for it is to open the patient's mouth, with their head supported by you; lift their tongue, and then carefully cut the nerve ligament along its length until the tongue is released from its restriction.
However, if there is any hardness or knotting in it, caused by a healed wound, then you apply a poultice and cut it along its length until you have severed the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And be careful not to make a fissure in the underlying flesh, and that you don't cut too deeply and cause a flow of blood.
Then wash the area of the incision with warm water or vinegar or cold water; then place a linen pad under the tongue. This is a bandage that the patient should wear under the tongue every night so that it doesn't heal again.
But if blood flows, apply powdered vitriol to the area; but if the bleeding persists, cauterize the area with a suitable, rounded cautery. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Of the removal of a ranula (cyst) under the tongue.
Sometimes, a boil similar to a small pouch forms under the tongue, hindering the tongue's natural function; and it grows large, filling the mouth.
And the procedure is to open the patient's mouth, sitting facing the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is dark in color, or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in that area, then do not touch that, for it is cancer.
And if it has turned whitish, in which there is moisture, then you put a lancet in place and cut it with a fine scalpel, and dissect it from every side; and if blood appears during your operation, then place crushed alum, that is to say, vitriol, on top to stop the bleeding.
And then return to your work until you have extracted it completely, until it has been washed with vinegar and salt; and treat it with other remedies until it is healed.

And if it is white, soft, and its root is subtle, then it is the one that should be incised.

However, if bleeding occurs, apply water decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaf, or similar astringent remedies, until the bleeding is controlled, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat or near the two ears, and incise them, as I said before, in a similar manner to the incision of two incisions/cuts.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess that had formed in her throat, which abscess was dark-coloured, with little feeling, and which had already closed her throat. The woman was breathing with difficulty, and she had been forbidden to eat or drink water, and she would have succumbed to death if it had been one or two days more.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had formed two ramps (or possibly,  "protuberances" or "bulges") before it emerged through two openings.
Therefore, I incised it surgically and placed a lancet into one of them, and pulled out a large portion of it. Then I incised the area around the other opening, and then opened her mouth and pressed her tongue down, and then I placed the lancet into the abscess.
And then I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Thus, the woman's throat was cleared, and she immediately began drinking water, and then ate food.  Then, I didn't stop incising that abscess many times for a long time, and another abscess appeared in the same place, which I incised until it was resolved.
Therefore, I applied a cautery to her throat, thus adding to the treatment, and then I walked away, not knowing what God would do to her from that point forward.
Chapter 37.
On the incision of a boil when it is abscessed.
When a boil develops on the uvula, and it is abscessed and long, then it is called a perpendicular boil; and if it is in the lower part, thick and round, then it is called a carbuncle (or furuncle).
But when it is treated with what we have said in the division, and you do not consider your treatment complete, and sometimes the abscess is already drained and superficial, then it is necessary to incise that which is swollen and rounded; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, of a dark color or black, and it is not causing pain, then it is necessary to test the incision, for in this case there is a fear of harm to the patient.
And when you see it according to the method I have described, by its whiteness and length, you should make the patient lie down and press the tongue with an instrument to determine its shape, then place a lancet on the boil, and draw it downwards, and incise it with one of two instruments mentioned in the incision of the two boils.
And it is necessary not to incise it beyond what is required for the natural boundary without further damage, for if you incise further, you will cause further problems.
And after the incision, use what we have described for the incision of two boils, and treat it until it heals.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, it is necessary to use cauterization without hesitation, and the method of cauterization in this case is with sharp water.  You should place the patient's head on your lap, then press their tongue with the instrument we mentioned, and then take the water mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and cauterize the area with this heated substance.  Place it neither thickly nor thinly, and fill the cavity of the instrument which has this form:
Let the extremity of the cavity, where the medicine is placed, be similar to the cavity of a spoon for gathering herbs, and place the instrument with the medicine on the wound.  The patient should lie on their side so that saliva or mucus from the mouth drains away from the medicine, so that nothing drips down the patient's throat and harms them.
Then apply your hand with the medicine and press it onto the uvula for half an hour until you see it blackened and subdued by the medicine's action.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the extremity of the herb, and dip the cotton into the medicine.  Insert the herb with the cotton into a tube from above until the cotton adheres to the tube.  Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it to dry and fall off after about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it.  Then, after the cauterization, clean the area around the uvula with cotton soaked in broth, and remove any remaining medicine.  Wash with cool water, and treat externally with a liniment and internally with remedies until healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incisions and less likely to cause fear.
Sometimes the uvula is treated with something gentler than cautery and incisions, as it is healed by fumigation in this manner: take mint, yarrow, oregano, rue, Arabian squills, chamomile, marigold, and similar herbs, and combine all or some of them in a pot.  Place them in a vessel of vinegar, and bring to a boil. The pot should be tightly sealed with proper clay, and a small hole should be made in the middle of the pot.  Over this hole, place a hollow brass or silver instrument shaped this way; it should be made of silver or brass.
And the extremity of the instrument should be inserted into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube, causing the uvula to become significantly clouded.
And repeat this many times until the area is dry.  Beware that you do not perform this treatment at the beginning of an abscess, for it would worsen the abscess.  You should only do this following a diagnosis of a mature abscess.
But if this instrument is not available, then take a reed and fit a piece of eggshell to its end, to prevent the patient's mouth from being irritated.  Because the eggshell will keep the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the extraction of thorns and what adheres to the throat, and other such things.
Many times a thorn or fishbone or some other thing adheres to the throat.

Or take an eel and bind it with thread, and put it inside, and it will be extracted; or take a leek and suddenly put it in and suddenly pull it out.

Chapter 39.
Of the removal of bloodsucker attached to the throat.
When a bloodsucker is treated with what we have described in the division of treatment with medicines and not with remedies, then consider the patient's throat in the sunlight, after you have pressed the patient's tongue with the instrument we have described to you.

Then take a vessel full of cold water, and open the patient's mouth into it, and rinse the mouth with it; and do not let the patient swallow anything, and hold the water for another hour with your hand, because as soon as the bloodsucker feels the water, it will drop off.
But if it is not expelled by what we have described, then fumigate the patient with cines or asafoetida using the instrument we described for fumigation of the uvula.

And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and keep the patient from drinking, and let them eat salty things and garlic, and do not let them drink water; and with this regimen it is necessary that it be expelled.
And this is the shape of the instrument with which the bloodsucker is treated when it is visible; and it is similar to tongs in shape, except that it has a curve in some places. which is placed on the bloodsucker, and its tip resembles a crane's beak, which is thin, on which it is filed, so that when you grasp something, it doesn't slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are of many kinds, in many ways, according to their appearance, each of which is discussed in this book.
And they are diverse in their puncture and incision in two ways, of which one is according to the type of abscess itself; and due to the moisture we handle.
And the second way is according to the locations on the body where they occur, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy area is different from an abscess in a joint; and for each of these, there is its own judgment concerning the operation.
And some abscesses should not be punctured until the pus has matured or filled.
Other abscesses should be lanced while they are inflamed, and have not matured to their fullness, such as abscesses near joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its presence extends until its surrounding area can be treated, and often the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, causing contraction of that limb; or the abscess is near a vital limb, and if you delay its puncture until it is mature, it may harm the vital limb; or if it is near the anus, then lance it while it is not mature; and you should not lance it while it is inflamed, or incompletely formed; but the one near the anus, so as not to spread the infection deeper into the anus and cause a fistula, or result in an area that won't heal. However, you must know the time for puncturing the abscess, and that is when it is mature.
And you know it is mature when there is a cessation of pain and fever, and a reduction in swelling and throbbing, and the abscess's head becomes firm, and the other signs.
And because the incision should be made in the location of the pus below the abscess, and from the healing of the swelling.
And the incision should always be made along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the limbs or feet, and not in areas of tendons, nerves, or arteries; and, in general, in all locations where there is no reflection; for in those that are reflected, use incision appropriate to that part of the body.
However, when the abscess is in a fleshy area, it is better to let it be until the digestion of it is confirmed by the swelling, as we have said; for if you make the incision before this, it prolongs the illness and produces more filth, and it strengthens the hardened layers and depth of it.
But some abscesses are incised according to the width of the flesh or body, depending on the necessity, or according to the specific part of the body.
And you should administer a small incision in small abscesses, and a large and wide incision or multiple incisions in large abscesses, according to the variety of their size.
And other abscesses require that the pus be hollowed out and incised, when it has been done in a way that it has reached a point where it is ready for this, just as happens with boils and buboes.
And some are incised with a three-angled cut; and some are incised like a myrtle leaf shape, like abscesses in the groin area.
And some of them are given a round incision, or a crescent-shaped incision, and similar to those.
But those that are not like that, such as superficial abscesses, you should only pierce them with a superficial, or flat, incision.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should pierce it and not attempt to remove all the matter at once.  Remove a portion, and then pack the abscess until the next day, and then remove a larger portion, and repeat this many times according to the stage until it is completely drained.
And especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly frail person, for often the animal spirit is weakened by the expulsion of pus, and the patient easily dies if you operate on them.
Observe this chapter with great caution, and when you make an incision to open these abscesses, you should clean the sores and then consider if the abscess's wound is small, or a flat incision, then apply a poultice of linen or old cloth.  If the abscess is large, and the incision has many fissures, then you must place medicated material into each hole until they come together.
And if a part of the skin has been cut away or indented in the abscess's perimeter, then you must fill it with old cloth or a linen strip, like when you remove breads or roots, without moisture.  Hold it tight for up to three days, then continue treating and healing it with appropriate medicines until it is healed.
But if you encounter blood flow during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar in which you've dipped a linen cloth, and place it repeatedly over the site of the blood flow.
But if the blood flow persists, then you must administer treatment and remedies, like the powders described in many parts of this book and its divisions.
But if it is wintertime, and the area of the abscess is close to many nerves, then you must soak powders in warm wine and oil, and place those over the affected areas.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you must place powders soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and keep it cool until the third day, as we have said.  Then you must drain the abscess, clean it, and administer appropriate remedies for its healing until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for understanding the universal treatment of abscesses, but according to the method of distinction, I have already described the way to treat each type of abscess individually, in separate chapters.
Chapter 41.
Of the incision of abscesses that occur on the nape of the neck.
Small abscesses occur on the nape of the neck, and these are of various types of swellings which contain membranes surrounding them; and the different types are of different sizes.
And some of them contain pus, and that which contains moisture similar to lime. And some contain moisture resembling dust, and similar to them; and some are like stones, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in their extraction, there is no fear, as their extraction does not cause any counter-action (or opposition) to the [process of] healing.
The procedure for their incision is to probe them with an instrument of a shape fitting this (or similar to this), which is called a "probe," until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is moisture, then cut it with an incision along its length by a superficial incision in this manner:
And begin the incision from the line B to the line G.  Then, once the moisture is evacuated, you scrape away the skin that contained the moisture, and leave absolutely no residue, for if you do, it may return if any remains.
Then, soak some cotton in Egyptian ointment, if available, and otherwise in water and salt; and fill the wound with that and leave it until the next day, for with this, you corrode any remaining bits of that skin.
Then, repeatedly apply Egyptian ointment to the cotton a second and third time if necessary, until you are confident that no trace of the skin remains; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodes, then you incise it according to this method: with an incision shaped like a cross, and insert hooks between the two edges of the incision and scrape it from every side, and exert your effort in extracting the membrane that contains it.
But if your effort encounters resistance, then do what I have told you first.
And similarly, treat the hardened abscess with incision and healing as we have described; and incision on a hardened abscess is easier, because it contains little blood and humidity.
And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which occur on the heads of some men, in which there is no humidity at all.  This is why I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken; and if someone were to strike with that, it would cause pain.
However, for other accidental abscesses on the head, this is not relevant, such as abscesses occurring on the heads of children and near the roots of the ears.  But the incision for all these should be superficial.
And place the perforation in the lower part of them, so that the body is relieved of the pus to the lower parts.
And then treat them with whatever is appropriate for healing.
Chapter 42.

When the blood has been separated/removed.

Chapter 43.
Of the incision of a boil on the throat/neck.
The ancients prescribed this incision for boils, and yet no one in our land has been seen to perform it.

Chapter 45.
Of the incision of types of tumors.
There are many types of tumors, and I have already discussed all their types in the division.

Thus I say that with a hernia, there is heat, fever, and intense pain until the ebullition of the excess is calmed; and when putrefaction is complete, then the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a tumor, there is no heat, fever, or pain, and it resembles a lion's tooth, which is in the own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a seed, and is made thus, whether larger or smaller, and there are two types, for it is either fatty and contains moisture, and the colors of the moisture are many, as I said in the division.

Chapter 49.
Of the incision of abscesses that occur from arrow wounds or vein wounds.
When an arrow wound is made, and the clot that forms over it, often an abscess occurs.  And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are from an artery or vein: for when an abscess is from an arrow wound, it is long and concentrated in the front of the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a wheezing or whistling sound in it.
And the one that is from a vein is round and clearly visible on the body.
And an incision on this abscess is a mistake, especially in those under the armpits, in the elbows, in the neck, and in many parts of the body; and if it is large, its treatment with iron should be avoided, and the one in the extremities or the head should be avoided.
But for those that are caused by inflammation of the wound of the arrow wound, make an incision along the clot; then use hooks to widen the incision around the arrowhead and clear away the tissues until it is exposed.
Then place a needle on it and drive it through to the other side, and tightly bind the arrowhead with a double thread in two places—which I show you in the extraction of two arrows that are in the temples.

Then place on it a poultice that you have soaked in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment made with herbs and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess that occurs due to the twisting of nerves.
Thus, abscesses occur in the arteries and veins, and similarly abscesses occur in the nerves when they are injured by blows or excessive exertion, and by similar causes.

And it is a severe abscess, and it is similar in color to the rest of the body, and it is in the majority of cases without any visible sign until it is tight with violence; the patient feels a similar stupor [or numbness].
And it is not an abscess that spreads through the body, and therefore it stays under the skin, and it is hurt in all directions, but does not extend forward or backward.
Therefore, those [abscesses] that are in the joints should not be incised with iron, for a strong contraction will likely ensue.

Chapter 52.
On the treatment of an eminence (or swelling) of the navel.
An eminence at the navel is caused by various things; either by fissures in the surrounding tissue over the abdomen, and this is a kind of rupture, a part of the intestines or tissue being involved, as in other ruptures; or by blood flowing from a vein or artery, as already mentioned; or by trapped wind (gas) in the area.
However, if it's due to a fissure in the surrounding tissue and consequent extrusion of tissue, then the color of the abscess is similar to the color of the body; and it is painless, and it appears in various locations. And if it's due to the intestine protruding, then the location of the swelling, differs significantly from what we have already described; and when you press on it with your fingers, it's hidden, then returns; and it's very close to the abdominal entrance, and it's greatly magnified right by the entrance of the abdomen (or groin), and with strong exertion.
But if it's due to moisture, then it is painless, and it is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor is it swollen.
And if it's due to blood, then the abscess appears with these symptoms, showing a darkening.
But if it's due to dead flesh, then the abscess is severe, large, and hard, and is fixed in a particular quantity (or area).
But if it's due to wind (gas), then the touch of it is painless.
And the operation on it is that you should consider whether the swelling near the navel is due to blood from an arrow wound, or a vein, or wind; then you must be cautious in treating it, because there is fear and anxiety as I have shown you in the chapter where I discussed abscesses from arrow wounds and vein injuries.
And if the swelling of the navel is due to the bowel or the mesentery, then you must tell the patient to hold their breath, and be standing and stretched out; then probe with a tenta around the entire navel, and then instruct the patient to keep still while you place your hands on their side, and then encircle the area around the navel, covering the spot you marked with the probe; and then extend the half of the abscess on the upper side with a large incision; and then tie it up around with a strong thread or silk, and with the appropriate method of tying.
And then pierce the middle of the extended abscess over the ligament, and insert your finger into that opening and look for the bowel; and if you find it and it's held by the ligament, then gently extract it and push the bowel back into the belly cavity.
And if you have found the mesentery, then take it with a knife and cut away the excess parts of it.
But if it's an arrow wound or vein, then encircle that area, and then return to your procedure; and take two needles and put strong threads in them, and insert the two needles into the circle you've made around the abscess in a cross-like manner, making them penetrate; and then compress the abscess in four sections over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and let the area be until the stretched flesh is softened and falls off on its own; or cut it when it is softened and about to fall off, and then treat it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
If the swelling around the navel is caused by flesh growth or moisture, then you must drain the abscess as we have said, and remove the blood or moisture you find there, and then treat it with what is appropriate to consolidate the area.
Chapter 53.
Of the treatment of cancer.
We have already stated, in the division, the types of cancer and the ways to treat them with medicine, and that surgery is harmful in these cases, as it might create ulcers; and we have discussed cancer originating in the breast and the precautions for its treatment.

Chapter 56.

Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
An acute hernia is nothing more than a collection of moisture in the white sac, which is beneath the folds of the genitals containing the testicles, and it is called the sac.

And if the moisture is in the proper fold, then the abscess is round with a portion of the testicle, and for this reason one thinks it is another testicle.

Chapter 65.
Of the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture, which develops in the extended flank over the abdomen, on the part of the groin's folds in the abdomen, so that some intestines (or bowels) are outside the body through this rupture.
And this rupture is either of the flank or of the groin, and these two types happen due to many causes: either a blow, or a fall, or a cry, or the lifting of a heavy object, or similar things.

And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the flank, is that at the beginning, it causes sudden, intense pain; and it is a diverse swelling appearing beneath the nearby groin, and this is due to the protrusion of the intestine, and the body part of the intestine being outside the flank.
And sometimes the intestine and its covering are extruded, and this is called an intestinal hernia or a bowel rupture.
Sometimes it is caused by flatulence, and sometimes a tear in the intestine occurs and is retained there.  This causes great harm to the patient, and sometimes creates severe pain, especially when pressed.
