And do not leave any cause of intestines in the groin; and if they do, it is slight; and it returns at all times.
However, if the time is prolonged with this, a fissure in the hip is likely until the intestine descends into the hip; and this happens due to the extension of the hip, which is in the ankle as we have said, which causes the hip to be stretched, and then softened, and the area becomes inflamed and evacuated.
And the cure for it is with cautery, as was previously described.
And sometimes it is cured with an iron according to this method: and it is that the patient lies on their back between your hands, and then the area of the rupture of the abscesses is dried with incision along the width according to the amount of three days.
And then press the white hip that is under the buttock until it is uncovered.
And then take the ray (likely a type of instrument) and place it on the prominent area of the hip, and press it to the navel (or abdomen).
And then sew the two prominent areas above the hip's extremity of the ray, and join one of them to the other with stitches.
And then remove the extremity of the ray, and do not sever the hip completely, nor extend the wound, nor any other thing like this, as I wish to make you understand in the treatment of an intestinal hernia.
And then treat that with the treatment for sores, and as you would dress wounds, cleanse it and restore the sore until it is healed; for the hip is contracted and the rupture's prominence is not adjusted.
And cauterization is better in this location, for it is closer to health.
Chapter 68.
Of the softening of the testicles' swelling.
Many times the swelling of the testicles in some men is softened by this, because the tissues surrounding it are softened, and his care is needed.
Therefore, it is necessary for the one treating him to have the patient lie on his back, and to apply any softened swelling over the natural swelling; then to sew the two edges together with sutures.
And if you wish, sew the excess of softened swelling at the beginning with three or four stitches, and with firm sutures.
And then cut between the sutures; and then treat with the treatment for other wounds until he is healed and the pus subsides.
Chapter 69.
Of castration.
Know that castration, in our law, is forbidden, but, for a reason, I am obliged to mention it in this book.  This mention is not necessary except for two reasons:  one, so that when a physician is questioned about it, he knows how to treat the condition to which it applies; and the other, that we often have need to castrate other animals for our own benefit, such as bulls, goats, pigs, and similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by a fissure or incision.
However, in the method of castration by crushing, the procedure is to immerse the animal in hot water until its genitals are softened and relaxed, hanging down. And then take them with your hand until they are broken and no longer appear beneath the touch.
But in castration by fissure and incision, you must hold the animal and press the scrotum with your left hand.
And then tie the suspensory ligament, and make an incision on each testicle until both are separated.
And then cut them further once you have scraped off each. And do not leave any cause of the inflammation on them, only the fine inflammation that is on the vessels.
And this method of castration, which is done by rubbing, is better, for a strong rubbing leaves no cause in the testicles for the animal to desire mating.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Of the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two ways: one is when a space appears in the scrotum, in the area between the testicles, shaped like a woman's vulva with hair.
And sometimes urine flows from that area in the scrotum.
But in women, it's a different type: the woman's vulva on the pubis resembles a pair of male testicles, somewhat small and protruding outward like the foreskin of a man and two testicles.
And the treatment is of three types: for two types of men and one type of women. And it is necessary to cut the adhered flesh until the inflammation subsides, and then treat those wounds with the treatment for other injuries.
And in the second type of man, where there is urine in the scrotum, there is no treatment or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Of swelling and prominent flesh due to female causes.
A slight swelling, caused by natural factors, will subside and become less noticeable; sometimes, however, it becomes exaggerated in some women to the point of spreading like it does in men and hindering intercourse.
Then you should remove the excess swelling with your hand or with a lancet.
And do not use the lancet excessively, especially at the root of the swelling, so that it does not cause a flow of blood.
And then treat it with the treatment for sores until it is healed.
But flesh that arises in the womb of the mother until it fills it, and which then emerges on the body's surface, resembling a tail (and thus some initially called this "tailed disease"); therefore, we must treat it like a swelling, and cure it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
Of the treatment of elephantiasis.
Elephantiasis is when the vagina of the woman is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to an illness that preceded it, and that illness is caused either by flesh not fitting properly or by a subtle or thick film.
And it is caused either in the region of the mother's womb, or in her ribs, or in the uppermost part of her body, or in the lowermost part, and you prohibit cooking, and ingestion, and childbirth, and strongly prohibit menstruation.
And the knowledge of this is determined by observing the woman, if the blockage is apparent, immediately; but if it is not apparent, then you search for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the blockage is due to a subtle film, and it is close to the two lips, then you cook and break it apart.
And you place powders on the two lips, and then place your index fingers from both hands on top of them.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs extended, and then forcefully extend the two lips until that subtle film is broken and the blockage is opened.
And then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area, and let the person lie with it on top during the day so that the incised area does not become solid.
But if the film is thick and dense, then it is necessary that you cut it with a sharp blade or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the blockage is due to flesh, then suspend that with a hook, and cut it.
And have with you the medicines that stop blood flow without causing pain, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and vinegar, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal quickly.
And keep that for several days and apply warm compresses.
And then it should be treated with further healing treatment until it is completely healed.
And sometimes it happens in the main birth of other tissues, and then it is necessary to cut them according to this method. However, if this is due to a brain abscess, the brain abscess which is in the main part should not be treated with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids, and of cuts and red swellings that occur in the vagina of women.
Hemorrhoids are understood as the openings of many veins through which much blood flows continuously; and when they are old hemorrhoids and are formed into lumps.
And I have already discussed the types of these [hemorrhoids] and their symptoms, and will now state which ones receive treatment and which do not.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and lumps, when they are inside the rectum and do not appear to the touch, then no iron-based treatment should be done on them.
But those which are at the opening of the rectum, where one can feel them, are those that can be treated.
And you should place the woman in a cool place, and then remove the lump with scissors, or a rough cloth, and cut them at the roots.
Then sprinkle over them the flow of blood from one of the cutting powders without biting, like acacia and similar ones.
And then you should soak their feet for an hour, and then place them in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make them sit in a decoction of pomegranate rind, quince rind, and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with it on their back.
And if the blood has stopped, good; and if not, apply two leeches, and to the breasts without incision.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice, or arugula juice, or hedge-nettle juice with diluted wine, and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of a bone fracture in the arm.
Many types of abscesses occur in the arm, such as cancers, stony abscesses, ulcers, tumors, herpes, fistulas, hemorrhoids, sores, and other abscesses. And I have already described these illnesses and their types, their symptoms, and their cures in the previous sections.
And it is necessary for me to discuss in this treatise the abscess that comes on the arm when it is one of the abscesses that collects pus, and if you wish to know how the operation with iron is performed.
Therefore, you must consider if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is sharp with throbbing and phlegm and fever, and if there is redness and if pressure on it elicits a sensation, then do not hurry with puncturing it, and treat it with what helps maturation until the sharpness of the symptoms subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman lie on a bed frame with two supports; and recline her on her side and position her feet at the lower part of her abdomen, and her thighs should be spread out, and place her arms under her armpits, and bind each limb with appropriate bandages.
And then the midwife stands by the right side and administers the instrument with which the opening in the womb is made, the form of which will be detailed in the following steps.
And when you wish to administer this instrument, you should insert, with the tip, the depth of the woman's womb, and what enters the womb from the instrument should be no more than the depth of the womb and leave the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the depth of the womb, then you must place powders on the lining of the womb, so that the entrance of any instrument into the womb is prevented.
And you must place the wick that is on the instrument on the upper side, and have the person administering the instrument turn the wick until the opening of the womb is made.
And when the discharge appears, and it is touched with the hand and is bloody and wet, then you must dry that area around the head with a linen cloth.
And when the discharge is evacuated, then you must place a cloth soaked in rose oil or green oil, in which some astringent substance is present, on the wound, and place the compress over the section within the womb.
And place outside the womb, and over the perineum, a clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then it should be treated for about two days with an ointment suitable for this purpose until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed in this way, so that it is cleansed with water and honey, and then with water in which iris root or long-stalked aristolochia has been boiled, and then returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep inside the womb, then you must refrain from treating it with iron, having cure it with what we have said in the division.
Chapter 75.
To teach the midwives how to treat living embryos when they develop in a non-natural manner.
It is necessary for the midwife to recognize the signs of a natural birth at the outset.  For example, when you see the woman pushing downwards, desiring to smell the air, and relieved by the weight of the birth upon her, and hurrying the birth of the embryo, know that this birth is according to the natural form.  The expulsion of the embryo is above its head, and the placenta either hanging or suspended by the umbilical cord.
Therefore, when you see these signs, the abdomen should be pressed so that the embryo descends easily, because when it descends upon its head, it leaves the placenta behind, which is then cleaned of excess material through proper purification.
However, a birth that is different from this is unnatural and undesirable. For when the embryo emerges feet-first and the body emerges before the head and feet, or one hand or foot emerges first, or the head and one hand emerge, or the head emerges and the body falls to one side, this is a sign of unnatural and undesirable births.
Therefore, it is essential for the midwife to be knowledgeable and astute about all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of those things, and what the meaning is in them, so that it is signified by this and understood through this.
When the child is born headfirst through a natural process, and the delivery is difficult for the woman, and her strength is weakened, then have her sit up, or have the women who are assisting her hold her, and evaporate the heat from her with collected fenugreek and with moist oils.
And then the midwife should insert her fingers between the mother's buttocks, using them to separate the placenta, or cut it with her nail until she removes the moisture that remains in it. And press on the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be treated with a poultice of linseed and a poultice of fenugreek with cumin.
And then command the patient, or the attendant, to be pressed and made to sneeze with warm cloths; and hold their mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled soon.
And if both hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then you should gently return them slowly. But if you do not succeed with the internal manipulation, then place the woman on a table and lift her feet up, and then tilt the table towards the ground; and the woman should be held to prevent her from falling during the thrusting.
And if the hands are not returned and the fetus dies, then cut them open and extract the remains of the fetus or tie the body to the patient's hand with cloth and extract it carefully, for that will be expelled.
When the fetus is on its feet, you should push it upwards, then gently turn the fetus, then grasp it, then take one of its feet and extend it slowly.
But when it has come out between the thighs, then you tell it to strain, and make it cough with care, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have described, then you return the fetus slowly until you place it in its natural position, for it will then be expelled with ease.
And if it is still not expelled by all that we have described, then take marshmallow and dissolved gum, and pound it thoroughly in a mortar, then apply it to the lower part of the woman's abdomen, the lower part of her belly. And then apply this poultice to warm water, until the hypochondria (lower ribs/side area) are affected.
And when the parts below it are soothed, then give the patient a fumigation of myrtle, and apply it to them. And when the fumigation has lasted for an hour, then you place it on a bedpan, and make it cough and hold its mouth, and remove it, and gently compress the lower part of its abdomen, for the fetus will soon be expelled.
The fetus comes out lengthwise with one of its hands. If the reduction of the hand is successful, but if you cannot reduce it, then lift the woman and make her move.
And if moving does not work, then have her lie down on the bed, and forcefully compress her thighs, and then administer remedies which I have previously described made of mucilages.
And if labor has begun, then have her sit on a stool, and then carefully attempt the reduction of the hand, drawing the fetus into its natural position, and make the woman sneeze and tell her to push until it is expelled.
The fetus is now lying on its back, with its arms outstretched and its face towards its mother's side. The midwife should hold the child's hand and then gently and gradually turn it, helping it with ease.
And when it is placed correctly, then apply to its head what we have described, consisting of oil and mucilages.
Then instruct him to breathe in small, shallow breaths, and when that is done, have her sit on a stool, and instruct her to lean over to her right side, and then instruct her to push; and make her sneeze with appropriate instructions. The prepared fetus is positioned on its side.
When the mother's labor is over in this way, carefully consider, and then you cut the afterbirth with your fingernail until all the moisture is expelled.
And if the fetus is suspended by its face and neck, then cut it at the navel, so that it is not held back and dies.
Then push the fetus into the parts within until you place it in the proper position, and tell the woman to bear down a few times, and it will be expelled easily, if God wills it.
The birth of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are born quite often, and sometimes three or four, but this is rare and seldom happens; however, such things are contrary to nature, and are not alive.
And count those, whether they are numerous, as second children, and separate them by section, distinguishing them by the ligament at the navel of each.  And sometimes they are expelled like a single fetus by a gentle, natural means, and sometimes by an unnatural means, as we have recounted.
And if some of them are difficult to deliver, and reach the point we have discussed regarding unnatural births, then treat them as we have described.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and subtlety, and with diligence, and is ingenious according to every figure, as it is prepared by ingenious means leading to health.
A recollection of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or even ten are formed in the mother's womb.  I have verified that one woman miscarried six, and another ten, all formed, the cause of which was determined by the learned sages' measurements.
And all of this should be part of the midwife's knowledge, for it is easily apparent to one familiar with this, and thus one should address it with what is appropriate.
Chapter 76.
Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat the dead fetus with the methods we have described in the division of medicine, and it has not yet emerged, and you are forced to use manual procedures, then you must consider whether the woman is of healthy constitution and free from any of the conditions that might endanger her life.
Then the woman should be positioned on her back, with her head lowered and her thighs raised.
And the attendant should hold her (the woman) in place, either by holding her in two places or by securing her to the bed, so that her body does not move during the extraction of the fetus.
And then moisten the mother's mouth with moist oils, and with mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
Then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where she can hook her fingernails onto the fetus.
And suitable places for this:  then consider if the fetus is prepared to descend from its head, and then you hook your fingers onto its brow/forehead, or the neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or in the fork of the throat, or in the appropriate areas of the ribs, or below the lower ribs.
But if the descent is toward the feet, then in the groin, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary to hold the fingernail with the right hand, and place the curve of it in that spot which is between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and hook the fingernail into one of those places we mentioned, until it reaches the empty space/cavity.
And then place and hook another fingernail, or a third if necessary, so that there is a pulling inward, and not an outward extension according to its nature alone.
And it should be moved in all directions, so that its expulsion is eased, just as is done when a tooth is removed.
And it is necessary in that place between them that the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife anoint some of her fingers with oil and insert those fingers and move what is retained.
Therefore, if the part of the fet is pushed out, then the fingernail(s) should be moved to the next spot that is further in/up; and do this until the entire fet is pushed out.
But if the hand is protruding before the other, and it is not possible to return it by squeezing it, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then pull it back until it is completely expelled, and cut it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus it is necessary that you do the same for the other hand and for the two feet.
But if the head of the fet is large, or squeezing it causes pain, or water is collected at its head, then it is necessary to insert fingers between the bony spaces, cut open the head and drain the water, or to cut it open with an instrument called "alundat", the shape of which will be found among the other instruments in the chapter following this one.
And similarly, do the same if the fet is naturally large-headed: then it is necessary to cut it with a scalpel, or to remove it with a "alunda" as we said; then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head has protruded and is squeezed towards the fork, then it should be cut open until the moisture in the foot is discharged.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it cause pain, then you cut the head as soon as possible.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is abscessed or hydrops, then you must pierce it until the moisture within is drained.
But if the fet is upon its feet, then the pulling of it is gentle and its direction towards the mother's mouth is gentle.
But if it is squeezed towards the belly and foot, then you must extract it with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and cut open the belly and foot until that which is within them is drained.
But if the other limbs are removed and the head remains, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's mouth is open, then she inserts her hand into the air passage and seeks the head with it, and pulls it with her fingers towards the mother's mouth, and then inserts one or two fingers into it and pulls it with those.
And if the mother's mouth is blocked by a burning abscess that has occurred there, then it should not be disturbed; instead, a flushing with moist, greasy substances should be administered first. And apply poultices; and keep the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetuses is on its side, then if possible, it should be turned; and that which we have said regarding a living fetus should be administered.
And if this is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you leave no cause within the second layer (of the womb or the body).
And when you have completed all your operation, then administer that which we have said about the treatment of boils which arise from the mother (i.e., the mother's body).
And if a flow of blood occurs, then you should apply astringent waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman whose womb contained a dead fetus, and then, again, became pregnant, and another dead fetus was the result. A long time ago, a boil occurred on his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to treat him, and medicated him for a long time, but the wound did not heal.
Then I placed some strong-drawing plasters on him, and a bone came out, and after a few days, another bone came out; and I was greatly astonished, as there are no bones in the belly.
And then I thought they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I treated the wound, and removed many bones; the woman, being in her usual state, and having lived in this way for a long time, discharged a little pus.
And I did not consider this strange, but rather that it is knowledge and a help to what the skilled physician administers with his hand in treatment.
Of the forms of the instruments needed to extract the fetus.
Form of the twisting with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of constriction in which the books are tied, in which constriction are two twists at the two ends of two cords. However, this twist should be more subtle than a constricting band, and be made either of linen or of wood.
And the width of each cord should be about two fingers and the margin of each about one finger, and the length of each is one and a half palms; and in the middle of the two cords are two fastenings of the same cord, already fastened, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the mother so that it may be opened by them when you revolve the two prescribed twists.
Another form similar to this one, more subtle and lighter:
It should be made of linen or wood, resembling forceps. However, at the end of it are two additions like this, and the length of those additions is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you wish to open the mother with them, then you have the woman lie on the bed with her feet hanging down, opening what is between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument like you would with scissors, in the amount you want, based on the opening of the mother's mouth, until the midwife does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
A method of pushing with which the fetus is pushed:
A method of applying pressure/support with which the large head is contained.
And it is similar to forceps, which have teeth at the end, as often happens, and sometimes it is made long like forceps, in this way, as often happens, with teeth like saw teeth with which it is incised and contained. A method of gentle pressure/support.
A method of using a hook with a spine/point.
A method of using a hook with two spines/points.
A method of a spatula with two extremities, with which the head is severed/cut off.
A method of two wide spatulas.
All those instruments are used, and the more the species of them are multiplied and prepared by the craftsmen, the easier their operation becomes and the more valuable they are to men due to their quantity.
And do not neglect those instruments, wherever they may be, and whatever you have at your disposal, for the necessity of them cannot be excused.
Of the extraction of the second/secondary thing.  (or "second part")
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should instruct the woman to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze with a stimulus, and firmly press your hand over her mouth and nostrils.  If by this it is expelled, it is good.
And if not, then you take a bowl, pierce a hole in the cover, and place in it herbs, opening the mother's mouth [or, placing in her mouth] such as camomile, rue, anise, chamomile, Arabic cicadas, cassia wood, and centaury. All of these herbs, or some of them, should be immersed in water in the bowl, and place that on the fire.
Then place a reed cannula in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's cavity.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it hasn't been expelled, then you order the midwife to immerse her left hand in sesame oil or in a mixture of marshmallow, and then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it.  And once she has found it, take it and gently pull it out bit by bit.  But if it is attached to the mother's womb, then insert her hand as we have described, and when she finds the afterbirth, remove it slowly and gradually, so that the mother does not suffer from a violent pull. Beforehand, it should be gently moved from side to side.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted, we have already described the treatment with sneezing and with the herbal oil medication.
But if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then be careful not to tire yourself out by further attempts at extraction.  Instead, whatever remains in the mother's mouth, put it on the woman's thigh, and then treat it with an ointment and a four-ingredient preparation. This will cause it to decompose over a few days, dissolve, and be expelled.
But when it decomposes, a foul odor rises to the head and stomach, and this afflicts the patient.  Then, fumigation and appropriate remedies must be administered.
However, some of the ancients already tested fumigation with dried nettles and figs.
Form of instrument with which the woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and the afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or earthenware.
And the slender end should be placed in the vessel, and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation should be placed above the flames of the fire.
And let the one who holds it ensure that the fumigation is consumed and replace it with another.
Of the treatment of the unperforated anus of the child.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which are then narrow and obstructed. Therefore, the midwife should perforate that constriction with her finger; and if not, she should perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the sphincter.
And then place upon it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wants to swallow.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a plague or abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then treated with what we have said about the treatment and the use of lead.
Of the treatment of fistulas that come from below.
Fistulas that develop in the lower, more posterior region, are swollen and large, and develop either near the anus externally or within the space of the lower region.
And it is a fistula or abscess.
And when this swelling is longstanding and comes from watery white moisture or subtle putrefaction, and sometimes these fistulas are even penetrating.
And those that are penetrating are known by the discharge and windiness that comes from them; and sometimes the patient experiences discharge, and often worms come out of them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the bladder, and into the urethra; and some of these penetrating ones reach the joint of the thigh and the area of the buttock.
And the way to distinguish a penetrating fistula from a non-penetrating one in the anus is to insert your index finger into the anus, and subtly insert a thin instrument of bronze or iron into the fistula if there is no twisting.
But if there is twisting in that area, then insert a thin instrument of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the instrument or hair against your finger.
And if you do not feel any of that, nor does any discharge, worms, or windiness come from the opening, as we have said, then know it is non-penetrating, and then focus on the treatment.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the urethra, the sign of that is urine from it, and prohibiting it until the area is healed with medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the thigh or into the bone, and pain arises in the surrounding area, the sign of it is that the probe penetrates, if there is no twisting within the bone, and pain arises in the surrounding part; and that it will likely persist; and that no treatment, nor ointment, will be effective in that case.
And for all those penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the treatment of those is the work and vanity of unskilled physicians, upon which you should not rely.
But for that which is not penetrating and not old, healing by cauterization is expected, as I have told you, and which I have already tested.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on their back, and lift their legs upwards, their thighs bent towards their stomach, and then introduce a lead probe, or an iron one if there is a twist in the fistula, until you know the spot where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels pain around the anus, then you should introduce your finger into the anus.
And if, when you feel the probe already penetrating on your finger, you feel something between your finger and that tissue or flesh, then know for certain that it is penetrating; and therefore do not work on it, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
But however, some might say that it heals in some men slowly.
And the treatment through which one expects improvement in them is that you should heat a fine cautery according to the fistula's width, as previously described, and introduce that heated cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those tissues that are joined and fistula-like, similar to a bird's feather quill, are burned.
And then treat that with some dressings until all the burned tissues are healed, and then treat it with medicines that generate and strengthen flesh, and if it is healed, it is good; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate your finger that is in the anus, and between that and the probe is a thick layer of flesh or tissue, and the fistula opens where the surface of the rectum is, then cauterize the rectum from the beginning of the fistula, and you must cauterize with the probe, and hold it in the fistula until the cauterization reaches the place where the tip of the probe is, and by the probe it is liberated and extracted.
And then cleanse those fistula-like tissues, which are like a bird's feather quill, and do not leave any cause of them.
However, if bleeding occurs between you and your procedure when you burn those tissues, then the best and easiest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire has two good uses:  it burns those joined tissues, and it cuts off the blood and dries the moisture.
And then treat that with dressings made of lint or a crushed cloth until the wound is closed, and then treat the sore with ointments that generate and strengthen tough flesh.
And those medicines, which are astringent and drying; when this is restored, it is already healed and you need not fear a recurrence.
But if the fistula has already pierced through to the anus and is extending from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and probe with it.
And therefore, if you feel a resistance and between you and it is a membrane or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no surgery to be done there but rather to proceed with a method of trust and hope.
Which case can be treated in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or cut it until you reach its cavity, so you can insert medicaments and achieve a cure; and do not proceed too much with cutting, so as not to injure the surrounding flesh, causing the patient unnecessary suffering, and then treat it with what we have said; for it will be easily healed; or separate that membrane and remove the pus that dries the flesh towards the anus, as we have said, with a probe or other instrument with a sharp end; then cleanse that fistulous flesh that is in the cut until you can apply a scab; and then strive to heal the entire wound with a cut, and if the outer opening is not healed, and the fistula remains open in the area of the anus, the patient will get better more easily. And sometimes the fistula is resolved in this manner: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the folds of the anus near the surface of the body, along with the crease and at the edge of the anus, then take a probe with a pointed end, which is like a needle prepared for making incisions in this way:
and insert it into that [fistula] wrapped in five [threads] or around them; and then insert the probed thread into the fistula until it reaches its cavity.
But if it penetrates the edge of the anus, close to the interior, then you remove the thread from that hole as if you were inserting your finger into the anus; and you extract the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each, and leave it for a day or two, and whenever you cut the thread into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh is cut between the two ends of the thread and it falls out.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and cured.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in any way you are prepared, but not too deeply into the flesh, for it is not your job to do so lest you cut the muscle; and then treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the probe with which the fistula is opened:  its curve is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so as not to cut what is unnecessary, as sometimes happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids in which blood flows in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds, for either they are between the buttocks, resembling a bunch of grapes, or they are like bunches of raisins. And they are small or large, and the blood flows from them constantly, and are single or multiple, and are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the degree of little moisture, from which flows yellowish water or a little blood with a continuous consistency, and according to the color of the consistency.
And the treatment for those inside the anus is to instruct the patient to sit and press until the anus opens and the hemorrhoids appear before you. Then you grasp them and suspend them with tweezers or with your nails, and then cut them off at their roots.
However, if the tweezers are not holding them due to their moisture and softening, then take them with a rough cloth or cotton or wool bandage, and pull them out with your fingers, and then cut them off and sprinkle some warm powders over the cuts, so that these powders act in place of cauterization; or cauterize them as it is preceded in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not open, then cauterize the patient with a cautery that has a small bite, and wash with that which is in the anus, and have it open slightly when the patient is straining.
However, if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then the cause is mild, and you should grasp them with your finger or tweezers, cut them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And for those that you have cut, you should apply sutures to them in this manner: take a linen thread and thread it onto a needle.  Then, pull the hemorrhoids to the outward edges, and have the needle penetrate the base on the opposite side. Tie the two ends of the thread to the part below the needle, in a horizontal manner. Tightly draw the hemorrhoid together with the suture, and then knot the thread and remove the needle.
Do this to all of them, and leave one of them unsewn, so that the excess blood can drain from that one.
Then place a cloth soaked in warmed oil or a plaster over the anus and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointments or other remedies until it is healed.
And if they are old and do not respond to that treatment, then you should scrape them with the point of a spatula or your fingernail until they are moist and the crust of pus above them, which prevents their consolidation, is removed; then treat them until they are healed.
Then you should scrape them with a more forceful scraping than the first until much blood is drawn and they are raw; then treat them until they are healed.
Know that ulcers, this is key, are not anything but a round, reddish spot on the body, resembling a little nail head, and they occur throughout the body, especially below the feet and in the joints, and they are often associated with pain in that area.
Then it is necessary to cut around the ulcer, holding it with forceps or tweezers, and remove it from its root; and then treat that area.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is prescribed in its chapter.
However, a wart is a small, rough, fistulous, round elevation on the surface of the body, and often appears on the hands of children.
And the treatment for it is to scrape and cut it, or to squeeze it with a file or with a needle until it bursts. And if you wish, cauterize this, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
However, a pimple is a small, pustular or fistulous, large elevation on the surface of the body, often found on the groin, and when the cold touches it, a sensation like an ant bite is felt around the body.
And the cure for it is to take a hollow tube of an eagle's or hawk's feather, and it should be strong. Place that over the pimple until it contains the circle of the pimple's parts; then, turn your hand with the tube until you cut what is within the pimple's circle, and place it on the groin of the flesh, and let the pimple be eradicated from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a tube of bronze or iron according to this method:
Let the part above the tube be thin, closed (that is, unperforated), wrapped so that the grasp of it on the skin is smoothed and its rotation is facilitated.
And if you wish to cauterize the pimple, do so as previously described in the cauterization of a boil in the chapter on cauterization.
Concerning the instruments that are used in giving treatment.
Sometimes treatment is done with silver or brass or cast or hammered bronze; and sometimes it is done with small and large instruments, depending on the patient's condition, for the treatment of young children requires smaller instruments; and for those with sore or painful bottoms, the treatment instruments should be small.
The form of large and small treatments:
The head of the cannula above it, to which the pouch is attached, should be wide in this manner; and it should have a distinct attachment point where the pouch is attached, just as before.
And the lower end, which is inserted into the anus, should be tightly closed (that is, not open) very narrowly, tapering to a point; and on one side of it there should be two openings, and on the other side one opening, and the size of the openings should be according to the thickness of the rays and a little larger.
And the pouch, in which the bladder medicines or sheepskin are placed, should be a palm in length.
And it should be pierced with many holes, just as it is rolled; and if the space between the holes is the thickness of the finger.
And then insert strong, crude (or raw) bile, about ten ounces or thereabouts, into those holes; and attach the pouch to that; and when the pouch is placed in the proper location, tie the top of the applicator tightly between two distinct points, with strong constriction, and then seal each hole with that.  However, the manner of sealing is that you fill the pouch with the medicine, which should be heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
And then the patient should lie on their back, raise their legs up, and place a blanket or linen underneath them; and then anoint the end of the cautery with oil or egg white or mucilage of fenugreek or similar; and then insert that into the anus with ease, and then the attendant should take the pouch and squeeze it with both hands forcefully until everything in the applicator has been emptied into the intestines; and then remove the applicator, and the patient lowers their legs to the floor.
And if the medicine causes a response, hold it in place for as long as possible; and if the patient sleeps with it all night, it is better in terms of recovery.
However, if you use this cautery for diseases of the bowels and their expulsion, repeat the cautery until healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or festering sores in the anus, the applicator should be very thin and gentle, like the applicators used for children.
Concerning the treatment of plagues.
I have already stated in the division of plague treatment what was appropriate for the book, and I now say that the treatment of those plagues is by hand and by medicine according to the condition.
Therefore, I say that plagues are diversified according to how the plagues are caused and according to the location where the plagues appear.
And the causes by which plagues are often caused are like the throwing of a stone, or the cut of a knife or sword, or a blow with a lance, or a stick, or an arrow, or similar things from many causes.
But the plagues that are in the parts of the body are like a plague that falls on the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the liver, and similar parts of the limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of certain plagues, so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the recollection of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple plague occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone throw or similar, and has only slightly cracked the skull, and is a significant wound, and you fear the onset of a hot abscess in that patient, then you should quickly bleed from the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and draw from him a quantity of blood according to the strength of the patient, unless some cause prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood that has come from the wound is little.
And let the bloodletting be on the opposite side of the wounded part; and keep the patient from repletion and from rich foods and drinks.
But if any reason prevents bloodletting, then the diet should be reduced, and he should avoid meat and wine.
And if an abscess develops on the wound, apply a poultice of roasted olive oil or wine in which a styptic has been dissolved.
But if you are sure of a hot abscess, then apply to the wound, if it is recent, its own blood, and if the air hasn't altered the powder that I will mention shortly.
And if the air has already altered it, then apply some ointments until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or from a cut or similar injury, and its edges are not brought together by flaps, then bring them together with sutures, as I will explain about their stitching.
But if the wound has already exposed the bone and tendon, and there is no retention but only a small suspension, then cut it, and then treat the wound with medicines and rebuild the flesh in a suitable place, and command the patient to eat foods rich in substance: heads and whole pieces of meat and similar, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the flesh that does not adhere to the bone, then you cut away all the putrid material and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood may flow, and it is not severed by medicines, then you seek out the artery, and if you find it unsevered, then you cut it with a scalpel, or tie it off; and if necessity compels you that what we have said is not sufficient, then you cauterize it until the blood flow is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is slight; therefore you can powder them with this powder, for it will consolidate them before the air alters them.
And the method for the powder is this: take one part of oil of linseed, two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of unquenched causs (likely a type of burning material/ash).  Grind it all finely and sift it, then fill the wound with it, and bind it tightly until it adheres well and is coagulated by the blood.
And then bind it over with dressings and leave it, and do not unbind it until it is suppurated, for the medicine is not eradicated from it until it is healed.
And sometimes this alone suffices, when you do not have linseed and dragon's blood at hand.
And you perform this same procedure on large wounds when it is appropriate to apply it, and they are tightly bound, and on fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially in summer, then this powder is not the intended treatment, but rather treat them with what we have said, or apply digestive ointments, or a poultice of barley flour mixed with water and honey until it forms a crust. Then treat them with the other remedies until they are healed.
But if the wound is accompanied by a fractured bone, and it is small, then you remove it with a dressing.
And I will describe the treatment for head fractures in what follows.
Of wounds to the neck.
But if the wound occurs on the neck, then there is no difference in treatment between a wound on the neck and a simple head wound.
But if the nerve or artery of the neck is cut, then if it is a nerve, it should not be treated with anything that causes harsh astringency, like zinc and verdigris (which is vitriol and similar to them), for they harm the nerves and constrict them. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, for the substance of the nerves is cold, and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if this is treated with mild medicines, such as washing with clean water, sometimes infused in oiled or un-oiled herbs, and salves when washed with clean water, and other softening stones according to this method, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or constrict its edges, and then treat it with powders until healed.
But if the wound is deep or the injury has reached the lowermost part of the bone, then perforate it at the deepest point.
But if an artery is already cut in the wound and blood is flowing, then separate or stop the flow, or tie it off, or cauterize it if the need arises.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or the whole spine have been cut, and the veins of the neck are spared, then you should close the edges of the wound with sutures along the neck, and do not touch it. First, align it and return it to its natural shape, then hold it with a suitable splint, and leave it for a few days. Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and healing properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has already affected the bone, then you should treat it, and if there are bone fragments, remove all you can; and those you cannot, leave them until the wound becomes pus-filled, and the discharge is lessened.
And if the wound is recent, then powder the area with the powder that you have been recommended. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with whatever the recollection brings forth in its chapter.
If it's a wound from a spear or a knife, and sometimes the depth of the wound, then consider further if there is airiness from the wound as the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if there is no depth to the wound, and the wound is recent with its own blood, then in the first hour you put powder on it, and do not press it so that the blood is not retained in the depth of it and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but put an attractive ointment on it, and apply it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not present, then you place a piece of old cloth over the wound opening to absorb the moisture; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that the accumulated material drains out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound, and the patient shows no spasms, tremors, or bad heart conditions, or constrictions of the windpipe, and the other bodily functions are good, then know that the wound is salvageable; and then treat it with poultices and other remedies until it is healed.
But if healing is impossible, and the wound is always open, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore treat it according to its own specific treatment.
And if the wound is from a cut with a knife or sword, and it is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it with sutures if it is large, or with powder if it is small.
But if the wound has already penetrated the bone, and pieces of bone are now exposed, then you should clean the wound and try to remove those pieces if they are loose. But if they are not loose, then leave them until the wound can heal, for removing them would be harmful.
And for other wounds that occur on other limbs, the method of treatment is the same as what we have described.
Of wounds of the belly.
The ailment which occurs in the belly is either severe, mild, or moderate.
A small rupture is difficult to repair in another way, and the reduction of them is more difficult if you don't quickly return the protrusion to its place, because it will widen and become larger, making its return difficult.
Therefore, the best rupture is moderate, because in that case the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, unlike the two types previously mentioned.
And know that the suturing of the belly is according to four methods, but two of these sutures are common and suitable for suturing other parts of the body.
And two other sutures are specific to the other parts of the body, but nonetheless more specific to suturing the belly.
And I will explain each to you with explanation and declaration.
Therefore, I say that when the rupture is small and some part of the intestine has come out through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either due to the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is the case, it is necessary that you warm it, either by submerging a damp sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or by soaking it in water with cumin, cypress, and spike, and apply that to the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes, this is done with vinegar, which has astringent properties, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And when you see the inflammation resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and marsh-mallow have been boiled, for this alleviates the internal discomfort with little effort.
But if the internal discomfort is relieved by this treatment, then gently enlarge the rupture with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is done properly and without incision, and there should be no attempt to dry it out when it is needed and weakens.
Concerning the instrument, consider its shape:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp, and the tip is fine, but not as fine as a needle; rather, it is slightly blunt.
And so, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine is outside, then its return should be according to its natural form, back into its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is wide, and it is in the lower part of the belly, then you must have the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you should raise the head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the belly, then always keep your attention and consideration on this, so that the part where the wound is may be higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, place what you have prepared, and then have the attendant hold the rupture completely with their hand and bring the edges together, and then examine whether there is some cause for further sutures.
And this is the common method of suture: one takes the needle, or several needles according to the size of the wound; and then place it on the edge of the rupture with the ring finger, and hold the needle firmly so that you do not push the two ends of the thread into the two ends of the bandage underneath the threads in the affected area, until you have pushed the needle from that side; and you have already joined the two ends of the thread and the two ends of the bandage, and you have made four stitches.
And then tighten with double thread around the needle, and sometimes from both sides together, and then bring the two edges and the wound together properly; and then leave the thickness of a finger and place another needle in it, and then tie it with thread like the first needle; and do not stop doing this if necessary with the needles until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And the needles should be of medium thickness, neither too thin nor too thick, because thin needles easily pierce the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to thread, so for this reason they should be of medium thinness and thickness.
And it is not appropriate to stick the needle into both ends of the thread too close together, so that the flesh is not pierced easily and that the wound bursts before it heals.
And do not make the stitches too long, so that the wound is hindered from healing.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not bother the patient during their rest; and you will place expanded powders on the stitches, all around, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is already healed.
And this type of stitching with needles is most suitable for small wounds, for in stitching them, only one or two needles, or thereabouts, are needed.
However, the second stitch is common: and it is that you sew the four ends of the thread and the two ends of the chip (or strip), all at once, with a needle which should be equally wrapped in a subtle and robust way.
And then, when you have pierced these four ends with the needle, pull the needle back from the same point where you began, so that the thread retracts from the most superior part of the wound, so that the stitching is like a woven cloth, as if the cloths are woven together tightly.
And place between each stitch the thickness of the little finger.
And this stitching is known (or, understood) by men.
And with such stitching, I sutured the wound that had occurred to a man in his abdomen, who had been wounded with a knife.  The wound was a gaping tear over a palm’s breadth, and the intestines had protruded about two palms from the middle of the wound and were exposed halfway down the limb.  And so, I restored it after the intestines had been outside the wound for 24 hours.
And the wound was consolidated within 15 days or so, and I healed him. He recovered; and afterward, he lived for many years, and was restored to his former abilities.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not recover; and by a miracle it was, for I did not heal him with ointment, for it was in a place where no ointment or other medicine was to be found, but I applied old wool to the wound twice a day and continuously washed it with honey water until he was healed.
But I will describe two kinds of stitches, proper according to the narration of Scripture.
And it is that you begin with a stitch of the needle, and pass the needle from the outside to the inside.
And thus, as the needle pierces the seam and the same laceration, according to its length and the entire wound, and leaves the end of the thread where you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate the other end from the inside to the outside at the other end of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make that penetration again in that same end of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is in this stitch, and make the needle penetrate the other end from the inside to the outside.
And penetrate with that your penetration, with which you make that penetration into the thread at the end of the wound which is on its side until all of it penetrates.
And then, starting from this same side, stitch it with the end of the thread on the other side, and remove the needle from the seam which it pierced, and then return it to that seam and stitch the end of the thread that is on the other side to this end of the wound; and remove that from the seam that is on its side.
And do this once and again, and do this once more again until you have stitched all that which is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this method of suture is that you stitch with a skin stitch, with which you sew skins, so that you stitch the thread once from the edge of the seam, and that you leave it again until you have completed it.
However, the second specific type of suture, which is called "Gualia," and which is reported by him and for him, is that you stitch it according to a semi-circular method with which some physicians stitch it, so that they bring together each part to its corresponding part on its appropriate side naturally; and they apply the end of one and the end of the wound to the other end of it, and this is done as I have explained to you.
You should insert the needle into the end of the wound close to the outside part, and have it penetrate the inside part of that wound, and leave two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and have it penetrate from the outside to the inside at two ends of the thread.
And then you turn each of these, and have each penetrate from the inside part to the outside part at two other ends of the wound, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common, superficial suture, and is a suture of which a previous reminder was given.
This is the method of Gualia.
And further, some might say, the suture of the abdomen is rectified with two needles, meaning that you thread a thread onto each, and begin by inserting the needle you have in hand, and have it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who have similarly known how to treat skin.
And know that when a rupture is in the middle part of the abdomen, then the suture of it is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen.
But the judgment of the treatment of plague is the judgment of other plagues.
Which means that when you obtain the plague fresh with its blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestine and suture it and treat it appropriately, then apply the flesh-healing powder to it.
And if the air has already altered the plague, then apply some ointments that are at your disposal until they form a hard stone and the threads fall out, and are consolidated, firm, and healed; and then treat it as the treatment of other plagues until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines available, then apply it immediately, as soon as it begins to cast off pus, and change it twice a day, as taught to you, until it is healed, for you do not require other medication in many cases when the plagues are simple.
But if you fear that the plague might spread to the major limbs, then you should soak linen in tempered olive oil, or rubbed olive oil, and place it around the affected areas, between the armpits and below the elbows.
And if there is pain or putrefaction in their intestines, which often happens, then cauterize it with black astringent wine, especially if the ulcer has reached the intestine and has created a penetrating exit into its cavity.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is easier to heal than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal, for the intestine called "jejunum" does not readily receive healing from a plague that strikes it; and this is due to the large multitude of veins within it, and the magnitude of its size, and the subtlety of its body, and because of its proximity to the nature of the nerves.
But if the plague's affliction is the tissue, and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your technique for reducing the intestines likewise.
But if it has spread to the surrounding area and is already green or black, then you should apply a ligature over the discolored area, so that blood flows, for there are veins and arteries in the tissue.
Then cut what is beyond that ligature, and hang the two ends of the ligature from the part below the plague outside, so that the pull of the affliction from the wound in the tissue and the emission of the plague's arm is relieved.
Remembering the afflictions when they occur in the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small, it is possible for it to be restored in some people. For this, I saw one who was afflicted in their belly by a spear wound; the affliction was on the right side of the stomach, and the plague was old and formed a fistula, and expulsion of gases and wind arose from it.
Then I considered the cure, knowing that it was not focused on healing, but I focused on soothing the inside so that it could heal, and the area could consolidate.
And I, having seen the area now consolidated, told the patient that an affliction might come upon them in their belly; but no such affliction occurred.  They remained in their usual state, eating and drinking, and then went into a bath and exercised as they had been doing.
And some experienced people have said that when an affliction occurs in the intestine, and it is small, it should be bound in this way: you should take large-headed leeches.  Then, bring together the two edges of the wound and place a leech with an open mouth over the two edges of the wound. When the leech has taken hold and tightly closed its mouth, cut off its head, because it adheres and does not detach.
Then place another leech near the first, and do not stop doing this with leeches, one after another, according to the size of the entire wound.
Then reduce the swelling and compress the wound; and those leech heads remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm comes to the patient.
And it is possible that the intestine be bound with a fine thread made from an attached animal's intestine, after it has been put on a needle; that is, take the end of this intestinal thread, which is very thin, and bind the end of it to the thread, wrapping it around, then insert the thread into needles and apply it to the intestine and then close up the rupture with this.
This method of suturing with leeches is only according to a path of trust and hope.
However, if the wound is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the delicate intestines, because then there is only a refinement, and not a total healing of it.
Of the treatment of ulcers and fistulas.
Know that every wound and abscess, when it grows old and becomes chronic, and becomes an ulcer, and is not healed and discharges pus constantly and is not excised, then, in any limb, universally is called a fistula and we call it that ulcer.
And a fistula, in truth, is a hard, white, fibrous knot, with no pain, and a cavity like the cavity of a bird's nest; and therefore that ulcer is called a *rizatz* (or *rizzat*), that is, a knot.
And in some people it is moist, which discharges pus; and the moisture is easily excised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes thin, and sometimes thick, and sometimes thin.
I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of them, and their treatment with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary that I make you aware of the treatment for a fistula that is made by surgical means.
And know that fistulas occur in all parts of the body; for among those are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the spine, or the intestines, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the articulated joints, such as the wrist and ankle; and among these are those that are close to the main part of the limb and similar ones.
Therefore, among these fistulas that are of this type, are among the difficult-to-heal illnesses, and they do not receive healing, and effort with that is toil and ignorance.
And if it is one of the fistulas whose depth does not reach the places I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its healing, and wish to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron or steel, and if the fistula goes in a straight path, then you will search for it with it.
But if the fistula is winding, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, with the lightness of its body, is suitable and easily follows the winding path.
And if the fistula has many roughnesses, it is not possible for you to mark it with a probe.
Then irrigate the openings with its openings, for the moisture with which you irrigate it flows towards the other openings and runs through them.
And then carefully investigate, according to what method you can determine if there are bones or nerves there, and if the cavity of the fistula is distant or close, or if it is a fistula with many openings, and strive with all your strength, with your questioning, asking the patient, and finding the source of the pain through your pressure with your hand on the area, and similar signs and causes, like an abscess.
And when you have considered all these issues with true observation, then proceed to the treatment with consideration:  consider whether the fistula is a superficial fistula near the surface, or in a salty place, or distant from a joint, or a nerve, or artery, or vein, or any of the locations that I have mentioned to you.
Then, dry the fistula as previously described, removing any filtering material and corrupted flesh, and anything that has grown within the opening of the flesh, or from the membrane or similar things, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of a deep location, and it is deemed appropriate, then it is necessary to incise it as deeply as possible, and then clean it of all its corrupted flesh; and then apply some wraps with sharp medicinal agents and insert them into the cavity of the fistula, which you have opened with an instrument.
And do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine dissolves everything that remains in the cavity of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then cauterize it according to what we have described.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, then you must investigate it and cauterize it, as we have described, if you do not find any reason for a vein, nerve, or main limb to be the cause, as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed and there is any corruption or blackness on it, then you should scrape that until all corruption is removed; and then treat it with what causes flesh to grow until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and still discharges pus as before, then understand that you have not achieved the complete eradication of the corruption; and then you must once again carefully expose it, thoroughly scrape and clean it with your strongest tools, and then restore it.
And if it is healed, well and good; but if not, then understand that the corruption is located deep within the body, which cannot be reached, and therefore, there is no remedy other than to leave it be.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to extract it, then you should extract it with delicate forceps suitable for this purpose.
And if there are many bones, then carefully remove all of them, and do not leave any part behind using your strength.
However, if you encounter any difficulty due to the humors, and there is no fear or error in their incision, then cut those ligaments or flesh if they are present, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at once, then place over the wound something that can protect it, and leave it until the flesh and ligaments around those bones are softened, and ensure that the wound does not heal, and that the incision you made in your operation remains constricted.
Furthermore, if you fear this, place on it cotton soaked in crushed olive oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, for when those ligaments soften, and the bones are separated from them, the extraction will be easier.
However, if it is a large bone, such as a bone of the leg or thigh or similar to those, and only the surface of it is corrupted, then cut that with a rasping instrument only on the back until that blackness and corruption are removed, and then repair the wound.
But if the part of the bone that is corrupted is large, and the corruption has already reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to cut around that and completely incise to the point where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it heals.
And I will now relate to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use this as an example and aid in your own operation.
There was a young man, about 30 years old, who experienced pain in his thigh, and the affliction spread from the interior of the body until the matter reached the thigh, and it was a matter of an abscess, with a large abscess, and it was not an external cause.
And then the condition was prolonged by the error of the physicians until the abscess opened, and many materials were discharged, and his healing was nearly halted until the leg became fistulous, and many openings appeared which all discharged pus and bodily humors.
And then he was treated by many physicians for about two years, and there was no skill or mastery in their treatment, until I was called in.
And I saw his leg and the materials flowing from those openings, in large amounts; and the body of that man was already emaciated, and its color was yellowish.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those openings and the probe reached the bone.
And then I carefully examined all the openings and found them extending to various parts of the leg.
And then I probed and incised one of those openings until I exposed a part of the bone, and found it decayed, already earthy and corrupted and blackened; and a portion of the leg was already corrupted, having penetrated to the marrow.
Then I closed what I had discovered and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I judged that it wasn't the bone itself, but that corruption which I had cut away and closed, and I had already drained it.
And then I applied restorative medicine to the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.
And then I again probed and uncovered the bone, a second time over the first discovery, and found continuing corruption with the bone.
And I closed what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I was forced to perform its cure and restoration, but it was not restored.
And then I discovered it again and did not cease to cut the bone little by little, specifically, forcing its restoration, but it was not restored until I cut around a palm's width of it, and extracted its marrow, and then restored it with medicines, and it was slightly consolidated and healed.
And no further repetition of the operation and section of it is necessary except for the weakness of it, and the small tolerance and fear of death, for it suffered at all times from a bad syncope due to excessive evacuation.
And thus it is healed completely, and nothing is in place of the bone but hard flesh; and its disposition in its body is good, and its virtue returned; and it is converted to its original state, and it does not present opposition to walking, or harm in any way.
But if the bone is prominent in a place already injured, then you must cut it in this manner: you must take a ligament and tie it to the end of the prominent bone, and order someone to pull on it; and place a thicker wool ligament over the first ligament; and then tie it over the flesh below the bone, and pull the end so you pull the flesh apart, and expose the flesh over the area you wish to cut, so that the cut does not damage the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or a board under the bone properly, for when you do this, the cut is not prohibited from not cutting the corrupted flesh.
And the cutting should be done slowly over the affected area, because if the corruption is inside the bone's cavity and does not appear on its surface to the senses, then the cutting needs to be repeated.
And if the bone is corrupted but not protruding, where one part is joined to the other, and the corruption is in the middle or a part of it, then you must expose all the flesh around; and then place wood underneath, and cut from one side where the corruption is until the cut continues to the other side.
And the cut should be made along the area of the corruption, a little at a time, as we said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it connects to the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then it is not a logical/reasonable (or expected) location but rather an unexpected/abnormal one.
And if the corruption is in the flesh of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, you must investigate the corruption in whatever way appears to you, and expose it, and clean it according to every possible arrangement, and with each attempt (or device)  try to correct it, as long as it doesn't damage any vein or nerve.
And know that the instruments for cutting and sawing these bones are very dependent on the position of the bones, and the preparation of them, and the thickness of them, and the subtlety, size and smallness of them, and their hardness and rarity; therefore, you must prepare a suitable instrument for each type of operation.
And know that this operation signifies to you the type of instrument you need; when great study, long experience, and knowledge of the methods of this art, and experience with illnesses, exist in you, then you will already know what is appropriate for the patient regarding instruments for every illness.
