Of the cutting edges and the sawing of bones.
The extremities [of the bone] might be damaged either by an internal or external cause, or if you have treated the corruption by those remedies and the cure doesn't work, or if the corruption has extended to a limb which wasn't originally affected, then it's necessary that you cut into that limb where the corruption has spread, so that the patient escapes death or severe harm which is a greater impairment of the limb.
And the sign of that which has happened to it [the limb] is that the limb becomes black until it's burned as though by fire, or it might become dark until the hardness spreads into that limb and consumes the entire body; and therefore, cut into that [affected area].
And similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the impact of certain poisonous things, such as the sea scorpion, or viper, or weasel, and similar ones.
And if the corruption or injury is in the extremity of the finger, then you cut off the finger, and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.
And similarly, if it reaches the hand, then you cut it off at the base of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread so that it affects the arm's forearm.
And if instead of that, then cut the arm up to the elbow at the joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond the elbow, then in that case is the death of the patient; and administering any other treatment for this is a waste of effort.
And similarly, do the same for the foot. When corruption reaches the toe of the foot, then you cut it off down to one of the bones (joints).
And if it spreads to the sole of the foot, then you cut off the entire foot. But if it rises to the knee, then you cut the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no cure possible but only to let the patient go and allow them to die.
And the way to cut or sever the limb is that you tighten the ligament in the part below the one you want to cut, and tighten another ligament over the spot; and the surgeon extends one ligament downwards, and the surgeon extends another ligament upwards; and you separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments, making a wide space until all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be cut or severed.
And it is necessary to place cloths on all sides so that the area does not touch anything, and that the patient receives appropriate and carefully applied poultices and dressings.
But if you encounter a flow of blood while working, then you cauterize the wounded spot carefully, or place some blood-stopping powders on it; and then return to your treatment until it is complete.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I relate to you what happened to a man on his foot, this same accident that I and you have discussed, which is that he got a blackening on his foot from a burn that resembled fire.
And that corruption first occurred in his toe until it spread to the entire foot.
And then that man prepared himself, since he saw the cure moving to the limb with the intensity of the pain and burn he felt, and he cut into that foot up to the joint, and the wound healed.
For a long time, that same corruption occurred on the tip of his index finger.
And then he came to me, and I was compelled to counteract that excess with whatever medicinal remedies I could apply to his hand, to cleanse his body; and the excess was not repelled, and it began to spread to the other fingers until it covered the entire hand.
And he urged me to cut his hand, but I did not want to do that, hoping for the repulsion of that excess, and fearing more the death of his hand from the incision, because the patient's vitality was at its lowest ebb.
And after he left me, returned to his land, and it later came to my knowledge that that man prepared himself and cut his entire hand, and it healed.
And I have recounted this story only to provide help in understanding this illness, and to serve as an example by which it is to be understood and treated accordingly.
On the treatment of sinuses and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in any fleshy member, and the abscess cavity extends to collect pus, and it is then broken and perforated, and the pus drains out, leaving an empty space like a vessel, and the surrounding skin is like a thin cloth and has not progressed to deeper corruption, and there is no damage to the bone, nerve, or ligament, it is called a sinus, and not a fistula.
However, if the cavity extends so that the corruption reaches another part of the body, it is then called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the treatment for it is to incise the entire surrounding skin, especially if it is thin and like a cloth, and to ensure that it is not adhered to the area by the corruption.
But if you suspect the skin is adhered and the corruption hasn't reached deeper tissues, and the skin shows outward progress, then you treat it with a clyster.
And if you consider it a sinus, and if it is large, and the discharge has a foul odor, then irrigate it with an Egyptian medicine made of olive oil, sour wine, and vinegar in equal parts, combined in a vessel, heated over a fire until it turns reddish and becomes like honey in consistency.
Then take what is necessary of it, dissolve it with water and honey, and irrigate the sinus with this mixture.  Press on the opening, and leave the medicine in place for two hours.
Then remove it with a poultice; and do this for as many days as needed until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.
And sometimes it is irrigated with ash water, when this medicine is unavailable to you;  and you take wood ash, put it into water, and then strain it, and irrigate the sinus with it until you are confident the sinus is healed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or ash water, then irrigate the sinus with water and honey, mixed with crushed vinegar.  Or irrigate with a mixture of wine and honey, because honey has the property of cleansing and washing, while wine helps to adhere the sinus, especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are confident the sinus is healed, then irrigate it with something that promotes the growth of flesh in that area.
And this means taking diafinicon ointment, dissolving it in rose oil and astringent wine; or irrigating with other medicines and ointments having the properties already described in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, preventing the enema tube from entering, then widen it little by little with a small iron, and apply heat wrapped in Egyptian or basil ointment until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you bring the edges together with sutures, leaving only the amount of space necessary for the enema tube to pass without obstruction.
And if the opening where the tube runs is elevated towards the upper parts, then lower it to the lowest point where the tube can run, because when the tube is constricted in the depth of the sinus, flesh will not grow there.
And if it is not possible for you to lower the sinus to the desired level of the lowest bone, then you must prepare the limb with a preparation through which the tube runs easily, in a manner that no obstacle or cause is hindering the tube's passage.
But that which is over the sinus opening must remain continuously to prevent anything blocking the tube's path. And so that you may know if what is running through the tube is much or little, or is digested or undigested.
And by this, consider the location of the sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment continues in this manner, and you see the matter discharged over the sinus opening has adhered, or shows signs of adhesion, then on the second or third day, you loosen or detach the adhesion.  When you loosen the scab, change the cloth and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus a subtle virus is spreading, do not despair of its adhesion, for healing will come to it. For some men, flesh grows slowly in their wounds, while for others, the opposite is true.
And if undigested matter has been discharged from the sinus for several days, then understand that the sinus will not adhere.  And if the sinus' adhesion is delayed and the cause prolonged, then you should adjust the drying nature of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and potency, make them dry like diafenicon ointment, when there is superfluous addition of zegi.
The final treatment is to take diafenicon ointment and dissolve it in warmed olive oil, and add old, aged wine to it, mixing it well.  Then use this.
And in similar cases, when there is no discharge, use diafenicon ointment, honey, when it is boiled until thickened, and apply it. Or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, grind and powder them, then add to the honey while it's heated on the fire.  Then apply this mixture to a cloth and press it onto the sinus; or apply the thickened honey to a cloth and press that onto the sinus.
Sometimes, long, thin aristolochia root and the root of the lily of the valley (and it is the lily of the valley) are used, along with powdered eryngo and centaury. Use these herbs individually or combined, grinding and sifting them, and adding the powder to the boiled honey mentioned previously. Use this preparation.
And if the sinus condition persists and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the bone area, or in other hard parts of the body or ligaments.  Then treat it with fistula treatment, as described above.
Concerning the treatment of whitlow (panaricium), of worn-down nails, of added incisions in the finger, and of cutting.
Whitlow is an abscess that forms beneath the fingernail of the hand or foot, though it can form on other fingers as well.  When prolonged and its treatment is less successful, or when the abscess is hot, or corrupted, it releases pus, attacking the root of the nail and frequently destroying it completely, and often spreading the corruption to the bone until it smells foul, and the fingertip is blackened, and the colour is dark.
And therefore, when you have done what we have described in the division and your treatment doesn't work, then you must cut away all remaining excess nail tissue with iron and subsequently cauterize the wound, as cauterization is very helpful in this.
However, if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the nail bed’s attachments are now pressing on the surrounding flesh, causing it to become inflamed and painful, then you should insert a thin blade under the corner of the nail where it is piercing the flesh, and lift the flesh up and cut the flesh easily, then apply some corrosive, burning medicines to the remaining flesh until it is all removed; and afterwards, treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then you must cut into the bone and remove it, for a corrupted bone in that place will never heal.
And if the decay and corruption have spread to the finger, then you should cut it (the finger) at one of the joints, depending on how far the damage extends, and then treat it until it is healed.
However, if the nail has suffered a blow or friction, causing severe pain, then you should first bleed the patient, and then carefully remove the nail with a sharp blade in a transverse cut, from top to bottom.  Be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, as you will cause the patient intense pain, and it could lead to the formation of excess, inflamed tissue in the area.  Then treat that area with whatever relieves the pain.
But a swollen finger, which is common in some people's hands, is often fleshy and soft, and sometimes it is located on the bone, or sometimes on the nail.
And some of them (swellings) originate at the joint, or their origin is in certain finger joints. But the one that develops in some finger joints is not harmful, and the one that arises in the joints is very likely to be painful.
And for those that are fleshy growths, the incision should be gentle, and you should cut along its base with a blunt instrument.
But for the one that arises at the base of the joint, the treatment is difficult; therefore, be cautious about cutting it out.
However, for the one that grows on a finger near the joints, you should first cut the flesh around it in a circular incision, down to the bone.  Then, use a suitable saw to cut through the bone.  Afterwards, treat the area until healed.
But if a hardening occurs on the fingers, whether due to a birth defect, a scar from an injury, a burn, or similar causes, then it is necessary to remove that hardening until the fingers return to their normal shape.
Then place cloths soaked in warmed oil between them to prevent premature healing, and separate them, or place a thin sheet of lead between them until they heal properly.
And similarly, if hardening occurs on the fingers near the palm, then you should remove that hardening, in a manner appropriate to the shape of the limb.
Of incisions of the veins, these are varicose veins.
Varicose veins are swollen, thick veins filled with melancholic superfluities that gather in various parts of the body, but most often in the legs, especially in those who travel, work, and carry heavy loads.
Therefore, you should administer purges of the body of black bile several times with a strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
However, the cure of them with iron is in two ways: one is that the vein is incised and the black blood is drawn out, and the other way is that the vein is elevated and the entire blood is extracted.
The incision is done this way: first, soak the leg in very hot water until the thick, festering blood in the leg is dissolved, then firmly bind the patient's leg from just above the knee to below the groin with a bandage.  Then, cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.  Next, elevate the black blood with your hand, moving from below the leg upwards, and then downwards again, until the desired amount of blood has been drawn and the patient's ailment is mitigated.
And then bandage it, and tell him to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are filled again, and this is noted by the patient.
But the elevation of them is done this way: you raise the patient's leg, if it is very swollen, and then put it in a bath and soak the leg in hot water until it turns red and the veins are prominent; or, if a bath is unavailable, you apply vigorous exercise until the limb is warmed.
And then, cut the vein in line with the vein, either lengthwise or behind the groin, or as low as the heel.
And then, open the vein with a lancet and scrape the vein from all sides until it feels thinner, and it should show a red color that is turning dark.
And then, as it empties, you see the vein's white cord.
And then you place a poultice below it until it is elevated and pulled out of the vein, and suspend it with a hooked instrument.
And then make another incision near that incision every three days, and scrape the vein from the upper part until it appears.
And then elevate it and cut it again behind the heel, and then pull it out and elevate it until it comes out through the second incision, and then pull it out from the incision above it.
And do this until you remove it from the incision that is highest or furthest from the others, until all of it is out; and then cut it.
And if you fail to extract and elevate it, then insert a needle with a strong double hook, and ligate it and pull it out, or place a poultice under it and move your hand in all directions until it is expelled. And be careful not to cut it; for if it is broken, it will be difficult for you to elevate it further and it will have caused harm to the patient through that damage.
And when all of it has been expelled, then place on the affected area wool soaked in wine and rubbed and un-rubbed oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having twists in which it is coiled, and is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary to cut through those twists and areas where it is coiled, and then suspend it with hooks until you raise it completely.
And be careful in your incision not to cut the vein and not to damage it, for it will be difficult for you to lift it; and exercise your prudence in this.
Form of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not sharp like the hooks, nor has a sharp tip so that it doesn't damage the vein; and it is of great flexibility, like a lion's mane, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein by its thinness; it has, in fact, a superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, like in the land of the Arabs and in the hot, arid lands of India, where little vegetation grows, and no grass or tree grows.
And it is more easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from beneath the skin, like serpents, worms, and maggots that generate between the skin and the flesh.
A sign of the onset of this [problem] is a violent inflammation in the leg, and a blister develops there; and subsequently, the vein begins to emerge from the area of that blister, appearing like a plant's root.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap a lead weight, which is the weight of a drake (or about 2 drakes), around it.  Wrap it and leave the lead weight suspended from the leg.
And as many times as there is any cause of this [problem] externally, wrap that in lead and seal it. And if it is prolonged, then you should cut off a part of it and wrap the remaining part; and do not cut it off from the root until it is completely removed, for if you cut it off from the root, it is contracted and enters the flesh, causing an abscess and eventually a bad ulcer in that area.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is completely expelled from behind it and no trace of it remains within the body.
And from this vein, some men have had it grow to a length of five palms and ten fingers, and I have been informed that one [thing] reached a length of twenty palms.
And if it is incised during treatment, then insert a reed into the opening and pierce it with a long stick along the body until all that is in the matter is evacuated, and administer poultices to the area for several days using medicine; and then treat it like an abscess.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or on the foot, and has many openings, and a branch comes out of each opening.
And therefore treat it as we have said in the division and with what has been said above.
Of the incision concerning worms generated beneath the skin, and it is called the bovine disease.
This illness is called in some lands "bovine disease" because it often affects cattle, and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and the flesh, and travels throughout the body, moving up and down, and is felt by the patient as movement from limb to limb until it breaks through the skin and is outside.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms, serpents, and other intestinal worms and ascarids are generated in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice, for when it travels through the body and ascends to the head, and reaches the eye, then a strong fever occurs, and it enters and destroys the eye, and this happens frequently.
And therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, it is done only by following its movement and appearance as perceived, and then you should grasp what is above it and what is below it with strong binding and constriction, and then cauterize over it and extract it.
And if it is hidden in the flesh and you don't find it, then you apply a cautery with fire to the location until it dies, and the greatest fear is the corruption that it causes, corrupting the area as we have said.
And if you see that creature near the head, close to the eye, then press on the brow with strong constriction, and then cauterize over it, and extract it; and it is necessary for the sick person to use methods to cleanse their body with medicines that evacuate bad, putrid humors, and guard against foods that cause putrefaction.
Of the section concerning the illness of the nafir.
This illness is called nafir in our land, and is a pain that comes upon certain limbs, and then moves from one limb to another.
And I have already seen that, as I will tell you. I was summoned to a sick woman in a village, and I examined her arm and saw a small inflammation in the vein of the arm's bend, and after an hour, I saw that inflammation moving like a worm creeping towards it, but more quickly than one might expect, like living silver moving from place to place.
And then the pain went to that place and settled in the muscle, and there it remained for an hour.
And then it moved to the other arm until it reached the other arm.
And then it returned to me that it was moving throughout her entire body, as I saw; and I was very amazed by its movement from limb to limb, and its speed. And I had previously seen such a thing with my own eyes in that way, but I have seen many instances where pain is moved from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes quite like that in the woman.
And I did not think it was anything else than that, because the woman was old, with dried-up body and exposed veins, and in this appearance, the movement of that air became apparent to the senses.
And it is necessary that this apparent return (or reversal) not appear in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you desire the cure for them, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not evident to your eyes in the way we have said.  Then you gently and lightly press on that area until the constrained air is expelled, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you cure it with body treatments that expel air, and use unpleasant-smelling pills and pills of Serapion and similar remedies.
Extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their types and the parts of the body where they are used, for their variety is such that some have large points, and small ones, and concave, and blunt, that is, solid; and others have three angles, and some have barbs; and some have feathers.
And the signs of the brain when an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone, and emerges from the skull which covers the brain, are sudden violence, fainting, dizziness, redness in the eyes, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms and confusion of understanding and vomiting of bile, and the speech is severely impaired and the voice destroyed, and white moisture resembling dust comes from the wound site, and it appears like the flesh has been softened.
Therefore, if these signs appear, then you abstain from treatment and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is superficial.
But the signs of an arrow striking the heart, when it is near the left breast, are that it feels like it has struck a hard, not empty, object; and the arrow's movement is very similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and the extremities feel cold, and there is cold sweat, and fainting; and therefore know that death is certain without a doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has struck the lung are frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck are inflamed, the patient's complexion has changed, and the patient breathes heavily and demands cool air.

But the veins bled in the arms are five.  One is the cephalic vein, which is located on the outside of the arm; the people call it "the head vein," it is smooth, and a mixed vein, composed of a branch of the basilic and another branch of the cephalic.  The people call that vein "the vein of the whole body." The basilic vein, located on the inside of the arm, is called "citillar", and the common people call it "the belly vein".  The forearm vein, located on the front of the forearm, is where bloodletting is done; this is the one that appears on the surface, clearly visible.  The *salvatella*, which is the vein between the said (index) finger and the little finger, (also) on the two (forearm) bends.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one is located below the bend of the knee on the outside of the leg; the second is *sophena*, and its location is below the leg on the inside; and the sciatic vein, which is located below the leg on the outside; and in the leg there are other veins similar to these.

And the benefit of bloodletting from the saphenous vein is for illnesses that are in the lower part of the body, such as uterine problems, and retention of menses, and for ailments of the kidneys and ulcers of the thighs and legs that have been present for a long time, and similar illnesses.

And therefore, make the incision on the widest vein, either below the leg towards the heel, or near its junction, because it is better and more effective.
And if you make the incision on the surface of the foot, then be mindful of the nerves that lie beneath the surface of the foot, and perform your bloodletting across the vein, as if you were trying to cut it in half/in two.

Of cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping is done with horns, or with leather, or with glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with incision and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping without incision is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And for cupping that is administered with incision and blood extraction, there are fourteen places on the body, of which one place where cupping is placed is at the nape of the neck, and it is the rear part of the head; and in the middle is the space between the two sides of the neck together; and cupping of the chin under the jaw, then below the mouth; and cupping on both shoulder blades; and cupping on the shoulder blades, and it is at the end of the shoulder blade; and cupping on the two elbows, and on both arms; and cupping on the two knees; and cupping on the two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood from the large veins, but from the small, scattered veins in the flesh, and therefore it does not diminish the vital force as occurs with bloodletting; nor should cupping with or without fire be performed for any illness caused by fullness until the entire body is evacuated.
However, if need compels cupping for an illness or for a continuous ailment, administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month and at the end, in the middle of it, and at any time.
Therefore, when any of these conditions occur, and particularly if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we command that cupping be performed after two or three hours have passed.
The benefit of cupping the neck is that it alleviates heaviness in the head and what is pressing on the eyes; however, this should be followed by the evacuation of the entire body.
And this cupping is in the location of the cephalic vein's bloodletting.
And it is not permitted for someone with a cold or one with a catarrh to use it, for it causes them great harm.
Therefore, it is not suitable for the elderly to use it, and those whose heads are afflicted with cold illnesses.
However, for the person who is continually being treated with it, it is made ineffective; therefore, you should instruct the cupper to slightly reposition the cup downwards in the affected areas, fearing the creation of ineffectiveness.
But cupping with alcohol is in the location of bloodletting from the vena fusca and the basilica veins: and therefore, it is applied to asthma, and constriction of the breath and the tearing of the windpipe, and to coughs, and congestion.
And the alcohol cupping should be raised slightly, for if it is placed too low, it will generate weakness in the body and the stomach.
And cupping with *alhagdahan* is used for accidental pains in the head, and for ophthalmia, migraine, and feverishness, and for pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is a substitute for bloodletting from the basilica vein.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press with too much force using a scalpel, so that he does not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin is beneficial for sores in the mouth, and for gum rot, and similar mouth ailments, and it is a substitute for bloodletting of the veins that are in both lips.
But cupping between the two shoulder blades is beneficial for a condition called *caphutan*, which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on both sides inside the hollows [of the armpits] is a helpful substitute for bloodletting from three veins, namely the basilica, the cephalic, and the brachial, because it draws blood from all the smaller veins in the flesh and those smaller veins draw blood from the larger ones until the drawing is done to the three main veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed too deeply with the incision, because the area is lacking in flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
And a cupping treatment applied to the buttocks is beneficial for hemorrhoids and ulcers in the lower rectum.
And you should tell the cupper to use a large, leather cupping glass, because the area needs a strong suction;  thin, easily pierced cupping glasses are ineffective and should be avoided, along with superficial incisions.
However, cupping on the legs is less effective, because it draws blood from the entire body, and it is helpful for chronic pains in the kidneys, liver, and bladder; it stimulates menstruation, and is helpful for fever and exhaustion; it stands in place of bloodletting from two jugular veins, and in place of drawing with two leaches.
However, it weakens the entire body and can cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit from cupping is comparable to the benefit of cupping both legs.
And the method for applying the cupping is to place the empty cupping glass initially and gently draw a moderate suction.  Do not prolong the application of the cupping glasses, but instead place them lightly and remove them quickly, so that the humors move to the area. And do not stop repeating this and continuing until you see the area reddened and swollen and the blood vessels appear.
And then you make incisions and repeat the suction gradually, and then consider the condition of the patient's body.
For a person with delicate, sensitive skin, you should make a single incision, and no more, to avoid ulceration of the area.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the incision slightly, and to regulate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then you should make two incisions, for in the first one, the path for the blood and its watery component is opened. But in the second, the examination gains the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very impure, then a third incision should be made to achieve the final result.
And finally, when we want to extract a little blood gradually, a single incision is sufficient.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we make many incisions; and if we know the blood is thick, then we need to make deep incisions.
And the term for incision where the depth is only the length of the gouge.
And if the affected area has a thick, hard, dark complexion, and its pores are tight, the places for the cupping glasses should be rubbed with softening, opening, and dissolving remedies; if it's summer, use oil of almonds, or oil of violets, or oil of mild myrrh, or oil of quince seed; and if it's winter, use narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or bloodroot oil, and similar ones.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then use oil of marjoram, or oil of balm, or oil of almond, or dill oil, and similar ones.
But if the patient has wide pores and tender flesh, then oil should be avoided, and the areas around the cupping glasses should be washed with cool water, or water from the plant Solanum, or quince water, or water from the plant Borrago, and similar liquids.
And for those whose blood is moist and watery, wash their cupping glasses with sour wine, or with water of myrtle and sumac, and similar things.
And for those whose superfluities are thick, wash their cupping glasses with old wine, or with water of plantain (likely a reference to a specific type of plantain, not the common one), or with an infusion of dill, or chamomile, and similar remedies.
And the cupping should be avoided before a bath; if taken, it should be administered shortly before the bath, for an hour or two, and the patient should rest after the cupping.
What is that which ought to be regulated for the cupping or bloodletting before the cupping?

And it is already placed on the kidneys when they are congested or have a blockage, for by the virtue of strong suction, it quickly relieves congestion and removes the blockage from its location.
Similarly, it works when placed on the liver and spleen to remove the stagnant air contained within them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty, with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.

Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs where cupping is not possible, due to their small size, like the foot, and the gums, and similar parts; or to limbs devoid of flesh, like the finger and nose and similar parts.

Thus begins the treatment of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations that accidentally occur in the bones.
O, son, know that foolish and vulgar physicians, and those who have never looked into the books of the ancients or read a single letter from them, boast about this chapter.

First Chapter.
A summary and collection of the essential elements for understanding bone fractures, before we begin to discuss the treatment of broken limbs and their diverse types. Therefore, let us begin this chapter with a summary of the subject; and the topics, understanding which is essential for practitioners at the outset and for upholding the truth of those things; that is to say, you and those who are eager to learn this noble art, whether they are your students or others.
And therefore I say that when an accident of fracture, dislocation, twisting, or crushing occurs, then it is necessary to begin with phlebotomy or to alleviate the situation, or both together, unless some reason prevents you, such as weakness, or the person is a child, or old and frail, or it is a time of intense heat or intense cold.
And afterwards, let their diet consist of fresh herbs and the flesh of birds and rabbits; and forbid them fatty meats and foods that fill them up, and any food that overfills the veins with blood, until you are sure of a suppurating boil, and if you do not fear a discharge of matter onto the limb.
And then, return the patient to their original regimen, the one they were accustomed to.
And therefore, once the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient must eat nutritious foods, substantial, thick and heavy foods with viscosity, such as cooked wheat with water, rice, broth, calves' feet, eggs, fresh fish, and thick wines similar to these; and with this regimen, the fracture heals more easily and better.
And know that broken bones in the elderly and the frail, in their initial injury, cannot be made whole and properly consolidated because of the wasting away of their limbs.
However, nature joins and brings together around the broken bone from all sides a substance similar to glue, thick and adhering, that binds it tightly until it is completely joined and united, until it reaches the point of strength and firmness, just as a fire does at the start, until any factor prevents it from continuing its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary to provide the patient with foods that are heavy, viscous, and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures vary according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the foot bone is different from a fracture of the arm bone; and similarly, all the other limbs have different types of fractures.
And I come with a reminder of every kind of fracture, explained in its own separate chapter.
There is a diversity of fractures within themselves. And the types of bone fractures themselves are diverse; for there is a curved fracture, and others that occur in star-like patterns; and sometimes a fracture occurs along the length of the bone:  and these are the fractures called transverse, oblique, and compound/complicated [referring to open fractures], just as is the case with a pen when it is being prepared to write.  And there are fractures with a gap/separation and a crack/disruption to the head, and there are small fissure fractures.
And for every type, there is a specific treatment or remedy in restoring it, according to the specific reminder of it in its respective places.
A sign of a bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is recognized: it is a twisting of the bone, and a prominence and appearance of it to the sense, and this is of it when you grasp it with your hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the bone, nor is it painful upon your touch when you press the bone with pressure, and the patient does not experience great pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there might be a twisting or a small fissure, then you should not waste time with your hand and a whole compressive force.  Instead, apply appropriate medicines to that spot, as remembered at that time that suits the location, and then tightly bandage it with a fine bandage.
And know that when the fractured spot is curved, but not completely broken in two or otherwise damaged in the structure, with the whole part of its composure remaining, then you must, from this point forward, begin to correct it, lest an inflamed boil occur there.
And if a painful boil forms there, then leave it for a few days until the boil subsides; and then bandage it according to any method you can, with gentleness and skill.
And know that the restoration and healing of a curved bone is easier than when it's broken/fractured in a more severe manner.
And then strengthen it according to the instructions that follow.
But if the bone is fractured, then an extension of the broken limb (whether hand or foot) is necessary; use your hand if the limb is small, or two ropes, or one rope and your hand.
And maintain the position in which you place the limb in a bandage according to its natural shape, while the fractured limb parts are extended, then you must carefully return those additions to their places with skill and in any way possible.
And be careful, with your strength, that your treatment does not cause pain or injury to the patient; and strive to fit one bone to its companion according to their best forms, to the best of your ability.
And it is fitting that at that time you carefully touch it with your hand, and if there is any different cause, correct it and make it whole with your skill.
Avoid excessive extension and compression, as some foolish doctors often cause a painful abscess by their treatment, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen many times with their treatment.
The quality of the stricture applied to the still limb is what I am recommending; so that you know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and largeness and in the form of those limbs.
And for those that are small, such as the arm, finger, and similar parts, the wrapping should be of fine, soft cloth.
However, for those that are large, such as the thigh or leg, the wrapping should be firm and strong, for the firm bandage unites the large limb and binds it securely in every part, so that no foreign matter enters.
And then, proceed with your treatment, placing a suitable bandage over the fracture site, using three or four wraps, according to the limb's needs; and gently tighten the bandage with your hand.
And then, approach with that to the most prominent part of the fracture site, and tighten less than you tightened the fracture site with your previous bandage. And then, gently loosen the wrapping of the fracture site a little, and gradually release the tightness, until you have pulled the area of the fracture apart significantly.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken area, using turns, and then proceed with the wraps to the lowest part of the fracture; and your procedure should be in the tightening of the wrapping and in releasing it, as we have described in the first wrap above.
And then place, between the wraps, a piece of linen or cloth to encompass the crookedness of the fracture, if there is any; and if not, do not put anything there.
And then place another bandage over these carefully applied wraps, immediately, if there is no inflammation or harmful abscess at the site.  And if there is, place on it whatever soothes the abscess and reduces the inflammation; leave that for a few days; and then tighten the bandages over it.
Let the splints be made of half of a slender, straight piece of wood, or be fashioned from pine wood, or palm fronds; and let the width of each splint be according to this shape and this form:
However, the splint placed over the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to the size and smallness of the limb; and then tighten the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then tie it securely above with thread, as we have said about the tightening, and let your tightening be over the area of the fracture, and the further away from the fracture the less tightness.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fine, and be made of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some fools do in their surgeries, using thick ropes or twine for threads, it is a great error, when the tightening lacks uniformity.
And similarly, very thin threads are not suitable, for you will not achieve the desired tightening with them, and there should not be less than a finger's width between each splint.
And if the patient is irritated by the edges of the splints near the tightening in those places, then place underneath those splints some soft wool or carded wool until it does not cause him/her any injury.
Sometimes a fracture occurs with a wound, and there is a disruption of the bone, and I will discuss it in detail in the second chapter of the singular cases.
And know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints on the first day; this is because when the limb is large, you should not place splints on it until approximately five or seven days, or more, depending on your certainty of avoiding a suppurative inflammation.
And this is the method of plasters with which the ancients treated fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
A method of a common plaster for fracture repair, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children, because it does not produce heat or cold: take ground flour powder, which is the medulla of the flour that remains on the walls of the mill after grinding.
And make it like this, but sieve it with egg white, and spread the mixture thinly and not too subtly; and then use it.
Another method of plaster for treating fractures, dislocations, and sprains: a recipe using assafoetida, opoponax, acacia, enula, and wild chamomile root, each one ounce; myrrh and aloes, half an ounce each; aloes, two ounces; all crushed, sifted, and mixed with rosewater or egg white if the patient's complexion is sensitive; and then use this plaster, as it is well-composed and heals slightly broken bones, and is beneficial for many people.
Another method of plaster beneficial for fractures and sprains: recipe using wild chamomile and white marshmallow root, one ounce each; myrrh and aloes, half an ounce each; acacia, six ounces; and Armenian bole, two ounces.
All are crushed in a mortar with great effort, sifted, and mixed with water or egg white, and then applied.
A method of plaster for joints and bones dislocated from their places, and for pain, fissures, and fractures: take a piece of absorbent wool, soak it in sour wine and boiled oil, and place it on the affected area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of restoring, but it is good for resolving suppurative sores and expelling pain effectively.
Another method of plaster for mending a broken bone: take leaves of wild fig and leaves of wild poppy; crush them all together and make a plaster from them.
A quick method of plaster for aiding in the healing of a broken bone, and when you want resolution of remaining suppuration: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile, and violet flowers, and ground elder; pulverize all into a powder, mix with wine if the affected limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then mix it with moist coriander water or with plain water and use it.
Another stronger plaster for resolving suppuration, better than the first, when you have a hard abscess preventing limb healing: take roots of marshmallow, seeds of flax, and bramble, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile; pulverize everything into powder, mix with willow water, or plain water, or wine, depending on the quality of the limb and the color (condition) of it.

A plaster for injuries and pain: take equal parts of chickweed, human skin (or bird's), marshmallow root, and salt; grind and sift them, and then mix to form a plaster.
However, the length of time the bandage should remain, and when it should be removed, depends on whether there is pain, swelling, or whether the bone is broken and out of place.  If none of these occur, then do not remove the bandage for several days. And if there is severe pain, swelling, or inflammation at the site, then you should unwrap or remove it immediately, and remove the plaster.
And take a clean cloth and a damp sponge of sea sponge, and soak it in lukewarm water, and with that wash the area until the swelling subsides and the pain is relieved; then let the limb rest for an hour; and then apply a layer of wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rosemary oil, and bandage it overnight until you are sure the inflammation is down, and the pain of the limb has subsided.
Then, repeat this subtle compression and apply a small plaster, without the initial tightness, and use it until healed.
And if the abscess, stiffness, pain, inflammation, and all the symptoms have completely subsided, and you no longer need a plaster or compression, then repeat the process as you did at the beginning.
And if there is no cause for any of those symptoms in the limb, then do not remove it for about three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all of this is left for twenty days, as it appears to you from the treatment of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and the flesh coagulates around it.
Then, apply more compression to the bandage than you did initially, and increase the patient's diet, according to what is necessary for their recovery.
However, if you find the fracture site is already drying out and weakening more than necessary, understand that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.  Therefore, moisten it with lukewarm water every time you remove the dressing.  Do this for three days.  Loosen the bandage slightly, and through this action, nourishment returns to the limb, leading to quick healing.
But what those foolish bone setters do to a fractured limb a second time, when it wasn't properly set the first time, and it is set with crookedness, is a mistake in their practice, a great danger.  If they had done it correctly, the ancients in their books would have written about it, and they would have acted accordingly.
And I have not seen anyone of them do this, which makes it more reasonable not to do it.
Second Chapter: Of fractures when they occur to the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their shapes are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow of a weapon, and the blow shatters all the bone to the skull, which is underneath the bone, just as a blow does to a wooden post.  Thus, this type is called a splintered fracture; or the weapon cuts a part of the bone, and cuts into the flesh but does not cut through to the back of the bone; and this type of fracture is called a simple separation.
And the severity of these two fractures is either great or small, and a fracture that is a crushing or a grinding; and the cause is a blow with a stone or a fall on a rock, and the like.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the membrane beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the severity of these two fractures is either great or small.
And a fracture is one that is hidden within the bone, in the smallness of a hair, and thus this type is called a hairline fracture.
And a fracture is one made either by falling or by a blow of a stone and similar things, that causes a portion of the bone to enter into its cavity, just as happens to the ribs when struck, and a part of their entry is into the cavities within.
This is more often the case in the soft bones, like the skulls of infants.
And in all these types of fractures, the pieces are scattered like separated shards. And I will come with the remedy for all of this, restoring it to its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by examining them and by inquiring about them with probes and examining the decaying flesh that is on top of them.
A splinter fracture is recognized because it's exposed on the bone, and it is cleaned and probed, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of a fracture, consider first the patient's symptoms, and if you observe symptoms that indicate great peril, such as vomiting of bile, extensive pain, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, swelling and stiffness of the affected areas, and similar symptoms to those in serious cases, without a doubt, it will not be healed.
And if you observe symptoms that are not severe, you expect recovery, then you begin the treatment of it.
The reason for this is that if the injured person comes to you at the beginning of the injury, and this is during the winter days, then you must study the examination of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all circumstances.
And if it is in the summer days, then you must try to examine the bone before the seventh day, fearing that what is beneath the bone may be corrupted by the swelling, and that the symptoms we have mentioned may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the swelling covering the brain, and it has small fragments and grinding, then you must incise the broken and ground part, as I will advise you.
And you must shave the head of the injured patient, and uncover the bone according to the custom and manner possible for you, according to the nature of the injury, and be gentle on the patient.
And if you encounter bone exposure, bleeding or a hot abscess, you must treat it with what is appropriate.  You should fill the area with cloths soaked in wine and rubbed oil, and allow the wound to rest until the abscess subsides, and the bleeding ceases. Then begin the process of examining the bone and removing it.
And this will be according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you cut the bone with a narrow, sharp scalpel.  This is its form: Then, you place another scalpel slightly further out than the first one.  This is its form:
And then place a scalpel further out than the second one.
And you should have several different scalpels, some wider than others, some shorter than others, with sharpened ends, and made of tempered iron or steel.
And use them with ease when striking the scalpel, so that you don't injure the head and spoil it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary to perforate it around before you use the scalpels with the instruments called non-penetrating probes.
And they are not called that (non-penetrating probes) except because they do not penetrate the bone's surface, since the probe has a rounded tip or edge on the one below the pointed head, like a small edge or circle that prevents it from going further and penetrating the thickness of the bone.
And it is appropriate for you to take a large number of these probes, each suited to the thickness of each bone, so that you have on hand for every skull bone a probe with a sharp tip, its length and shortness being according to the thickness of the skull.
And this is the method for three types of probes: To perforate around a broken bone, you should place the probe on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone is already pierced; then replace the probe in another place, and place whatever is needed between each hole with the thickness of a finger, or around this amount.
Then, with scalpels, cut between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as you can to create a light, airy separation, as we have said, until you have removed the bone, either with your hand or with any other tool, such as fine pliers and forceps.
And you must be very careful that the scalpel does not touch any part of the skull.
And therefore, once you have encircled the bone and separated it from the skull if it is joined to it, and have extracted it, you must file and smooth the rough surface of the bone with another tool similar to a scalpel, but which must be thinner and finer than the other scalpels.
And if any small pieces of bone remain, then remove them easily with the tools you have; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments that we have mentioned.
Another method of operation is gentle, free from fear, which Gualia described and praised highly; and this is his teaching:  It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and most evident, until, when you have uncovered that part and placed below it the end of this slow and gentle scalpel, of which this is the shape:
And it is that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part of it extends in two directions along its length, just as often; so that the lenticular part is adjacent to the skull with its sharp cutting face against the bone.
Then strike the scalpel on one side with a small mallet until you cut the whole bone easily, as it is already loosened, and you are sure that the periosteum will not be damaged in any way that you fear, and if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them, surely, even if he were asleep.
Then if any part of the periosteum remains attached to the bone in some places, cut it off with the lenticular end of the scalpel, and remove it easily, for it is released without injury and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the periosteum, and that which is cut off or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughness and subtle irregularities remain, then it is necessary to smooth out that roughness and eradicate those irregularities with fine razors, for which you have prepared a number of different sizes so that you can apply the most suitable ones in every place and the most appropriate ones according to the way in which you are progressing with your operation on the fractured bone and its shape.
And it is necessary that in your procedure, with which you smooth the bone at the beginning, you use the widest of those razors, and then progressively use the finer ones, and do not cease to do this continuously until you reach the finest and most subtle of them all.
But for each of the other small, capillary fractures and subtle fractures, it is necessary to administer treatment according to the way in which you are progressing towards the correction of them.
And it is a cause that is not hidden from all those who have studied in that art. And we have shown, in this writing, what we have written concerning large fractures.
And if the flesh is retracted when you expose the bone, then it is necessary to take a cloth equal to the size of the wound, immerse it in warmed olive oil, and place it over the wound opening; then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and warmed olive oil, placing it over the first cloth, doing so as gently as possible so as not to injure the flesh; then apply a broad bandage, and do not tighten it except to the extent necessary to hold the wound closed.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are certain of a hot abscess; then remove it and then administer medicines that are useful for dryness, such as flaxseed and ground barley, and powders of cinquefoil and similar things.
And you should make a powder from these medicines, ground finely, that is sprinkled on the wound, as they are dry; and generally, administer any medicine to it that has the property of cleansing and not causing pain or irritation.
And be careful, using your skill, that the wound in your treatment is clean; and do not allow pus or anything else to accumulate; nor allow the virulence to worsen; because when it is set upon the flesh of the brain, it will corrupt it and cause decay, and this will produce a terrible evil for the patient.
And the flesh of the head suffers damage, especially when the treatment is disregarded, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, if you see in the patient the symptoms we have described, know that it is very dangerous without a doubt.
And if the blackness is not from the medicine applied, but is due to the properties of that medicine, then you should take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these, moisten a cloth and apply it to the head.  Then treat it with the appropriate healing spices until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken in two parts at once, but sometimes only in one of those parts, because it has two bones.  The lower part is cartilaginous, and isn't broken, but only shows bruising, twisting, and a curving.
But if one of the two parts of the nose is broken, you should insert your little finger into the nose, and use your index finger to hold the fracture inward, and your thumb outward, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful not to cause the patient pain with your actions.
And if the fracture is in the more prominent part of the nose, and the finger cannot reach it, then it must be manipulated with the tip of a thin, slightly broadened instrument.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same.
And strive for its restoration on the first day after the fracture, if possible; and if not, then approximately on the seventh or tenth day, you should apply a cautious poultice.
And gently insert a piece of linen into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part, or place two pads, if the fracture is in two parts, and make the pads so thick that they fill the nasal cavity.
Some ancient healers suggested that the pads be soaked in broth and changed daily, but I do not find that advisable. Instead, the pad should be soaked in a mixture of beaten egg white and ground powder, and then leave it in place until the bone has set and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a pad, a piece of linen wrapped around a bird's feather is inserted into the nose, particularly if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is unable to breathe properly.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do it this way, and if you wish, use pads.
And if another abscess appears, apply a plaster of cerot or cotton soaked in sour wine and olive oil, or diachylon to the nose.
But if no fresh abscess appears, then you should plaster the existing one with sesame flour and powdered herbs mixed with egg white; then place a linen bandage over it, and do not bind the nose with anything else.
But if the nasal bones are fractured or broken into small pieces, then you must dissect around them and remove them with instruments appropriate to the task.  Then, repair the fracture and heal it with things that promote flesh growth and consolidation, and with appropriate plasters.
And if a plague develops inside the nose, then you must treat it with pads and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Concerning the restoration of the jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by pus, observe whether the fracture is only on the outside, and not broken in two but bent inward, in which case its repositioning is easy.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you must introduce your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then introduce the said index finger of your right hand; and push the projection of the fracture inward with ease to the outside, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; thus properly aligning it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to apply extension to both parts according to the straight line until its alignment is possible.
And if you encounter the loosening of teeth due to the break, then bind those you expect to remain with gold, silver, or silk thread; and then place a compress on the fractured jaw, and then place a doubled cloth over that, and place a large, properly made bandage, or a piece of cloth equal to the length of the jaw; and then tie that on above, according to your preparation of the bandage and as its application suits, until it is no longer damaged.
And order the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him easily digestible foods.
And if you estimate that the shape has already been altered in any way, then you should remove and loosen it on the third day; and then rectify what has been altered and plaster it with pounded powder, with egg white, or with flour of rye, after you have removed the compress, and place a soft wool plaster over it.
And then, when that plaster is properly affixed and the bone's position is not altered, leave it, do not remove it until the fracture heals and is strong; for often this fracture is mended in three weeks.
And if another time a boil occurs, then administer what we have said many times for its soothing until it is healed, and the boil is gone.
And if the fracture is with a plaque, then consider if it is a piece of the bone separated or splinters; then you carefully remove those splinters with whatever is appropriate for removing them from the instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with leeches, according to the amount needed.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then you examine the wound's opening; if it is wide, then apply a suitable plaster to it and allow it to heal until it is sound.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
Often the collarbone is fractured on the front side, near the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
When it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without any splinters, it is easy to restore; or it is splintered, and it is difficult to restore; or the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the injured and broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards that area.
And then bind the fracture with your fingers until its shape is properly formed, neither protruding nor recessed.
And if you require more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a pad of cloth or wool, of a size according to your needs; and extend and elevate the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned.
And if you cannot pull the collarbone ends outwards because it has become fixed, then the patient should be laid supine on their neck, and place a cushion of medium size on their muscle.
And the assistant should press the muscle on both sides below until the collarbone bone is elevated from its position, and then you should realign the fracture and bind it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the collarbone and they are loose, then you should cut around them and remove those splinters easily.
And if it is lodged in the bone, then you should make an incision there with one of the scalpels you have prepared for this, after you have placed the instrument used for the chisel below the collarbone; and this instrument is made of wood or iron, of which this is its shape:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But its length should be as much as is possible for the operation; that it should have two ends like a vise, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the opening of the wound you made when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure of a suppurating boil, then you should close the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the opening is small, or you fear a suppurating boil, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's opening.
And if a suppurating boil occurs, then soak a cloth in vinegar, olive oil, and wine, and place it over that.
And be careful not to move the splint from the broken bone.  For this reason, you should see the patient daily, and when the ligaments are softened, or you see the splint has moved, you should then adjust and tighten the bandage.
And then place the patient's bedding over their neck, and place a small cushion under their armpit near the bed, so that their arm is elevated away from the injured area, and this elevates the fracture of the collarbone or shoulder blade, or the arm close to the neck.
Do not loosen the bandage unless a swelling or boil appears in the area within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see the area needs it, and adjust the support and restore it, leaving it until the fracture of the collarbone is restored and has solidified.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in 28 days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is fractured on its outer side, and it is not broken in the middle but at the edges.
And therefore, when it is fractured in the middle of its area— and it is only known by touch—then according to the nature of the fracture, you must prepare it and restore it to its natural shape in any way that is possible for you.
And then place on the area powder of mill with egg white and stop the lion; and then place a doubled cloth poultice; and then place on it a thin sheet of lead, according to the size of the whole shoulder blade or slightly more.
But if under the lead there is a hollow in any part of the shoulder blade, then you must fill that hollow with lion's wool, and then raise the lead to a level.
And then tightly bind over it with long strips of cloth with appropriate constriction and firmness to the lead until it is no longer displaced from its position.
And examine the dressing every day; and whenever it is softened, bind it again and tighten and re-position the lead if it has moved from its place.
And let the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in twenty or twenty-five days.
And therefore, when this number of days is completed, then you dissolve the bandage, and be certain that the injury is of the bones, from which the beginning is not feared, nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the site of the heavy, pressing bone and place the splint underneath, then you dissect upon it, remove it, and do as we have said in the fracture of the furcula for the treatment of an abscess if it should happen here.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes breaks in the middle, and this heals quickly; but its extremities heal slowly and are crushed.
And of the symptoms when it is broken in the middle, it is that it inclines to the inner parts, and great pain occurs, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and a strong fever, and vomiting of blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is revealed and not hidden from the sensation.
And the restoration of it is to place the patient lying on their back, and place a cushion between their shoulders; and then press on their muscles, and align their sides with the hands of two people on either side, and be meticulous in the operation and in the adjustment according to every way possible and suitable, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
And then place a poultice and bandage over it, and place a thin sheet of willow or linden wood, or something similar in lightness, over it, wrapping it in cloth; then be careful in binding that over the broken bone so it is not damaged; and bind it in a circular shape on the back sometimes, and tighten it with suitable pressure; and then carefully monitor the bandage, and when it is softened, tighten it.
And if the bandage needs to be removed due to irritation, swelling, pain, or an abscess forming in the area, then you should remove and loosen it, remove the poultice, and correct any problems that arose from it with a treatment method; and then reapply the poultice, if needed, and adjust the bandage until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
Of the restoration of the ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fractured rib occurs only in thick areas, being the middle part; and the extremities or ends of the rib on the front side are not damaged in this way, because they are cartilaginous. And its [the injury's] recognition is not hidden from the feeling during the examination with the fingers.
And the restoration should be such that you align the fracture with your fingers in the best possible way, until it is aligned as it should be, and then apply a poultice and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if needed.
And if the fractured rib is angled inward, the patient experiences intense pain and stabbing, like the pain experienced by someone with pleurisy, because the bone pierces the lining; and there is difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is a difficult condition to treat.
And the ancients were resourceful in dealing with that with great ingenuity.
And from them [the ancients] it is said:  you must give the patient foods that prevent inflammation and gas, so that the belly is not inflated and swollen, and the fracture is driven outward.
However, we would not consider this a light matter for causing a hot abscess if it does not occur; and if it does occur, then it is already helping with that.
And some of them say that it should be placed over a cupping glass and that it should be applied with force; and it seems a reasonable approach, but nevertheless it is feared that the cupping glass will draw the superfluities to the location due to weakness.
And some of them said: the area should be covered with wool that has been soaked in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the dressing is snug when you wrap it around according to its rounded shape; and then the patient should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, in terms of diet and medicine.
And if a severe force injures the patient, who cannot bear it, and the bone pierces the membrane with a damaging puncture, then we fear for the patient.  In that case, we must cut open the area, expose the fractured rib, and then place an instrument that preserves the shape – the description of which comes before– and carefully cut the bone and remove it.
And then we should bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, by stitching, and treat it with plasters until it heals.
And if a boil occurs inside, then you should mix powders in rubbed oil and apply it to the area, and treat the patient with whatever is appropriate for an internal boil.
And let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Of the restoration of fractures of the spine of the back and neck.
Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and often in their articulation, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And if this happens to someone and you wish to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: if their muscles are already softened, numb, dead, and you cannot move them, nor extend or contract them, and when you press them with your fingers or pierce them with a needle, they feel no pain, then know that they are not healed, and in such a condition it is fatal.
And if you move them and feel contraction with your fingers and the puncture, then know that the bone's integrity is intact and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see similar signs in the vertebrae of the neck, and want to know if it will be healed or not, then consider the patient's feet.
Then if you find that the feet are also softened, similar to what happens in the muscles, and if when lying on their back they experience involuntary wind or bowel movements, and sometimes they lie on their backs wishing to urinate and cannot, then know that it is fatal, and do not attempt to treat them.
But if you do not see any of those signs, the condition is better and less severe.
And the treatment for that is to apply a soothing poultice, whether hot oil rubbed on the affected vertebra, or with beaten egg yolks; apply that several times a day until the inflammatory boil is soothed.
And then apply a comforting and soothing plaster, and bind it firmly; and order the patient to rest and be quiet, and not to sleep on the affected area until it is healed.
And if you observe damage to the bone, or any cause that has already separated it from its place, then it is necessary to cut the skin above the bone and remove that bone; then rejoin the two sides of the wound, with stitches if it is large; and then treat it with a healing ointment until it is healed.
But if the back of the bone is fractured in such a way that it is at the end of the bone, then it is necessary to insert the index finger of your left hand into the anus, and align the fractured bone with the other hand as far as possible through manual repositioning; then apply a plaster and bandage, if you need them, and firmly bind it.
And if you find any broken splinters there, then remove them and treat the wound with the previous treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the [broken] width/breadth/extent.
The bones of the hips are often fractured, and if they break, then the fracture is usually only a separation of their ends, and they are sliced lengthwise, bending inwards;  the patient experiences pain and throbbing at the site, and the leg becomes numb when fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, in the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only at the end, then you will  secure it according to what is prepared for you, by aligning it until it is rejoined to its natural form.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or already bending inwards, then you should lay the patient on their stomach until the restoration of the fracture is prepared for you. And then, when you have secured it, place a plaster on it, and afterward place a splint of wood or bone on it, and tighten it firmly so you don't fear further puncturing of the fracture or the splint moving.
And secure the hollow with what fills it until it holds the tightness in equality, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a suppurating abscess occurs, then you cease the stretching and restoration until the suppurating abscess is calmed, and place on it what calms it according to what precedes; and then return to the restoration, and tighten it as is appropriate.
And if the bones are shattered or comminuted, then you must set them.
Chapter 11.
Of the restoration of the fractured joint.
Adjutori" is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness in this form:
And two bandages be tied to its two ends, and then it be suspended from an elevated place.
And have the patient lie on a bed; and then place the patient's broken arm over the piece of wood until the joint of the arm is aligned with the middle of the wood's curved area.
And then suspend something heavy over it, or the attendant pulls on the ends; and then the doctor and the attendant hold the fracture with their hands together, until the fracture returns to its proper position.
The other method is that the patient be placed supine on their neck, and the arm be held up by a bandage; and then order two assistants, one of whom holds what is above the fracture with their hands, and the other holds what is below it; and each of them pulls in their direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten a bandage over the fracture, and another bandage over that, and each of the two assistants pulls in their own direction.
And therefore, if the fracture is close to the muscle's end, it is necessary to place half of the bandage on the joint and the other half below the fracture, towards the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then the bandage should be placed over that location and over the elbow.
Apply it easily and without effort, and then, once it is bound as necessary and applied properly, tighten it if no boil or abscess develops.
However, if an abscess forms, then leave the tightening for seven days, and place on it wool soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is to place a poultice over the fracture, then wrap a fresh cloth around the poultice; then adjust the arm on this support, and place their open hand over their own muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the support on the arm so that the arm is in the position of splints, unless any reason from the broken bone forbids this.
And then use splints.
And you place on the fracture that splint which is the widest and strongest of the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten over the splints with the tightening that I have mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is so that your tightening over the area of the fracture is tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the tightening should be.
And therefore, adjust the positioning of the splints and the tightening as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position; then you do.
And if you fear a suppurative abscess, then you leave the tightening and the splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
