And then consider the ligament in all parts for three days, so that no swelling or inflammation occurs in the area; or, the limb should be deprived of food to avoid the superfluity of tightening; and therefore, rectify all this as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause for this will occur, then do not loosen the bandage until approximately three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and let their hands be on their stomach.
And place a poultice of full wool soaked in oil on the injured part, and consider at all times of night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not distorted, or that the ligament is softened; therefore, correct all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until it begins to strengthen; and then, it is necessary to make the food heavier.
For, according to the custom of the injured limb and leg, they should be strengthened in 40 days; and then, it is necessary that they be loosened, and a bath be administered, and be medicated with ointments that rectify that.
And if the fracture is severely crushed, then do not loosen the ligaments and splints from it for up to 10 days or until two months.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearm bones; of which one is small, and it is the one that sits alongside the said finger, and the other is large and is the one that rests below the small finger, on the lower side.
And frequently the larger forearm bone is broken alone, or the smaller one, and frequently both are broken together.
And when the smaller forearm bone is broken above, then its restoration is easier, and its healing is quicker.
And when the forearm bone is broken below, the fracture is serious, and healing is difficult.
And even more serious than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the broken bone is the smaller forearm bone above, then it is appropriate for the physician, during its restoration, to apply a small extension with ease until it is healed.
But if the large forearm bone is broken, then a more forceful extension is needed.
And if both forearm bones are broken together, then you must apply the pressure much more strongly.
And you must position the hand during the restoration and extension of the bone, resting on a cushion, with the wrist above all the other fingers, and the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient be lying on a smooth, hard surface.
And place a cushion in front of him, at his height or level, so the patient does not feel pain.
And then one attendant extends the lower arm with their hand or a bandage, and another attendant extends the upper arm similarly.
And then the physician manipulates the bone until it is reduced to the best possible position.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are complications, then you must use your skill to reduce each piece to its proper place.
And if there appears a separate complication that hinders the healing process and you are not hopeful for its restoration, then you should operate on it and remove it according to the method we have mentioned above.
But if the fracture is with pus, I have already made a separate chapter on this, and therefore take care of that according to that chapter.
And if an abscess arises at the beginning of your treatment, then you should apply a cloth with a plaster made of roasted oil and white wax, and it should be medium in thickness and delicate, and press the cloth firmly over it until the abscess is subsided; and then remove the plaster and apply a prepared poultice of ground powder mixed with egg white, and then place plasters; and let the plaster that is placed over that pus-filled fracture be thick and strong.
And know that the names of the plasters for the arm are found in the major arrangements, or that is, the fracture in one or two parts together.
And then apply your pressure over the fracture site as firmly and vigorously as possible, and the more the pressure is applied to the parts above the fracture or the parts below it, apply the pressure a little more gently according to what is recalled in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, and not hard or rough, and the pressure with which it is held should be medium between delicate and firm, just as we have recounted.
And consider the limb and its ligament for several days. And if any cause arises requiring rectification, such as swelling in the limb, then you must immerse the limb in warm water until the swelling subsides.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the pressure.
And if the constriction is already softened and the bone is already repositioned and similar to the others, then rectify all of that with your skill/expertise.
And consider further if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive constriction, because then you must soften that a little and leave it for several days until nourishment flows to it, and then gently compress it.
But if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to unbind him/her for approximately 20 days or thereabouts.
And then the patient's hand should be suspended by its own active movement, and allow them to sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or a very slight one is restored in 28 days, and all of this is according to the disposition of the complexions and the effectiveness/capacity.
Chapter 13.
Of the restoration of fractures of the finger(s) and of the fingers.
Know that the finger(s) of the hand and the joints of the fingers are easily fractured, and they are often injured by friction.
And when the hand is injured by fracture or friction, then it is necessary that the patient be placed lying down and that a cloth be placed evenly over it, and then place the patient's hand on that extended cloth.
And then the attendant extends the broken bones, and the physician binds them together until they are firmly rejoined.
And then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, if no suppuration has occurred; and then place a splint over it according to the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen.
However, if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, then make a bandage in the shape of a sphere, and tell the patient to squeeze that with their fractured palm; then it should be bound with a long bandage.
And let the splint be of leather with a bandage in which it is raised, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and bind it as necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outside parts, then it is necessary that you place a splint on top and another splint on the lower part of the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the bandage, just as the hand is wrapped around the fingers with ligaments.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be bandaged as needed and then bound to the palm; and if you wish, place a small, straight splint on it so that the fracture is raised and does not move.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or the index finger, or the ring finger, or the little finger, then it should be bandaged and tied to the finger next to it, or all the fingers should be tied together in sequence, which is better; or place a small splint over it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and then, the arrangement of a hot abscess and its opposite — with your operation — with what is necessary when any of those causes occur, according to the repeated narrative of it.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and is apparent to the sense according to the part it has been broken and the most severe degree.
And the restoration of it is done so that the ligament is tightened over the fracture and another ligament under the fracture, and the patient is laid prone on their stomach and on their face.
And then the physician extends all the ligament to its appropriate position, according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then the ligament should be close to the knee joint, at the level of the hip joint, so that the extension reaches the area above, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, then the ligament should be positioned close to the knee, and the extension should be toward the parts below.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural shape and the bone is properly joined; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, lest the affected limb becomes inflamed. But if inflammation occurs, then you leave it for several days until the inflammation subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied like this:  Wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, folding it two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap the leg until you reach the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the knee, in the lower part, and let the ends of the splint extend beyond both parts; then wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.
And then place splints on the thigh, over the fractured area, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the gaps between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the fracture is held together.
And then start with a strong bandage in the middle over the fracture site, using three or four wraps; and as you move away from the fracture site, your bandage should be smaller, lighter, and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the splint downward until they reach the ankle, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side to ensure the bandage does not shift from its position.
And then leave the bandage on it, ensuring that no corrosion, boil, inflammation, or similar problems affect the limb; and if any of these arise, then remove it carefully, and address whatever happened immediately, as we have mentioned many times.
But if there is a piece of bone protruding, then you should push it back in as much as possible; and if not, cover it and leave it, and treat it with what preceded the remembrance until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh, or other parts to which splints are attached, is tightened, as we said in the treatment of the arm.
However, in the restoration done in this manner, the patient does not experience limping; and if it is restored alone, or the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that he limp, as is always the case.
And know that the thigh is kept tight for 40 days, or a little more or a little less, and all of this depends on the diversity of the constitutions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of a fractured kneecap (or kneepan).
Know that a fractured kneecap often results in significant pain, but in many cases it also causes considerable friction (or abrasion).
However, if the injury is a fracture, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this may or may not be accompanied by inflammation, and all of this is determined by feeling (or examination).
And the restoration of it is to bring together the broken parts with strips until they are united and joined as much as possible by means of approximation, ease, and careful work.
And then apply a plaster and place it over that circular area, if you need one; and secure it with appropriate bandages.
And then consider all the methods we have described in other fractures, similar to an abscess or something of that sort, so that every accident is met with what is appropriate to it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the thigh bone; and the other is thin, and is called the shin bone.
And the treatment for any of the types of fracture affecting these two leg bones is similar to the treatment of arm fractures, and thus the repair process is the same.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is completely dislocated.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is displaced forward.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it (the fracture) below, and it is among those causes that are not hidden from you.
And then administer extension, reduction, and splinting of the fractured bones accordingly.
However, if the leg fracture is extensive with many splinters, the extension should be less and gentler, and you should alleviate it with your skill.
And if the leg requires any procedure on the arm, and when you have reduced the splinters and completed your entire operation, then you will take pieces of wood, similar to those placed under the supports, and made similarly from palm branches; choose those with a small diameter, and avoid the thickest ones, and their length should correspond to the length of the leg, from the knee to just below the joint.
And then wrap each with a cloth in two layers, according to its length, and place one over the leg on one side and the other on the other side, stretching from the knee down to the lowest part of the foot.
And then bind the two splints in three places: at both ends and in the middle.  With this binding, the leg is prevented from bending to either side, and it remains in a good position.
And now a splint of wood, corresponding to the length of the leg, has been applied; and the leg is placed in such a way that it is supported and prevented from moving.
And this is especially necessary to do in cases of fracture, and when there is an open wound accordingly.
And from now on, check the leg every day and diligently examine it, watching for abscesses, inflammation, or other similar problems.  If any of these arise, apply appropriate treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the ankle and bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the ankle itself is not common; however, fractures of the foot bones do occur sometimes; and fractures of the foot's toes are rare, and they mostly experience attrition rather than a fracture in the area of the joints.
And if the foot bones experience a fracture, and those bones are sometimes elevated above each other, then the patient should place their foot on the ground in a way that allows for this elevation.
And then place one foot on what elevates those bones, and then press down and squeeze them until they return to their proper places.
And then apply a plaster and bandage on top, and place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot.
And then secure that with a descending compression, since you have wrapped it with cloths and other materials you need.
And when three or four days have passed, then you loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones either joined, or broken, or dislocated, and the operation for those cases is as you know.
But if any of the fingers are broken, then set them, and join them as I have shown and recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. And then place a splint along the length of the broken finger, and slightly wider than it; and then place that board under the foot, as I have described to you, and tighten it with descending compression.
But if what is broken of the bones is two, three, or more, then you place a splint of its own size, wrapped in linen, on each finger, and tighten the board at the bottom of the foot, and one end of the board should be above the abdomen of the foot so that it is held firmly.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the things that I have told you about other fractures and accidents; and be prepared for all accidents with the necessary precautions.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the thigh bone, the hip bone, and the penis of the male.
When the thigh bone is fractured, then make a splint with carefully shaped wood, and then slightly incline it towards the back, and hold it in place behind the leg.
And then the midwife fills the thigh with wool, and it should be positioned in the thigh like a bandage; and then the woman should be laid down, and gradually raise her back, for everything must be straightened until the fracture area is positioned properly and the broken bone returns to its place.
Then place poultices on her back, and when she wants to urinate, she removes the wool easily and then urinates, and you reposition and return it in the manner in which you initially treated it, and then return to the initial poultice.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it will be restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and insert a reed tube into its opening; and then insert the entire bladder into the thigh, and then blow into the tube with force until the bladder is inflated within the thigh, for the fracture will return to its place.
And then it should be filled with wool and remain so for several days, as we have described, until it is healed.
And when the hip bone of a man or woman is fractured, then administer for its restoration and alignment what we have described for the hip bone.
And that which is correct is not hidden in these proximate and relatively minor fractures, especially for one who has some study, use, and understanding of these words in my book; for this fracture indicates to you in the most important cases a direct, straight path to restoration and tightening, and therefore understand well.
But in the case of a fractured penis in a man, take a bird's feather, place it inside the penis, then put a bandage of cloth around it, ensure it is wrapped, and leave it thus for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractures of the bones when afflicted with plague.
It is convenient for the one who suffers a fracture with plague, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar, that you bleed them, and perform phlebotomy, as soon as the conditions of phlebotomy indicate it to you, or as we have stated above.
And if the plague flows blood, then it is necessary that you apply a poultice to it in order to stop it, so that you powder it with crushed glass, if no other cause is present.
And then begin restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this, unless an abscess occurs.
However, if a severe abscess occurs, then you leave the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days in total, for you will cause further harmful accidents.
However, if the broken bone is prominent above the exposed skin, then it is necessary that you forcefully reduce it and enclose it with your hands with ease and little extension.
However, if extension is not prepared for you and the alignment of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, whose length is seven or eight inches, and its width is according to the size of the injury; and therefore it is necessary for the physician to take of these three or four, according to the amount required for this in the treatment of any type of fracture.
And it should be round, and have little thickness so that it is not bent by the pressure upon it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp tip with a margin at its extremities; and let the top of it be thick, tapering towards the middle parts.
And this is its form:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Therefore, it is necessary that the pointed end be placed raised upon the prominent end of the bone.
And you push that in one stroke until the bone returns, and it is aligned with some approximation.
And therefore, strive to align the fractured ends, and if the fractured end is small, and does not accept the instrument properly, then it is necessary that you cut the end of that bone until the instrument has the ability to do so.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have described, then cut it with what is appropriate for bone cutters, which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever manner is possible for you.
And then smooth out what remains of roughness and small splinters on the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and soon after the reduction the patient experiences intense pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and then do what you believe will greatly benefit the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then soak a cloth in black astringent wine, and if it is summer, specifically, do not apply a plaster or any other oily substance to the wound, so as not to cause festering and corruption.
And then, after administering the styptics during your bone restoration, leave the wound uncovered.  Then cut the wound with shears and use bandages according to the wound's size.
And be very careful not to compress the wound with the fracture, for foolish doctors have often done this, causing death or a corrosive or festering illness—that is to say, gangrene—in their patients. And if your constriction is gentle, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear any of the bad accidents we have described, and you find pain in the area, then you should not place styptics on it, but rather make a bandage of hard cloths in place of styptics, and bind it tightly.
And when, after a day or two, pus has begun to form in the wound, then remove the cloth you placed over it with wine, and then apply poultices, along with ointments with which we customarily treat wounds, such as tetrafarmacon ointment and similar ointments.
And you should loosen the bandage and examine the wound each morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed, and the limb should be prepared in such a way that the pus can drain from it to lower parts with ease.
If the wound persists for many days and is not consolidated or the pus is not draining from it, then know that there are obstructions preventing the pus from draining, so you should examine the wound with probing. And of those obstructions, those that can be separated should be removed and extracted; those that cannot be separated and cause pain in the limb, you should cut them open if possible.
And if the wound is afflicted with swelling or inflammation, or other types of corruption or putrefaction, then you should apply a remedy for that specific condition, as described in the relevant chapter concerning its treatment.
And concerning this, where my instructions end and your expertise begins: when a large bone is fractured, treat the limb as you would a fractured thigh bone or a similar injury to a large limb, and do not attempt to pull or remove it, for often death results from this. Leave it until it can heal naturally. Often this will happen within 20 to 30 days. Then treat the wound if you see fit, or if it doesn't heal on its own.
Chapter 20.
Of the treatment of inflammation that arises in the area of a previous fracture.
Often this inflammation occurs in the area of healing from a fracture, especially if it's near a joint, and the shape of the limb is distorted; and it greatly hinders the natural function of the limb.
Then consider if the inflammation is recent and apply medicines that have a tightening/styptic effect, such as alum, vinegar, myrrh, and ceruse, or similar ones.  Use some or all of these, mixing them with a styptic wine, or egg white, or sour wine, and apply this mixture to the inflammation on a bandage.  Secure the bandage tightly.
Leave the bandage tight for several days; then remove it and apply another one until the inflammation is gone; or apply a lead sheet to the affected area, as lead has the property of reducing swellings in the limbs.
However, if the inflammation has hardened and become deeply embedded, and removal is necessary, then cut it out from the highest/most prominent part, and remove the excess or slice it with lancets until it is gone.  Then treat the area until it heals.
Chapter 21.
Of the restoration of a fracture, when it is restored and afterwards the limb remains weak and frail, not according to its natural strength.
When a fractured bone is set, and afterward the limb remains weak and frail, this is due to several causes: one is an inadequate joining and binding of the limb; or excessive tightening of the ligaments, preventing proper nourishment to the limb; or excessive application of remedies; or inappropriate movement; or insufficient blood flow and weakness in the patient.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishment and make their body supple until the blood is replenished; administer baths; and bring joy and cheer to them.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws nourishment to it, or continue applying warm water compresses until nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of broken bones when they are improperly set and the procedure is not what it should be.
When, after the limb has been reset, there is still crookedness, a prominence on the broken bone, and a swelling, and this is disfiguring the limb's shape, but the limb is nevertheless not impeded from its natural function, then you should not accept the claim that the limb is newly broken.
But many foolish doctors and bone-setters have done this in our land, and this procedure is highly reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is deadly.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be soaked in lukewarm water in which softening herbs are boiled, such as marshmallow leaves and roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and a softening poultice should be made, such as a well-prepared diachylon; or a mucilage of marshmallow root should be taken, pounded with hen's grease or beeswax oil, and applied with that; or fatty figs should be taken, crushed with pigeon droppings, and similar to those medicines called healers of consolidation.
And sometimes it is dissolved by consistent gentle friction, done with moist hands, and movement of the limb in all directions should be administered at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become hard and bony and requires iron for its treatment, then the pus on top must be stopped and the continuation of the bone must be dissolved, and the excess swelling of the bone must be cut away with very sharp instruments, and ease must be administered through care and skill in this.
Chapter 23.
Concerning dislocations.
Dislocation is the displacement of any joint from its proper place, preventing movement, and distorting the shape of the limb, causing the patient great pain and injury.
Therefore, when an accident causes a dislocation, it is necessary to quickly try to reduce it, and not delay its reduction, for if delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and very hard, making the dislocation difficult to reduce.
And therefore, it is not appropriate to manipulate or extend the limb during the inflammation, because often this causes spasms and lasting pain.
But when this happens, you should then apply leeches to the patient's inflamed area, and let them remain until the inflammation subsides; then, the limb should be bathed in warm water and oil, and then reduced easily.
And every limb is thus cured, so that the memory (or recollection) will bring it back to its place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocations, following the precedent for fractures; from the highest part of the body down to the lowest.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of lower jaw dislocation.
So dislocated are both jaws if not quickly; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their places and slightly softened; or they are completely and fully dislocated, until they are softened at the joint, so that saliva drips from the patient and he cannot keep it in or close his mouth, and his tongue is entangled in his speech.
However, when the dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little effort.
However, if the dislocation is complete and full, then it is necessary that a gentle reduction of it be administered, and not delayed at all.
And it is necessary that the attendant hold the patient's head, and that the physician insert the fingertip of one hand into the site of the jaw dislocation inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands are outside, with which he holds; and he commands the patient to cease his jaw movements and to make each part go still; and the physician aligns the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then administer steaming with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And therefore, when they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place on those powders of cloth with a cerot (a type of medicated plaster), made of wax and rubbed oil; and then be tied easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be supported between two pillows, so that it does not tilt to the right or left side; and do not force them to masticate anything; let their food be soft and easy to swallow, until when the pains have subsided and the jaws are healed, then let them eat what they want.
And this should be administered easily, and do not exert pressure on the opening of the mouth for eating, drinking, and breathing, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws, when dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, then often fever and profuse sweating occur, and the patient's stomach is easily agitated, and easily vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is perilous; and often the one who experiences this dies within a few days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the furcula and the extremity of the muscle.
The furcula is not dislocated from within its connection with the foot; and sometimes it is dislocated outward, and this is evident to the touch.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms. And then tightly press the area with your palm, for it will return.
And then apply a poultice and powders, and compress that.
However, the extremity that connects the thigh and continues from it is not dislocated in any single day; therefore, it should be reduced and bound as we have said and as is appropriate for you to do.
And then apply a poultice, powders, and a bandage, and instruct the patient to adhere to rest and quiet until healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of muscle dislocation.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated in any way other than three, of which one is that it is dislocated in part from the upper portion, and in part from the lower portion.
And the second is that it is dislocated in the upper part of the muscle, and this is done forcefully; and it is not dislocated in the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in the front parts by the nerves.
And most often it is dislocated and comes out in the lower parts below the upper portion, and most often in those whose flesh is thin, it is easily dislocated; but in those in which there is much flesh, it is forced out with difficulty.
And often accidents happen to some men through blows or falls, and their muscle becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and is swollen because it is already dislocated.
Then you must explore this until you are absolutely certain of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts near the clavicle; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, for you find a clear difference between them; and you find a concavity at the head of the muscle, and below the clavicle, near the touch of the head of the muscle, like an egg, and the patient cannot bring their hand to their ear, nor move it at all.
If it is dislocated towards the foot or upwards, and you then find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And a gentle reduction of this dislocation is made when it is fresh and the patient is young.
And the reduction of it is that the attendant raises the patient's hand upwards; and then places your fingers below the patient's clavicle, and elevates the joint with strength back to its place, and the attendant raises the patient's hand and extends it upwards; and then places it down, so that it returns easily.
But if, despite what we have said, it does not return, and it has been a considerable time since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and an application be administered that softens and smooths, like a poultice of the roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and be administered.
And then the patient should lie on their back and be placed under the patient's collarbone a ball of wool that is of a moderate tightness, and then the physician places their heel on the ball, and elevates the head of the muscle with strength, and pulls the patient's arm so that it is not moved downwards, and another attendant holds the patient's head, so that it is not moved downwards, because it will quickly return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: have a man present who is taller than the patient, and stand on the affected side, and place his arm under the patient's collarbone and lift the collarbone upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another attendant pulls the patient's arm downwards from their belly. But if the patient is light, then something else should be suspended with them, so that it weighs them down, because the dislocation returns quickly.
And it is easily reduced in another way: a long wooden post is placed in the ground, the top of which is round, like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, and the patient is placed beneath it; then, over the top of the post, heavy cloths are placed; and the patient stands according to the length of the post, and then the patient's arm is extended downwards on the other side; and the patient's body is extended on the other side with force, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult due to all the causes we have mentioned, then use this cure: take a piece of wood the length of two arms, and its width four fingers, and its thickness should be moderate, at the level of the collarbone, in this shape:
And over the round top, heavy cloths should be tied so that the wood does not harm the patient.
And then place it under the patient's collarbone, and extend the arm or the upper arm along the post down to the lower parts, and tie the post to the assistance and the arm and the hand's extremity.
Then place the arm on a stepladder of suitable width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the other side of the body is suspended, so that the joint enters quickly.
And when the entry is complete by any means possible, then you should place beneath the collarbone a wool pad of sufficient quantity; and then apply a prepared plaster of ground powder with vinegar and egg white over the entire muscle, wrapping it around; and then bind firmly under the collarbone with proper bandages; and the bandage should wrap over the plaster, and the patient's hand should be attached to their neck, and left undisturbed. Do not move the patient's hand for seven days.
And the patient's food should be limited until the limb is strengthened, for this is more conducive to its recovery.
And then the food should be increased gradually, to seven or five (presumably meaning a gradual increase over a period), and movement should be practiced.  And if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is repeatedly dislocated due to humidity or another cause, then a cautery with three holes, as described earlier in the chapter on cauteries, should be applied.
And if all this is done, and after seven days the bandage is removed, and the joint is not healed, and the plaster and bandages are reapplied several times, and it is still not healed, and the arm cannot be raised, then know that the nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, have been cut, stretched, or softened, and know that the joint will never be put back in its proper place.
For the dislocation of the foot and the breast or back regions, reduction is performed by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to its place.
And another bandage should be applied, and care should be taken until it is healed.
And if, after healing, there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths frequently until the heaviness is relieved and it returns to its original condition.
Chapter 27.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the elbow joint is difficult to dislocate and similarly difficult to return to its proper place; and it is dislocated in all directions, and more so in the front than in the back.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is evident from the pressure or touch, according to whichever way it is dislocated.  And when you adjust the dislocated elbow with your hand, this is manifestly clear to you, for the joint is hollow and the arm cannot bend nor touch its muscle.
And you should act quickly, and reduce the dislocation before it becomes severely inflamed, for if it becomes severely inflamed, its reduction is difficult, and the limb may not be completely healed.  And it is especially difficult when the dislocation occurs in the back parts, because it is worse than all other kinds of dislocations, causing greater pain, and frequently leading to death.
And the restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's arm should be extended by two hands, and the arm stretched out.  The doctor's hands should be on the elbow, above and below it, and he should push the joint with the tips of his fingers together, or with the heel of his palm, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front, then it returns by the doctor bending his hand with his hand, until he strikes the elbow with the heel of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous extension and force.
And it is that two assistants should extend the arm, and two assistants should hold it in that extension so that it does not move back from the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, since it is wrapped with a long folded cloth over the patient's hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this aids in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with forceful impulse until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary to apply a poultice to the area, which contains astringency and a binding agent with egg white, and it should be bound tightly downwards, and the arm should be suspended from the neck and left in that position for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and therefore, if the joint is positioned correctly, then you loosen the bandage from him and leave him be.
And if you see that the joint is not properly tightened, then you re-apply the poultice and bandage, and leave it for several days until it is tight; and then loosen it.
But if there is a complication during the reduction of the joint, and the affected area is in motion, then administer moistening baths and gentle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or apply a soothing poultice, that is, a fatty substance, to the joint; and then bandage it, and leave it bandaged on the limb day and night. And then remove the bandage and put the limb in a bath; and when it is soaked, then you rub the joint with gentle friction, and then reapply a soothing poultice, which is oily material, one time, and the second, and the third. with intervals of bathing, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place a warm, moist cow's hide over the affected area, and bind it tightly; and do this several times, for it is moist and brings the area back to its proper position.
Chapter 28.
Of the treatment of a dislocated shoulder, that is, of the joint between the arm and the hand.
A dislocated shoulder (malassen of the hand) is sometimes dislocated, and the reduction of its dislocation is easy; the opposite is true of other joints, but nonetheless, speed is essential in its reduction at the moment of dislocation before the area becomes inflamed or a suppurative inflammation occurs there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's hand joint on a table, and the attendant extends the arm.  The physician then places their hand or palm over the area above the joint, and pushes until it returns to its proper place.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm is already facing the stomach of the hand; if so, the patient should place the back of their hand on a table during the extension and reduction.  And if the dislocation is in front of the back of the hand, then the patient's hand should be positioned accordingly on the table, so that the physician's hand can apply pressure to that joint area.
And so, if it returns quickly, all is well.  But if not, then hold it firmly, while preventing inflammation and leaving it be; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, it will not tolerate nor allow success in its reduction, if days pass.
However, if the joint remains crooked; and you find no cause in the patient, only that the hand is weakened and cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves are severed or strained.  In such a case, there is no remedy other than applying cautery near the affected area, and applying firm pressure on the area.
Therefore, once the injury is healed, apply a bandage as previously described, and let it remain firm for five days.  Then, loosen it, leave it off, and exercise the patient's hand.
But if the movement of the affected area is hindered and you find some heaviness, then soothe it with hot water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
Of the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in various ways.
And when one of those fingers is dislocated either towards the palm or towards the back of the hand, then extend the finger and push the dislocation back with your fingers until it returns to its place.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it over the part to which it was dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is properly aligned in its own place.
And when it is night, bandage it according to that description, and do not cease unbinding and exercising it with movement during the day, and bandage it at night; and do this for several days until it is firm.
And do the same if it is dislocated towards the back of the hand, and bandage it to that part and do your first operation on it until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts of the body.
Chapter 30.
Of the treatment of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs in any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and the affected vertebrae remain significantly displaced from their positions, then in such a case there is no cure, for death is imminent for the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's bowels discharge involuntarily, and they cannot retain them; and often some of the patient's limbs are paralyzed – two legs or two arms, or one of them; or a vertebra is displaced from its place, for often it is displaced and often the displacement is done in four directions.
Therefore, this is already displaced behind and is called a dislocation.
But the treatment for this is that you should consider if the dislocation occurred due to youthfulness, because in that case there is no cure nor complete healing.
But for that which comes about by accident or by impact or similar causes, the Ancients described various methods of treatment at length, from which the most beneficial can be learned; however, I have already abridged the details which would have been excessive in their length, due to my proximity to your understanding.  My exposition, using various instruments, will now demonstrate and explain this.
And of those, only the one that occurs on the back, as I am recommending, is curable.
And it is necessary that the patient be laid on their face near a wall, and a soft padded instrument be placed under them so that their foot is not dragged.
And then a straight piece of wood should be placed, driven into the ground at the end of the wall near the patient's head, and another piece of wood should be placed beneath the patient's feet at the opposite end of the wall.
And one attendant should hold the first piece of wood, and it should not be placed in a hole; and another attendant should hold the other piece according to the same form.
And then wrap a cloth over the patient's head and firmly tie a strong bandage around the forehead; then extend the bandage to the piece of wood placed near the patient's head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage around the patient's hips, thighs, and legs; and finally, tie all the bandages to the other piece of wood placed near the patient's feet.
And now each attendant should extend the bandages to the corresponding piece of wood, and the two pieces of wood and the bandages should not be moved from their respective positions, but nevertheless, they should not be fastened as we have described.
And the physician places their palms on the spine with strength until it returns to its proper position, or until a table is placed there, and then they adjust their feet on the table until it returns.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a table whose width or length is approximately three arms' length, and hollow out a space in the wall near the patient where the end of the table will fit. And then place half of the table on the spine, and the physician places their feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is straightened and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open with a turning device operated by hand; and you place two pieces of wood at the patient's head and behind their neck, each one arm's length in length.  Similarly, place two more pieces of wood behind the patient's head, each one palm-width apart.  A hole should be drilled into each piece of wood, through which the turning device can pass, and each should be firmly secured to the ground so as not to move.
And a round post (or rod) is placed on which the turning device is situated, and it is turned within the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at the end of this (the round post) is a hole into which the post fits, whose length is a palm's width where it is turned; and in the other two pieces of wood, there is something similar to this.
And now tighten the bandages you have placed on the patient's feet in the hole that is towards their head, and the bandages that are on their thighs in the device that is toward their feet.
And now, next to each turning device, stands a helper who turns the device with their hand, while the physician straightens the spine as we have described.
And after the spine is straightened and the area is adjusted, then apply a thick plaster with egg white, and then linen; and after that, place a wooden splint over the plaster, whose width is about three fingers, and whose length covers the affected area of the spine.
And then bandage it with the bandage we have described, and have the patient use subtle nourishment until they are healed.
And if any lingering issue remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then medicines that soften and soothe should be administered using the method we have described for a long time; and a lead lamina should be applied to that area.
And sometimes a protrusion occurs in the lower part of the spinal column, and it is caused by that dislocation; and that bone that protrudes should not be treated with this medicine, because it could easily cause death.
Chapter 31.
Of the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint should only experience dislocation, and not the sort of partial displacement and cavity-related issues that occur in other joints.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, or outwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and less so forwards or backwards.
And the sign of a dislocated hip inwards is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the injured leg, the injured leg is longer and the knee is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their foot towards their groin.  And the area where the groin is tender and inflamed, marked by a hot abscess, is because the head of the thigh bone has moved there.
The sign of dislocation outwards is that the symptoms are the opposite of those with inward dislocation.
The sign of a forward dislocation is that the patient's leg extends fully, but cannot bend without pain in the knee, and if forced to go forward, they cannot go further forward.  Their urine is retained, the groin is inflamed, and they walk on their heels.
The sign of a backward dislocation is that the knee does not extend, nor can the patient bend their leg towards their groin. The patient's leg is shorter than the other, the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh bone is towards that area.
However, the reduction of these types of dislocations requires you to consider if the dislocation is old and longstanding, already accustomed to its position.  If you apply reduction then it remains in its position; this is not a complete cure, and therefore you should not rely on this method alone for a complete cure.
However, in a recent dislocation, one of the four types, you should attempt to manipulate the joint both inward and outward, and to the right and left, because it will likely return easily and require no further treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, you must prepare a strong assistant, and extend the patient's leg from the lower part, either with their hands, or with a bandage tied above the knee.  Another assistant should extend the patient's upper arm in such a way that they place their hands below the patient's armpits.  Then, tightly wrap a strong bandage around the thigh, and a third assistant should hold the bandage's end.
And its extension should be either from the inside of the knee to the groin area, or from the back to the buttock area.
And all of these extensions should be performed at once until the patient is lifted off the ground by their body weight and hangs suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four types.
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have described, it is good; but if not, then for every type, it is necessary to describe the specific treatment.
In the specific reduction of a dislocation, when it is dislocated and the dislocation is on the inner side, the patient should be placed on their right side; then place the ligaments on the root of the thigh in the area between the head of the thigh and the area below the groin; and then extend the ligament from the groin area to the highest part of the groin.
Then another strong assistant should take the patient's arms, and firmly grasp the thick part of the affected thigh, and extend it outward with forceful extension, for this will return it to its place.
And this type is of easier treatment, with which this limb is reduced than the other types.
But if you are excused and do not follow the complete procedure with this type of treatment, then it is necessary to tie the patient's two feet together with a strong, light bandage, around both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each be about four fingers, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by a length of two fingers.
And then let the patient be suspended from the top of the post, and be in the house; and let the patient's height from the ground be about two arms' length.
And then command a strong young man to hold the top of the thigh, and let another young man be suspended with the patient, and the other young man, pressing the thigh with strength,  will push, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
However, in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is outward, the patient should lie on a bed, as we have previously described when the knee was bent; and the bandage should be firmly secured around the affected leg and the patient's foot.
Then two posts should be placed, one at their feet and the other at their head; and then posts should be placed fitted in the middle of a bed, draped with damp cloths, so that the patient is not harmed by the post being between their thighs, so that the extension to the lower parts is not pulled.
And then each attendant should extend on their side, and the physician with his hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then the leg should be slightly extended, and it is according to this same description on a bed; and the physician places the palm of their right hand on the afflicted limb, and then grasps it with the other hand, and with this grasping they pull the extended part downwards to the area of the injury.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then the patient should not be extended downwards, but raised up;  instead, they should be placed on a hard surface, just as one would do for a sprain if their hip is dislocated outwards, following what we have said about lying them on two pillows; and they should lie on their side, and the ligaments should be extended, just as we have said, not much.
And pressure should be applied with a cloth to the location of the affected joint.
Therefore, when the reduction is complete – and the sign of its success is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's legs, and when you see them aligned and the patient squeezes their leg and expands it without hesitation, then know that the limb is properly returned – and then you close the thighs and place a bandage and bind it firmly so that the hip is not moved in any direction; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
Then remove the bandage and the splint, and compare one leg to the other.
Now, if you see them aligned properly, then know that the dislocation is now fixed, and then release the patient.
And if you see any signs of swelling, then you re-bandage and bind it according to your initial method, and leave it for three days.
And then release it, and have the patient walk with support for a few days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of a dislocated knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, inwards, downwards, and backwards; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply their leg to the thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration in all these ways is that you have the patient lie straight, so that they extend their leg as much as possible; place your two hands on their face, and place their foot between your feet; and then apply your hands to their knee, and hold them between your fingers on the knee firmly, and another attendant extends their foot until the knee returns to its place.
A sign of its repositioning is that their leg will adhere to the thigh with effortless ease.
And then bandage it and fix the leg to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days, until they are healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction of it by you is excused, then administer strong extension with ligaments, as previously described in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
The ankle is sometimes dislocated by a slight movement, and sometimes dislocated according to the completeness of the displacement, and the dislocation is either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that the ankle often swells towards the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for a slight dislocation is a gentle return; it is to be extended easily with the hands and pressed until it returns to its place.
However, in treating a complete dislocation, you must have the patient lie straight, and have a strong and able assistant hold the back of the foot on the affected side, in the middle of the area.
And then you, with your right hand on the highest part of the foot, and with your left hand on the lower part of the foot, at the location of the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left twice, and then extend with your left; and push the foot's ankle with your right toward the leg without difficulty.
And do this twice, as we have counted.
And then push the ankle of the foot onto the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
Therefore, if it returns in one or two attempts according to this method, and if you see the foot swollen, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, for that returning.
But if you are prevented from reducing it by what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and place a firmly fixed mat between their thighs, and you have already placed a cloth over them so that the patient does not get hurt.
And then the patient's assistant holds their thighs, and another assistant extends the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied around the foot's ankle; and each assistant extends against the other's extension, and the mat is placed between the patient's thighs to hold it, so that the patient's body is not pulled away from the parts below their extension.
And then the physician grasps the dislocation with their hands, and another assistant holds the healthy thigh in the lower parts, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore, when the dislocation returns and the success of its reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot to the leg with bandages.
And you should be careful or guard against the nerves which are located behind the heel, so that the bandage on it is not forceful and hurts it severely.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is softened, tighten it.
And then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and prohibit the patient from movement for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and after that it will not receive a cure.
And if an abscess occurs, then you must apply what we described earlier, in another place for treatment and poultice, until it is removed.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the treatment of dislocations of the toes of the feet.
It is necessary that the dislocated [toe] be pulled back with gentle traction, without exertion; and this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in one of the toes of the foot, then it is necessary to have the patient place their foot on a soft surface, such as the ground or a table, and remain positioned as if walking.
And then, stand yourself, and place your foot on the area above the joints, and then press down on it with your foot firmly until it returns to its place, and you see that it is now in place and no longer appears as a bulge.
And then place a board beneath the sole of the foot that covers the entire foot, with two ends, and then secure it with decent and firm bindings for three days; and then release the bindings and keep the patient from walking for many days, until the healing is established and the return to normal function is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocations that are caused by a bruise, or by a fracture, or by both together.
When any of those causes occur, and you exert yourself in the treatment and restoration, death often results.
Therefore, one should not presume to treat such cases, but only those skilled in the art, who have long experience, are merciful and compassionate, cautious, not rash or presumptuous; and who, in the beginning, use only medicines that ease hot inflammations, and leave the patient to divine judgment, except for those in whom there is hope of recovery from the illness, and in whom there appears some hope. Then strive to immediately effect reduction in the beginning of the cause, before a hot inflammation develops.
And if the limb returns as desired, then administer a regimen that alleviates hot inflammation and treat the bruise with whatever desiccant ointments are appropriate.
And if a dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and the bones are separated, then you must treat the fracture, and do what has been said regarding simple illnesses for the injuries in those places.
Guard and avoid, according to your ability, that which is within the paths of fear, just as my teaching has preceded you; for this is better for you than your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and more beneficial to your true body during your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis, perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
